Q&A: Finra s CEO on the MetLife Settlement; Richard G. Ketchum, chairman and CEO of the Financial Industry Regulatory Authority (Finra), explains the brokerage industry s self-regulator s $25 million settlement with MetLife Inc.

By WSJ Staff 
1,760 words
3 May 2016
19:45
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Richard G. Ketchum, chairman and CEO of the Financial Industry Regulatory Authority (Finra), visited The Wall Street Journal's Washington bureau Tuesday morning, shortly after the brokerage industry's self-regulator announced a $25 million settlement with MetLife Inc. Finra had accused the insurer's MetLife Securities Inc. unit of allegedly misleading customers about retirement-income products. MetLife neither admitted nor denied the findings. Mr. Ketchum spoke extensively about the case. Here's a transcript, edited lightly for grammar and clarity.

WSJ: Give us an overview of the important issues on your plate.

MR. KETCHUM: If I kind of look at the last seven years and what we've tried to do with respect to Finra, sort of coming on the other side of relooking at regulation after the credit crisis &

It is first and foremost trying to rethink the examination and enforcement program to be much more focused on data analytics, much quicker with this   with this strong demand of accountability. MetLife, which we can talk about later, is just one example of our focus with respect to conflicts and supervision in the securities industry, and the continued challenge with respect to the securities industry making decisions often in a very broad-based way with respect to products where there are clear incentives for them to continue to sell without effectively providing the right type of meaningful disclosure, getting it right, and without the right type of training and with respect to the registered persons who are doing it.

You know, time and time again, particularly with respect to complex products which have been a lot of our cases in the last few years, it's not that the initial disclosure doesn't reasonably describe the risks, and it may not   may do a reasonable job with respect to fees, but it's how it's handed from place to place, and what actually gets presented to the customer from the standpoint of really the training and understanding of the advisor, the quality of the supervision over it, and the understanding of the customer.

And you know, MetLife's a perfect example. I do believe it is the   having gone back and looked at this, I do believe this is the highest fine for   certainly it's the highest fine for a variable annuity case from Finra. I can't recall anything from the SEC that comes close with respect to it, outside of sort of collateralized mortgage-type things that tends to be at multiple levels. I think this is about as highest as it gets, as well as a focus on getting money back to customers as well.

WSJ: To just start with the MetLife case, you mentioned data analytics. Is that sort of where this case comes from, or did you hear customer complaints, or?

MR. KETCHUM: This case I feel really proud of but, no, this was done the old-fashioned way. A member of our enforcement team was following up on a MetLife cause exam with respect to one registered person and one customer complaint. And rather than doing what you'd classically do in that situation, which is to discipline the individual and move on   you know, we do   you know, we're barring and suspending over a thousand people a year, and cause exams are kind of one of the things that Finra does as the basic regulator of the financial managers. This person noticed the disclosure didn't seem to make sense and the numbers didn't add up.

This person basically looked at the numbers, escalated it, and that started an exam that resulted in this case, eventually.

WSJ: The numbers for one broker didn't make sense, and then  

MR. KETCHUM: This is numbers   this is the numbers for one registered person with respect to probably not one VA [variable annuity] contract, but with respect to just a variety of flips from the standpoint of variable annuity contracts with affected persons. So that's basically where the case came from.

But data analytics ought to be able to allow us to do that, and see   you know, what you want to do is be able to see early that a firm is becoming very active, whether it be in a complex structured product, a new rollout of an ETF [exchange-traded fund] that is either leveraged or more concentrated, suddenly doing a wide range of switches with respect to variable annuities, or asking the question after something blows up in Puerto Rico or Detroit. You know, who continues to sell this stuff, whether   you know, where is it concentrated with respect to which firms and where the concentrations are. So yeah, I mean, it's great when one individual sees something like this and blows it up. All we need to do is to regularize that so we're able to ask the questions easier.

WSJ: And in the MetLife case, did it   was the impression that there was this really aggressive push to sell the replacement products, or it was more just a very lax environment that didn't adequately train the representatives?

MR. KETCHUM: Well, the numbers we're talking about here, in the tens of thousands, there was clearly a significant push to sell replacement products. Large numbers of them involved what are referred to   you'll see in the release or in the order   GMIBs [guaranteed minimum income benefits], which are essentially modified guarantees. I mean, you guys know VA stuff. And, as you know, guarantees on the other side, in 2009-2010, started to become more limited and less offered with respect to VAs because some of the firms took such hits from the standpoint of the market performance in 2008 and '(0)9. So, you know, these   the replacement products were certainly regularly offered. We didn't   we didn't find anything   to be clear, as you'll see in there, we didn't find anything that suggested improper pressure from the standpoint of offering it. But clearly, a large part of the focus of MetLife in this time was providing replacement products, and a large part of the sale was particularly the guarantee, or the GMIB. And the understanding of the advisers providing those products, you know, the nuance from the standpoint of really understanding the fees and what people were giving up with respect to their existing contracts versus the ones they were passing on, and the understanding of the costs involved in the GMIB or the guarantee, just wasn't there. And the error was occurred   it occurred again and again and again.

WSJ: This is one of the largest fines you've ever levied. What do you   what do we make of that? Is this sort of an area that's just rife with misconduct, or was it just so much more widespread than other cases? Or what do we   what should we make of that?

MR. KETCHUM: I think the answer to that is yes. (Laughter.)

Look, while we've always looked at the breadth and the widespreadness of the conduct and the quality of the supervision around something from this standpoint, we took some steps to provide greater flexibility with respect to the sanction guidelines to clearly allow that to be   to be included from the standpoint of the review. And this is kind of a perfect storm.

Where's our focus right now? Our focus is in situations where there's   where there's meaningful conflicts with respect to fees and where there's   where there's a consistent failure in supervision with respect to clearly providing the information a customer needs to make her decision with respect to them.

As you know, because you're very focused in there from a consumer standpoint, VAs have   you know, I think the general sales practice around VAs and disclosure is much better after the changes we made in our suitability rules over a period of years. But this is a perfect example. The structure was there. The form was there to provide detailed information to the customers with regard to the impact, which is exactly what should occur with respect to switching, because switching is the most   the most likely moment of concern with respect to VAs for all the conflict reviews, as you know. And yet, notwithstanding the structure, notwithstanding the clear form, they again and again did not pay attention to making sure that the information was right and that the advisers truly understood the   both the new contract and the value of the old contract. And that's just not acceptable.

So, yeah, this is   this is exactly the type of case where we're going to demand higher fines here and going forward. And I'm very proud of what the Enforcement Division did.

WSJ: I think you've kind of answered this already, but just to help us put the MetLife case in context, do you see this as a problem   I mean, was this something specific to them, or a kind of problem that you expect to see more frequently if you monitor it right?

MR. KETCHUM: Well, I think   this is a problem we've consistently seen, which is switches in variable annuity contracts have shown up as an area of investor harm for a period of years. It's why we changed our suitability rule to concrete the amount of disclosure that gets provided.

No, we're not   I wouldn't say that we're seeing a significant number of other things of this type of firm-wide nature with respect to switches right now. What we are seeing is the broader question. We continue to see firms have problems managing their supervision with respect to more complex products, whether those are structured products, whether those are the more complex ETFs, or whether it's something like variable annuities.

I mean, I'll kind of look at the other   another major set of cases we brought in 2015. They involved a variety of breakpoint issues, both with respect to UITs [unit investment trusts] and mutual funds. Again, it's not that   you know, it's the type of things that firms ought to have the controls to be able to catch and they're just not consistently catching.

Keywords: This Technology Bubble Is Ready to Burst

By Christopher Mims 
874 words
3 May 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B1
English
Copyright  2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications

The Ernest Hemingway line about going bankrupt "gradually, then suddenly," refers to the bankruptcy of one of his characters in "The Sun Also Rises." Tuesday's Keywords column about the tech bubble incorrectly said it referred to the author's own bankruptcy.

(WSJA May 4, 2016)

(END)

When the dot-com bubble burst in early 2000, the fallout for publicly traded stocks was quick and severe. The Nasdaq Composite Index fell 37% in the 10 weeks following its peak on March 10, 2000.

For startups, the immediate impact was less dramatic. In the second quarter of 2000, venture capitalists invested $25 billion in startups, down only 5% from the first-quarter peak.

"There was a lot of suspended disbelief between March and June," says Keith Rabois, then a vice president at PayPal Inc. and now a partner at Khosla Ventures.

Mr. Rabois and others think we're now in a similar period of suspension of disbelief. Startup investment has cooled. Valuations are falling.

But Mr. Rabois says many investors and entrepreneurs haven't yet grasped the new reality.

"If that suspended disbelief ends, all hell breaks loose," he says.

The parallels between the two eras aren't perfect. After a seven-month decline, the Nasdaq index has gained 12% since early February.

As of April 18, it was within 5% of its post-2000 high. Initial public offerings have all but disappeared, but venture-capital funds raised a record amount of capital in the first quarter.

Mr. Rabois says the record fundraising actually is a bearish sign. Winter is coming, he says, and venture capitalists know it.

"One of the reasons people are raising all these funds isn't because they want the money, but because they believe their own metrics are inflated at the moment, and they want to get that money before companies in their portfolios start crashing and burning," he said.

Some startups also are employing a similar strategy.

Suhail Doshi, chief executive of app-analytics company Mixpanel Inc., says his company has trimmed spending, including shedding some employees, to weather any downturn. About 90% of the $77 million that Mixpanel has raised is still in the bank, says Mr. Doshi, who says that at its current spending rate, the company wouldn't need additional funds for more than a decade.

Companies such as Mixpanel may prove to be the exception. Many startups are at risk of being stranded as funding dries up.

At least 145 private companies have won valuations exceeding $1 billion. As venture investments flowed freely in recent years, executives at many of those companies spent wildly, to outgrow rivals, bolster recruiting or for other reasons. Now, they are left with a difficult choice: cut costs drastically to become self-sustaining, or seek additional capital on ever-more-onerous terms.

Venture capitalist Bill Gurley of Benchmark described this phenomenon at length in a recent blog post, in which he alleged that "dirty term sheets" allow some companies to continue raising money at higher valuations by promising bigger payoffs to new investors at the expense of older investors. That ultimately could render worthless shares held by employees and even some founders.

As some of these companies with high burn rates run into trouble, Mr. Rabois predicts a "catastrophic shift downward," not just for one-time highfliers but across startup investing. That is because venture-capital firms are more interdependent than many people realize.

Investors who have been burned at one company will be more reluctant to support high valuations elsewhere. As word spreads through the venture community, other investors will turn cautious as well.

Venture investing is like any other market, torn between greed and fear. "Without a doubt, the fear cycle is what we're living through right now," says Yatin Mundkur, a venture capitalist at Artiman Ventures, in Palo Alto, Calif.

Cracks are emerging at some companies. Messaging app TangoMe Inc., mobile-gaming company Kabam Inc. and the parent company of wearable-device maker Jawbone, all valued at $1 billion or more, have laid off employees in the past few months. Another sign of pressure: Nine of the 88 U.S. companies valued at $1 billion or more, according to Dow Jones VentureSource, have changed CEOs in the past several months.

The list includes health-benefits broker Zenefits Inc., last valued at $4.5 billion, where founder and CEO Parker Conrad resigned in February after coming under fire for inadequate compliance procedures.

The biggest elephant in the room is Uber Technologies Inc., valued at $62.5 billion. The company claims it is profitable by some measures in North America, but it is spending huge amounts of money to capture markets in China and elsewhere.

If the world's most valuable startup must curtail its ambitions, it would send shock waves through the system.

Precisely when current weakness in the system forces a correction even sharper than what we've already seen is impossible to predict, because it is about mass psychology as much as it is the financial system. At that point, companies may fail the way Hemingway said he went bankrupt: "gradually, then suddenly."

Pro Private Markets
Maana Raises $26M for Corporate Big-Data  Search

By Deborah Gage 
530 words
3 May 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Google Inc. so dominates the market for search that its corporate name became a verb, with the phrase  to google" now listed in the dictionary. But finding the right data inside companies, where information tends to be scattered among islands of applications, generally behind corporate firewalls, has been harder.

Maana Inc., a four-year-old startup, has been working on this problem and raised $26 million. Surprisingly for such a young company, its investors include some of the largest corporate venture arms in the world.

Saudi Aramco Energy Ventures led the Series B round, which includes another new investor, Shell Technology Ventures, as well as several current investors--GE Ventures, Chevron Technology Ventures, Intel Capital and Frost Data Capital. GE and Chevron are Maana customers.

 We took something that worked so well in another world, the consumer Web [and adapted it]", said founder and Chief Executive Babur Ozden.   &Corporate data silos are like websites, [but] the data they contain is far more challenging. [It is] data from machines and databases and third parties and apps, sensor data, and to have one product be able to read that type of data without anyone telling it what type of data it's reading, we solve that."

Rather than raising money from venture capitalists, Maana has intentionally sought funding from corporate investors because  our product is being designed by solving some of the most challenging problems of the largest companies in the world," he said.

Although Maana's development was at first  haphazard," according to Mr. Ozden, who said the company first went to market in 2013 by going to Fortune 100 companies and knocking on doors, its software is now used in manufacturing, field service, cybersecurity and other areas that require massive data analytics.

Maana's other founder, Chief Technology Officer Donald Thompson, spent 15 years at Microsoft Corp. working on machine learning and search and sometimes advising large corporate customers.

Like Google's search engine, Maana gets smarter the more it is used. The startup has managed to penetrate corporations that use software from some of the world's largest tech vendors--International Business Machines Corp., Oracle Corp., Teradata Inc., SAS Institute Inc. and Palantir Technologies Inc.--not by replacing them but by running alongside them so that  everything is [now] built on that structure and not randomly on different data silos. It's a new way of doing analytics at the world's largest corporations," Mr. Ozden said.

GE Ventures Managing Director Michael Dolbec said Maana  sandbagged" him.  It's been underhyped because it didn't follow the standard venture script," he said.  It's a humble company that's succeeded in an industry where hype turns off customers."

Maana has raised a little more than $40 million altogether and is about three years away from profitability, Mr. Ozden said.

As part of the new round, Saudi Aramco Energy Ventures Investment Director Dhiraj Malkani and Intel Capital Director Andy Fligel join the board, with Carl Stjernfeldt, the North American Venture Manager at Shell, joining as a board observer. There are two more board seats for independents.

SoFi offers equity to tempt new partners

Ben McLannahan 
535 words
2 May 2016
19:02
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Online lending group SoFi is offering institutional investors slices of its equity in exchange for commitments to buy loans, as it tries to lock down secure sources of funds.

The new equity round, which could come close to the $1bn SoFi raised in the biggest ever fintech fundraising last year, should give the fast-growing student-loans specialist more firepower to expand into mortgages, personal loans and wealth management products.

But unlike the group s "https://www.sofi.com/press/leading-marketplace-lender-sofi-secures-1-billion-in-series-e-funding-led-by-softbank/" in September, which was populated by venture firms such as SoftBank, SoFi is now targeting insurers, pension funds and sovereign wealth funds in Europe and Asia, trying to tie them in to deeper, more strategic partnerships.

According to Mike Cagney, co-founder and chief executive, the advent of negative interest rates in Japan, in particular, has sparked interest in the high-yielding assets that SoFi is generating at the rate of about $1bn a month.

 We re hoping for meaningful, predictable, take-down of loans through a couple of large partners, while also raising some more equity capital,  he said.

The San Francisco-based group is the biggest and most aggressive of the non-bank financial technology companies that have sprung up in the wake of the crisis, competing with the big brick-and-mortar banks on marketing, underwriting and other areas where traditional lenders have struggled to keep up. Last year, SoFi funded more than $5bn of loans   quadruple its total for 2014.

But like many of the other 400 or so platforms across the US, SoFi is now running up against funding troubles. Online lenders lack the deposits needed to fund loans like a traditional bank, relying instead on institutions, hedge funds or individuals, to buy them. But wobbles in credit markets since the turn of the year have underlined the "https://www.linkedin.com/pulse/reflections-lendit-sergey-vasin-cfa" of securing multiple sources of steady funds   even if it means giving up a share of profits.

 What we have to do is to find alternatives to deposits,  said Mr Cagney, adding that he hoped to close the new equity round by late summer.  It is difficult to be originating $1bn-plus a month if you don t know where those loans go. 

Mr Cagney said the fundraising is likely to place a higher valuation on the group than the last one, which was estimated to value SoFi at about $4bn. Such fundraisings   known as  up-rounds    are becoming less common in fintech, as institutions become choosier about which platforms to back.

 Because of the nature of the funding that is coming through, inherently it will add a lot of value,  he said.  If it s not an up-round, we wouldn t do it. 

More combinations like SoFi s could follow, said analysts, especially for companies such as Avant and OnDeck, which hold loans on their own balance sheets before trying to find them a home with banks and hedge funds.

 Platforms that have secure sources of funding in place will slingshot past their rivals during the next downturn,  said Ram Ahluwalia, chief executive of PeerIQ, a data and analytics firm.  The name of the game is who can find lowest-cost resilient capital.

Pro Private Markets
Software Investments Skid 26% Lower

By Deborah Gage 
644 words
2 May 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Venture capitalists slammed the brakes on taking stakes in software companies in the first quarter.

The software pullback came as investments fell across the broader tech landscape and valuation concerns cast a pall across Silicon Valley.

Software investments declined to $1.84 billion from $2.48 billion in the fourth quarter, a 26% drop. That's also a big slide from the third quarter's $3.4 billion, which was the highest level on record since the dot-com boom, in 2000, according to industry tracker Dow Jones VentureSource.

A bellwether of broader information technology investments, software companies make up the biggest component.

A frozen tech IPO market weighed heavily on investors. The first quarter saw not a single new tech issue go public. Shortly after the first quarter, IT company SecureWorks Corp staged an IPO. The company priced below its expected price range and "http://blogs.wsj.com/moneybeat/2016/04/25/secureworks-isnt-exactly-paving-the-way-for-more-tech-ipos/"below its IPO price of $14 per share. Shares closed Friday at $13.33.

Investors and entrepreneurs say there's still too much uncertainty about IPOs, and no one wants to risk being a flop.

Other concerns loom as well, particularly in the political arena. "http://apttus.com" co-founder and Chief Executive Kirk Krappe, whose business software company was valued at $1 billion in September, said he'd expected to take his company public this year.  What we all forgot is that it's an election year," he said.

The uncertainty created by the election coupled with a drop in valuations for software-enabled services companies by as much as 50% means Apttus will likely wait it out for an IPO.

The timing of an IPO is more likely the first half of 2017, he said. Apttus is working with Morgan Stanley and was cautioned by a few bankers.  Any uncertainty is just bad," Mr. Krappe said.

Another issue is price. With the stock market correction in January, valuations of private companies also plunged, according to investment documents and venture capitalists, but entrepreneurs are still adjusting to the idea that their companies are worth less than they were before.

The database company Couchbase Inc., which in March raised a $30 million down round, "http://blogs.wsj.com/venturecapital/2016/03/15/couchbase-raises-30-million-for-promise-of-nosql-databases-growth/"its valuation gains from 2015 and 2014 and is now back to 2013 levels.

Companies that are over-valued in this climate may be in for a tough slog as the year progresses if they can't raise capital, especially if their valuations top $1 billion. The Wall Street Journal's Billion Dollar Startup Club on Friday had 87 U.S. companies valued at $1 billion or more.

Despite software's slide, IT investments edged slightly higher in the first quarter to $3.3 billion, up from $3.2 billion in the fourth quarter. Still, that was well below its peak of $4.4 billion in the fourth quarter of 2014.

To be sure, some software startups are having no problem raising capital. "http://www.levyx.com", which makes big data storage and management software, and is less than three years old, raised $5.4 million in Series A funding "http://www.wsj.com/articles/big-data-startup-levyx-raises-5-4m-1461843003"a couple months. The funding closed in mid-March.

 There's still a lot of interest in big data technology like ours," said Chief Operating Officer Luis Morales.  We got an excellent group of investors."

Also, many tech startups loaded up on capital and may just conserve cash to wait it out for better times.

There are those, too, that are quietly growing and running profitably. These companies can weather tough times and will be fine, said Industry Ventures Managing Director Hans Swildens at a WSJ Pro Venture Capital event in San Francisco on April 20.

These companies just don't talk about it, said Mr. Swildens.  Why should they?"

Write to Deborah Gage at "mailto:Deborah.Gage@dowjones.com"

UK COMPANIES
Clydesdale and Yorkshire to launch app that 'learns' users' habits; Banks

By Emma Dunkley 
504 words
30 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
17
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Rising demand for mobile banking and pressure to cut costs have prompted Clydesdale and Yorkshire to launch a digital service using artificial intelligence to help customers manage their spending and saving.

The banking group, which recently listed on the London stock market, has developed a digital service called "B" with the aim of giving customers more control over their money.

The new app, which has been designed with input from thousands of customers during the past two years, draws parallels with mobile-based Atom Bank and Tandem. It is part of a shift towards digital-only banks that proactively help customers manage their spending, saving and borrowing.

B prompts customers when they are about to enter their overdraft limits, or gives a "thumbs up" if there is plenty of cash in an account. It enables customers to view all their credit cards, accounts and loans in one place.

Using artificial intelligence , the app also "learns" the more it is used, by analysing spending patterns to tailor the service to individuals. For example, it can flag up to customers how much they have spent in a certain coffee shop during a period of time, or prompt them to top up their savings for, say, a car.

David Duffy, chief executive of Clydesdale and Yorkshire Banking Group, told the Financial Times: "Customers have gone digital in every aspect of life, and want banking to go the same way. It's not about having 'cool' technology start-ups, it's about how digital is applied to enhance the customer proposition."

He aims to expand the app for small businesses in the coming months, with a pilot phase under way.

B is being launched as an entirely separate system that is plugged into Clydesdale and Yorkshire's core banking platform, from which it will be able to draw on customer data to drive its artificial intelligence.

The banking group said that the launch reflected a significant shift in customer behaviour, especially among "millennials" seeking digital banking on the go. The new technology will enable customers to open accounts in about 10 minutes and paves the way for instant lending in the future.

Clydesdale and Yorkshire announced earlier this month that it would close 26 "unsustainable" branches across the country to meet "shifting customer needs". The banking group closed 28 branches in 2014, and invested 45m in service improvements.

Mr Duffy, who became chief executive last year, oversaw the banking group's flotation in February , as part of a demerger from parent company National Australia Bank, which was seeking to exit the UK.

Mr Duffy's focus is on steering the bank back to health by drawing a line under past misdemeanours, such as its mis-selling of payment protection insurance and poor lending practices. Part of his plan is to slash costs. According to its full-year results last year, the banking group's cost-income ratio is at 77 per cent.

Business
Companies Pay for Supply Chain Peace of Mind; Outside firms track the potential impact of earthquakes, war and other disasters on global supply chains.

By Loretta Chao 
547 words
29 April 2016
16:08
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A growing number of firms are looking to help companies with global supply chains prepare for threats that range from financial problems at key suppliers to war and extreme weather.

One of the latest entrants is accounting firm KPMG, which is adding earthquakes, social unrest and other problems to a supply chain intelligence service launched last year. The software, Third Party Intelligence, tracks news sites and other data, cross referenced against the locations and financials of a company's suppliers. Analysts then assess the risk and notify clients when there may be trouble ahead.

The service is one of a growing number of solutions that hope to use external data to help manage risk, a need underscored by the disruption caused by deadly earthquakes in Japan to companies from General Motors Co. to Sony Corp.

 As supply chains are becoming a lot more distributed and more global, companies are working with much smaller suppliers" that are more difficult to track," said Noha Tohamy, vice president of research at Gartner.

Such companies  are wanting to use big data and outside data as part of their understanding of risk exposure, combining information they have of their own supplier performance with what's happening weather-wise, or with political unrest &to come up with mitigation strategies," she said.

top logistics news

* Get the latest logistics and supply chain news and analysis via an email newsletter. "http://on.wsj.com/logistics"

But the market is still small, Ms. Tohamy said.  Once a natural disaster happens, you get a lot of interest but then interest fizzles a little after that," she said. Companies want to have more visibility into their supply chains, but it can be difficult to track far-flung suppliers. It is also hard to make a business case for such investments when other projects, like reducing inventory or getting transportation capacity, are competing for the same funds, she added.

Rob Cheng, head of growth at supply chain software company Elementum, said this is a problematic approach to risk management, because most losses come from more mundane risk factors.  The economic impact of day-to-day issues often exceeds the cost of headline-grabbing events like the earthquakes in Japan," he said.

Unexpected quality problems, like shortages, shipping delays or productivity issues, that slow down supply chains, are what cost companies millions of dollars, he said, adding that companies should take  a more holistic approach," and organize their supply chains for the ability to react quickly if one part of a complex, interdependent network of suppliers goes down.

Companies need to anticipate potential problems because they  can no longer afford to react, which is typically what a very good supply chain professional does," said Jorge Blanco, a managing director at KPMG Spectrum, a division of KPMG. For example, a warning signal would be triggered if a supplier experiences decreasing liquidity at the time that a port strike, nearby natural disaster, or socioeconomic tension becomes an issue.

Problems  typically do not occur for one reason typically it's three or four disconnected reasons," he said.

Moody's Corp. on Friday slashed its guidance for the year and reported profit plummeted 20% as a decline in global bond issuance dented results in the most recent quarter.

Results came in far short of Wall Street views, prompting a selloff of Moody's shares.

On a Friday earnings call, Chief Executive Raymond McDaniel said reduced global bond issuance in the first quarter weighed on Moody's financial performance despite strong results in its analytics division.

Moody's, the world's second-largest ratings firm, is a beneficiary of busy credit markets, because companies and banks pay the firm for its bond grades. But when debt issuance slows, Moody's profit is affected.

 There is still quite a bit of uncertainty for the remainder of this year," Mr. McDaniel said.  We do expect more momentum in the second half of the year than we've had in the first half."

In the first quarter of 2016, the global debt markets slowed to a trickle as oil prices collapsed and fears persisted on the direction of the Chinese economy. Moody's said first-quarter corporate debt issuance fell 20% versus the prior year, with high-yield bonds tumbling 60%. Leveraged loan issuance and complex mortgage deals also saw double-digit drops.

 It was worse than we thought," Moody's Chief Financial Officer Linda Huber said.

Moody's shares slid 4.9% to $94.26 in early Friday afternoon trading.

Investors rely on the bond grades issued by Moody's, Standard & Poor's Ratings Services and Fitch Ratings. Collectively, they issue about 95% of ratings globally, a total virtually unchanged from before the financial crisis.

S&P earlier this week said first-quarter ratings revenue had declined 9%.

For 2016, Moody's now anticipates revenue growth in the low-single-digit percent range, compared with its previous forecast for percentage growth in the mid-single digits. The company expects earnings of $4.55 to $4.65 a share, down from previous guidance for $4.75 to $4.85 a share.

In the most recent quarter, revenue at the Moody's Investors Service unit, the biggest revenue driver, dropped 13% to $525.1 million, hurt 1% by currency.

Meanwhile, in the analytics division, which provides financial data and other types of market intelligence to investors and banks, revenue grew by 11% to $291 million.

In all, the company reported a profit of $184.4 million, or 93 cents a share, down from $230.1 million, or $1.11 a share, a year earlier.

Revenue fell 5.7% to $816.1 million. The company said currency translation shaved 2% off the top line.

Analysts surveyed by Thomson Reuters projected earnings of $1.06 a share on revenue of $852 million.

U.S. revenue fell 4% to $480 million, while foreign revenue declined 8% to $336.1 million. Revenue generated outside the U.S. fell to 41% of total revenue, compared with 42% in the quarter last year.

 The weakness in issuance was probably broader and a little more severe than people expected," said Timothy McHugh, an analyst for William Blair, in a note to investors.

BANGKOK Thailand's economy remains on shaky ground, data from the country's central bank released Friday suggested, with exports continuing to contract in the first quarter of the year while a persistent drought subdued consumer spending. 

The Bank of Thailand said exports, which usually account for around two-thirds of the country's gross domestic product, fell 5.6% in the first three months of the year compared with the same period last year. Two other key indexes, private consumption and investment, went up only slightly in the same window, rising 2.0% and 1.6% respectively. 

 Overall spending remained at a low level," the central bank said in a statement.  Meanwhile, spending on non-durable goods slowed down as households grew more cautious, particularly in the case of farming households, whose purchasing power has been adversely affected by the drought." 

And, despite a 1.83% year-over-year increase in the Manufacturing Production Index in March, the index still fell 1.1% in the first quarter. 

The snapshot presents a worrying picture for Thailand's ruling junta, which seized power nearly two years ago. Since it took control, confidence has waned and foreign investment has plummeted, falling 78% in 2015. The El Nio weather pattern, meanwhile, has brought about the worst drought the country has seen in more than 60 years, parching agricultural land and depressing rural spending. 

Some economists also said the impact of stimulus measures introduced late last year, including tax deductions for shopping or travel and incentives for property purchases, has begun to fade. 

 The positive effects from the junta government's stimulus measures at the end of 2015 have dissipated," Moody's Analytics said.  Consumer confidence remains weak because of the uncertainty stemming from the upcoming constitutional referendum." 

Prime Minister Prayuth Chan-ocha's government is pushing for a new charter which, among other things, would guarantee seats for soldiers in the country's Senate and allow the legislature to select a nonmember, such as an army officer, to serve as prime minister. It will be put to a national referendum on Aug. 7, and authorities recently passed a law that critics say is designed to stifle opposition to the junta's draft. 

The drought is another source of concern. Moody's said Thailand's food production is also expected to  take a particularly large hit in the coming months because of severe drought affecting rice farmers."

"http://graphics.wsj.com/brexit/"

Global investors are steering clear of U.K. commercial real estate ahead of Britain's referendum over its European Union membership.

U.K. commercial property transaction volumes hit 10.9 billion ($15.9 billion) in the first quarter, down 43% from the corresponding period in 2015, according to data-provider Real Capital Analytics. London deal volumes were down 47% to 5 billion.

In a survey of U.K. property brokers, only 5% reported increased interest from overseas buyers in the last three months, down from 36% in the second quarter of 2015, according to the Royal Institute of Chartered Surveyors.

 Some of the slowdown is because investors are pushing the pause button," said Andrew Hawkins, director at broker JLL, while speaking at the London headquarters of RICS on Wednesday.  And why wouldn't you? Nobody wants to get sacked because they made the wrong decision."

The U.K. "http://www.wsj.com/articles/david-cameron-to-announce-eu-referendum-date-after-cabinet-meeting-1455964352". Earlier this month, credit-rating firm Standard & Poor's Corp. "http://www.wsj.com/articles/s-p-warns-over-commercial-property-values-if-u-k-leaves-eu-1460035564" votes to leave the trading bloc in a so-called Brexit.

 If Britain votes to leave the EU, there will inevitably be impact on the U.K. commercial property market, particularly for London offices," said Robert Noel, chief executive of Land Securities PLC, the biggest listed real-estate firm in the U.K.

Mr. Noel said that should businesses stall decision-making during a period of uncertainty, demand for office space would fall, leading to an oversupply and falling rental values.

Uncertainty over the referendum has sparked a decline in foreign investor interest since the referendum became a certainty last May, said Simon Rubinsohn, chief economist at RICS.

Weakening demand follows"http://www.wsj.com/articles/surge-in-commercial-real-estate-prices-stirs-bubble-worries-1439415930"after the 2008 financial crisis. Low interest rates have made property returns appear attractive compared with other asset classes like bonds.

The U.K., and London in particular, became a focus for foreign investors. Property values, from high-end homes in central London to financial-district office buildings, surged.

Earlier this month, U.K. landlord Grosvenor Group"http://www.wsj.com/articles/global-real-estate-boom-is-coming-to-an-end-u-k-s-grosvenor-warns-1461071783". Volatile stock markets, weak oil prices and political uncertainty around the world have all contributed to weaker demand.

 Uncertainty over the Brexit vote is certainly contributing to the drop in the U.K. market," said Tom Leahy, director of market analysis at RCA.  But it needs to be seen in the broader context of the slowdown across Europe."

European transaction volumes in commercial property fell 40% in the first quarter to  ¬46.7 billion, RCA data show. Sharp falls were recorded in the U.K., Germany and France, the three biggest European markets. France saw the biggest drop, with deal volumes down 59% to  ¬3.5 billion.

It is not just a European phenomenon. Investment volumes in the U.S. were down 20% in the first quarter, while activity also weakened in Australia, Canada, China and Japan, RCA said.

The U.K. hasn't been immune to the global demand downturn, property chiefs said.

 I think the market peaked 18 months ago," said Chris Taylor, chief executive at the property arm of Hermes Investment Management. In addition to a potential Brexit, broader worries about global financial markets have created a  perfect storm," said Mr. Taylor at a U.K. property industry event in London Thursday.

After the June referendum,  I think there will be a realization that the market had gotten a bit ahead of itself," he said.

Some investors are still willing to scoop-up property ahead of the vote.

 I'm not sure it's a buying opportunity, but if there are sellers due to a potential Brexit &we would be looking to acquire assets under those circumstances," said Bill Hughes, head of real assets at the property arm of U.K. asset manager Legal & General, during the Thursday industry event.

In the past week, Mr. Hughes said he had met separately with a Japanese investor and Chinese investor willing to close deals ahead of the referendum.

Still, the looming uncertainty surrounding a potential Brexit have weighed particularly hard on the U.K. market, brokers and analysts said. Brexit concerns were a primary reason behind Chicago-based JLL's first-quarter adjusted operating loss in the Europe, Middle East and Africa region, the company said on Wednesday.

The broker reported an adjusted operating loss of $10 million in the region for the first three months of the year, compared with a loss of $3 million in the corresponding period of 2015. Globally, JLL's adjusted operating income for the first quarter was $45 million.

Of barriers preventing investment in U.K. property,  the biggest is the threat of a Brexit," said David Sleath, chief executive at property investment firm SEGRO PLC, at the Thursday event.

With transactions down considerably, the market  won't recover before the referendum," Mr. Sleath said.

"http://www.levyx.com" raised $5.4 million for high-powered storage and management software that the company said cuts infrastructure costs for big-data applications.

The Series A round was led by OCA Ventures out of Chicago. Amino Capital (formerly zPark Venture), Sumavision USA Corp. and unnamed tech executives also participated in the round.

Levyx co-founder and Chief Executive Reza Sadri said the startup is bridging the gap between high-performance big-data software and the commodity hardware used in data centers, letting users fully exploit both.

By taking advantage of parallelism in processors, solid state drives and other new technologies, he said,  you can have hundreds of CPU cores in our system. Our software can scale across all of them in a single system, so you can achieve things you'd usually achieve using tens or hundreds of servers."

Levyx also has a data-indexing technology that plugs into Apache Spark, a popular open-source project, although it is not limited to Spark.  What Linux was to the operating system, Spark is becoming to big data," Mr. Sadri said.  It's the universal platform that everyone is moving applications to."

Levyx started in 2013 and has been working with a customer to perfect its technology, which is useful for cybersecurity, financial applications, Internet of Things, data center management and other areas that need to process large amounts of data very quickly, Mr. Sadri said.

He compared big-data processing with the  mainframes of 50 years ago, which were expensive and difficult to manage and accessible to a handful of large companies. Processing large data sets is exactly like that right now."

Although Series A rounds are getting harder to raise, Levyx raised its funding in a little over a quarter because investors are interested in big-data technology, according to Chief Operating Officer Luis Morales.

The funding closed in March and takes total funding in Levyx to $6.4 million. Levyx is based in Palo Alto, Calif., and Irvine, Calif.

Business Software Maker Anaplan s CEO Steps Down;  It s the right time to bring in a new set of talent to take us to a much higher level,  CFO James Budge says

Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Pressure is building for the chief executives of Silicon Valley's richly valued technology startups to show profitability or step out of the way.

This week, business software maker Anaplan Inc. became the latest tech startup to make a change at the top, saying the company needed to execute more quickly toward reaching a profit.

Anaplan, which only three months ago "http://blogs.wsj.com/venturecapital/2016/01/14/anaplan-achieves-1-09-billion-valuation-with-new-round/", said CEO Frederic Laluyaux has left the company after more than three years at the helm. Anaplan, which hasn't named a replacement, said the decision was mutual. Mr. Laluyaux couldn't be reached for comment.

 The board and Fred believe it's the right time to bring in a new set of talent to take us to a much higher level and become a much bigger company," said Chief Financial Officer James Budge.

The move reflects a profound shift in Silicon Valley, where until last fall venture capital flowed freely and startups spent lavishly to grow at any cost and meet steep valuations. But a volatile stock market and concerns about a global economic slump have prompted venture investors including mutual funds, hedge funds and banks to pull back funding, leading companies to rethink their business models.

 Unbridled growth [is no longer] the most valued characteristic," said Ravi Mohan, Anaplan board member and managing director at Shasta Ventures.  Now, it's profitable sustained growth, and we're building a company that reflects that."

Mr. Mohan said Anaplan, which has raised $240 million in funding, has pushed forward its plan to reach profitability to 2017 rather than early 2018.

Valuations for enterprise software companies have fallen this year by more than 40% in some cases, according to venture capitalists and data on recent funding rounds, especially in competitive markets. Anaplan is one of nine companies that have changed CEOs in the past several months among a group of 88 U.S. companies valued at $1 billion or more, according to Dow Jones VentureSource.

Health-benefits broker Zenefits, last valued at $4.5 billion, is the highest profile of the bunch, with founder and CEO Parker Conrad resigning in February after coming under fire for inadequate compliance procedures. Zenefits also missed its own financial projections last year, The Wall Street Journal reported.

Other U.S. companies valued at $1 billion that have recently replaced their founder CEOs include business software maker AppDynamics Inc., note-taking app maker Evernote Corp., data analytics company Mu Sigma Inc., messaging startup TangoMe Inc., security company Tanium Inc. and medical-appointment site ZocDoc Inc. Software startup DocuSign Inc. is searching for a new CEO to replace its current one, who isn't a founder.

Boards often wait too long to change CEOs, says Beau Vrolyk, the chief executive of business-software company Engine Yard Inc. who replaced the previous CEO in 2014.  They'll give a CEO the benefit of the doubt and support them and do everything they can to make the person successful," he said.

Mr. Laluyaux wasn't a founder of Anaplan, but was brought in as CEO in September 2012, replacing co-founder Guy Haddleton, who remains on the board.

Anaplan, founded in 2006, has about 600 employees and 70,000 users for a software platform that lets companies pull data from finance, sales, human resources and other business operations. The company has strong competition, both from other venture-backed startups such as Adaptive Insights Inc., and from tech giants International Business Machines Corp., Oracle Corp. and SAP SE.

Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

SnappyData Inc. on Wednesday said it raised $3.65 million in Series A funding, a move that comes after the database startup spun out of Pivotal Software Inc., where it was incubated last year.

The database is intended to let users ask questions from large volumes of data and quickly get useful answers, without compromising performance, according to founder Sudhir Menon.

SnappyData runs in-memory and is built on Apache Spark, a popular open-source project that integrates data streaming and machine learning. By combining Spark and SnappyData, users don't have to stitch software together, Mr. Menon said--they get operational analytics, streaming analytics and data modeling on a single platform, along with high availability and other features that are important to corporate customers.

 We're the first ones to build a database on Spark and first to build out the approximate query processing piece to ask questions of a sample," he said.

SnappyData has no customers in production yet but several are trying the software, he said. Popular uses so far include the industrial Internet, a category in which companies such as GE are trying to get data from sensors spread over large distances.

Except for the query processing part, which SnappyData is building with prospective customers, the database is an open-source project and the company is working to build a community of developers around it.  We understand how the game works," Mr. Menon said.

The funding comes from GE Digital, GTD Capital and Pivotal Software, which spun out of EMC Corp. and VMware Inc.

Mr. Menon and his co-founders, Richard Lamb and Jags Ramnayaran, have worked together for 13 years, Mr. Menon said. They developed GemFire, an in-memory data grid that made its way to VMware through the acquisition of Gemstone Systems in 2010.

Citigroup Gets a Rebuke on Pay --- One-third of voting shareholders reject compensation plan; stock down 9% in 2016

By Christina Rexrode 
632 words
27 April 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright  2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

About one-third of voting Citigroup Inc. shareholders cast their ballots against the bank's pay plan for top executives Tuesday, a rebuke for a bank that has been focusing on improving relationships with investors.

About 64% of shareholders voted in favor of the bank's pay package, Citigroup said at its annual meeting Tuesday. That was down from last year's vote, when about 84% voted in favor. The average shareholder support of executive pay packages at companies in the S&P 500 was 93% last year, according to ISS Voting Analytics.

Meanwhile, a proposal asking the bank to consider breaking itself apart got just 3.5% support, according to preliminary results. Last year, a similar proposal at Bank of America Corp. gained about 4% of the votes, showing that shareholders of big banks aren't as enthusiastic about breaking them into smaller firms as some politicians and analysts are.

On Citigroup executive pay, two influential firms that advise big shareholders on how to vote, Institutional Shareholder Services and Glass Lewis & Co., had recommended that shareholders vote no in the "say on pay" proposal, saying the bank needs to more closely link the executives' pay to the bank's performance. Citigroup's stock price is down about 9% this year, compared with a 4% drop in the KBWNasdaq index of bank stocks.

Citigroup has defended its pay packages, including 2015 compensation of $16.5 million for CEO Michael Corbat. At Tuesday's meeting, Chairman Michael O'Neill noted other accomplishments, including passing the Federal Reserve's annual "stress test" last year and boosting earnings to their highest level in nearly a decade.

Mr. O'Neill acknowledged that the share price was "a fly in the ointment" and not where the bank wanted it to be, and said the board would be considering other ways to tweak the pay packages for next year.

Chris Spahr, an analyst at CLSA, said he wasn't satisfied. "It looks like there was some grade inflation" when the bank decided how to pay executives, Mr. Spahr said at the meeting.

Mr. Corbat, the CEO for about 3 1/2 years, has worked on shrinking a bloated company and improving relationships with regulators. A middling stock price this year has continued to be a drag on the bank, though the shares are up 26% since Mr. Corbat took over.

Charles Elson, director of the Weinberg Center for Corporate Governance at the University of Delaware, said Tuesday's say-on-pay vote is "a real problem."

"They have to ask themselves what is creating this discontent," Mr. Elson said. Citigroup's stock price, he added, is the "glaring issue."

Messrs. Corbat and O'Neill throughout the meeting echoed a familiar theme: that the bank has gotten simpler and smaller. This month, it was the only bank to pass regulators' "living wills" test, which examines whether banks could break themselves apart without requiring taxpayer assistance.

The breakup proposal was submitted by Bartlett Naylor, a financial-policy advocate at the liberal lobbying group Public Citizen. At the meeting, Mr. Naylor questioned whether shareholders were telling the bank how they really felt, and said that Citigroup shareholders still were "clobbered" from the financial crisis.

Mr. O'Neill said the board had considered other alternatives and still believed that the bank's current business model was the best way to boost results in the long term. "It's not that we didn't study it," Mr. O'Neill said. "We studied it very hard."

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. shareholders will vote on a similar proposal next month.

MONEY LAUNDERING

Key U.S. Regulator

Is Leaving Her Role

Jennifer Shasky Calvery is stepping down as director of the U.S. Treasury Department's Financial Crimes Enforcement Network, the regulator said.

Ms. Calvery will leave her role at the end of May after more than three years in the job. Over that period, anti-money laundering concerns gained new importance and reshaped the way banks do business, in part because regulators imposed record fines for lapses in controls.

FinCEN has enforcement powers and also serves as the financial intelligence unit of the U.S., collecting millions of secret reports filed by financial institutions on their customers, including reports known as suspicious activity reports, or SARs.

Job Moves: who is hiring and where

By FT reporters 
2,923 words
26 April 2016
12:57
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The latest job moves in the trading industry and coverage of people making the news in the world s financial capitals

LCH promotes Maguire to global rates role

LCH Group, the world s largest swaps clearing house, has promoted Daniel Maguire to an expanded role as global head of its rates and foreign exchange derivatives business.

Mr Maguire will be responsible for the development of listed and OTC rates and FX derivatives at LCH. He has been head of SwapClear, its over-the-counter (OTC) swaps and listed rates business for the last two years, and begins on May 1.

At the same time LCH also appointed Paddy Boyle, a former Goldman Sachs trader, as head of FX product. Mr Boyle was previously global head of G10 FX options at Goldman Sachs and was a member of the Bank of England joint standing committee on FX. He will report to Mr Maguire. (Philip Stafford, April 26)

Euronext s CFO Dauge to join fintech start-up

Amaury Dauge is to end his 15-year career at Euronext, the European exchanges operator, after announcing he will step down to join a financial technology company in New York.

He will be replaced by Giorgio Modica, a former banker at BNP Paribas, as Euronext s new chief executive Stphane Boujnah reorganises the company s finance team.

Mr Dauge became chief financial officer in 2014 and oversaw Euronext s demerger from former US parent Intercontinental Exchange, and its flotation on four bourses in Europe. He will leave on June 1st.

Mr Modica was previously a senior corporate financier focusing on the stock exchange sector globally, as well as for the Italian and Spanish markets. He advised NYSE Euronext during its ill-fated attempt to merge with Deutsche Brse, as well as the subsequent IPO once NYSE Euronext was acquired by ICE. He has also worked at HSBC in Milan and MCC/Capitalia in Rome.

The appointments come amid heightened speculation that Euronext may join in potential exchange consolidation and reshuffle of assets. Deutsche Brse is planning to merge with the London Stock Exchange Group but it may face a bid from ICE. The US group has already indicated it would be willing to sell the LSE s Borsa Italiana, according to two people familiar with the talks.

At the same time Mr Boujnah also appointed Camille Beudin, a former mergers and acquisitions banker as Euronext s new head of M&A. She previously worked at Deutsche Bank, RBS and ABN Amro.

 Giorgio has been so close to Euronext for so many years that he was the natural person to lead the finance team at Euronext,  said Mr Boujnah. (Philip Stafford, April 19)

LSE names Ross as chief executive of CurveGlobal

The London Stock Exchange Group has filled the chief executive gap at CurveGlobal, its upcoming new interest rate derivatives joint venture, by naming Andrew Ross of Morgan Stanley to the position.

The position has been unfilled since the LSE said in October it would lead a 30m bank-backed futures trading venue that to compete in European markets dominated by Deutsche Brse and Intercontinental Exchange. Two years in preparation, CurveGlobal represents the biggest push by the LSE into interest rate futures since it was beaten to the 550m sale of Liffe, the London derivatives market, by Euronext in 2002.

At Morgan Stanley, Mr Ross was responsible for agency-listed derivative electronic trading and was also European head of clearing. In his 10-year career at the bank, Mr Ross also been head of CVA trading in London.

 CurveGlobal s success will be built on its ability to bring together a broad swath of exchange-traded derivatives and OTC market participants including the buy and sell sides, prop trading firms and market makers,  said Michael Davie, chairman of CurveGlobal.  It s therefore vital that our chief executive has the depth of experience and relationship with each of these communities, which is why I m delighted that Andrew will be taking on the role. 

The LSE added that France s BNP Paribas had become the seventh shareholder of CurveGlobal on January 29. Bank of America Merrill Lynch, Goldman Sachs, Barclays, Citi, JPMorgan and Socit Gnrale are also backing the project, as is the Chicago Board Options Exchange, the US exchange that owns the Vix volatility indices. The LSE will contribute 9.5m, and the banks and CBOE a further 20.5m. (Philip Stafford, February 5)

Intercontinental Exchange, the US exchanges operator, has reshuffled several senior executive positions following two recent major purchases.

All three will report to Jeff Sprecher, ICE group chief executive.  These promotions reflect our commitment to delivering on our strategic and financial objectives. Exchanges have generally been unable to establish a presence in fixed income and forex markets. However a combination of unlawful activity by banks, deep structural change and the emergence of cheap and reliable technology that has allowed alternative ways of trading to emerge. MARKETS & INVESTING
Clock ticking for human stock pickers; Equities

By Robin Wigglesworth in New York 
458 words
26 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
20
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Computers poised to 'mostly replace' people, predicts star manager

A rising fund manager star at T Rowe Price predicts that computers will become increasingly important in asset management, supplanting the industry's traditional backbone of human stock pickers, economists and analysts.

Sudhir Nanda, the head of T Rowe's new dedicated "quantitative management" arm, has for the past decade managed the $761bn asset manager's Diversified Small-Cap Growth Fund , a $2.3bn computer-powered, systematic vehicle that invests in small US companies. The fund has outperformed 93 per cent of its peers over the past five years, returning over 10 per cent annually, according to Bloomberg data.

T Rowe last week launched three new funds to complement Mr Nanda's own vehicle, underscoring the rising interest in more systematic, computer-aided investment approaches even among the big traditional asset managers.

Quantitative hedge funds , which utilise powerful computers, complex mathematical models and automated trading algorithms have been around for several decades, but Mr Nanda predicts that the traditional investment management industry will also become increasingly technology driven.

"Having a human is still important. Humans aren't going to be completely replaced, but they will be mostly replaced," he said.

Asset managers including BlackRock, Goldman Sachs Asset Management and Schroders are pouring money into new technology, "big data" and more computer programmers to ensure they do not fall by the wayside in an increasingly ruthless investment world, where many fund managers have over time failed to beat their indices .

While Mr Nanda stressed that T Rowe's own human portfolio managers have a decent record of navigating financial markets, he said it was inevitable that many jobs would naturally be done more efficiently and cheaply by computers.

"Our humans have done pretty well compared to most humans, but we definitely see a trend where machines do more and more in markets," he said. "They're becoming more powerful, so they're going to do more."

In a speech this year Andrew Haldane, the Bank of England's chief economist, considered whether there was an industrial revolution brewing in the finance industry - brought on by huge strides in technology - that might see many jobs replaced by robots.

"Driven by significant advances in technological capabilities, the explosion of big data, more powerful software and low-cost, scalable cloud computing, artificial intelligence appears to finally be at a real tipping point," the Institute of International Finance, a finance industry trade body, wrote in a report last month.

"The use of AI in financial services is likely to have an outsized impact on functions and employment as the automation of advanced roles becomes feasible," the report concluded.

A Rebuke for Bank On Pay at the Top

About one-third of voting Citigroup Inc. shareholders cast their ballots against the bank's pay plan for top executives Tuesday, a setback for a bank that has sought to improve relationships with investors.

About 64% of shareholders voted in favor of the bank's pay package, Citigroup said at its annual meeting Tuesday. That was down from last year's vote, when about 84% voted in favor. Citigroup's stock price is down about 9% this year, compared with a 4% drop in the KBWNasdaq index of bank stocks.

Citigroup has defended its pay packages, including 2015 compensation of $16.5 million for Chief Executive Michael Corbat. Jennifer Shasky Calvery is stepping down from her role as director of the U.S. Treasury Department's Financial Crimes Enforcement Network, the anti-money laundering regulator said.

Ms. Calvery will leave her role at the end of May after more than three years in the job, FinCEN said. Over that period, anti-money laundering concerns gained new importance and reshaped the way banks do business, in part because Fincen and other regulators imposed record fines for lapses in controls.

FinCEN, a small unit of the Treasury, has enforcement powers and also serves as the financial intelligence unit of the U.S., collecting millions of secret reports filed by financial institutions on their customers, including reports known as suspicious activity reports, or SARs.

More than 17 million SARS have gone to the government since bank-surveillance laws were expanded shortly after the Sept. 11, 2001, attacks.

Insurance tech start-up investment surges

Oliver Ralph, Insurance Correspondent 
477 words
25 April 2016
16:44
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCMA
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Investment in insurance tech start-ups more than tripled last year as a previously sleepy corner of the market begins to draw a surge of interest.

While areas of fintech such as payments have boomed in recent years, so-called insurtech has been slower to develop.

That all changed last year, thanks to some of the world s biggest insurers. Investments in insurtech rose from $800m in 2014 to more than $2.6bn in 2015, according to data from Accenture, the management consultancy. While much of the money has come from traditional sources such as venture capital and private equity groups, established insurance companies have also been big investors.

 We are seeing a growing focus from insurance companies on insurtech,  said John Cusano, head of Accenture s global insurance practice.  They see it as a ripe place to invest their capital and they also see it strategically as a place to invest in distribution, the internet of things, automating the back office, and data and analytics. 

Many of the world s largest insurers, including Aviva, Axa, Allianz, AIG, MetLife and XL Catlin, have established their own in-house venture capital funds and insurers have committed more than $1bn to investments in start-ups in an effort to find new ways to grow and head off potentially disruptive threats.

 There is an increasing view that insurance companies need to transform, become digital and make use of the technology, especially the big players,  said Mr Cusano.

Deals completed last year included the acquisition of LearnVest, a "http://www.ft.com/fastft/2015/09/08/us-robo-advisors-aum-could-reach-5tn-2025-chart/", by Northwestern Mutual of the US and an investment in Limelight Health, a health insurance quote platform by MassMutual Ventures and Axa Strategic Ventures.

The investment boom shows little sign of easing in 2016. According to data from CB Insights, the first quarter of the year was the second largest ever for investment in insurance technology with more than 45 deals raising $650m.

The US took the lion s share of the market in the first quarter with 60 per cent of the deals, including a $400m funding round for Oscar, a US health insurer.

However, interest is also growing quickly elsewhere. At a start-up boot-camp event this month held in a church in London s Spitalfields, 600 people turned up to see 10 insurance technology companies demonstrate their wares. They included Fitsense, which helps health insurance companies to use data from wearable devices, and SPIXII, which helps people to choose the right insurance cover.

Instech London, an initiative to link start-ups, investors and insurers, has been attracting crowds of up to 250 people to its meetings.

 We ve got an awful lot of people coming along from the insurance industry who are trying to understand what is changing,  said Paolo Cuomo, co-founder of the group.

Markets
Regulators to Call for Banks to Have Year s Worth of Liquidity; Rule to be proposed Tuesday would affect large U.S. banks and could crimp industry profits, critics say

By Donna Borak 
1,046 words
25 April 2016
23:48
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON Large U.S. banks would have to prove they have enough cash to withstand severe market turmoil lasting as long as a year under a new rule set to be proposed Tuesday.

The regulation would require about 30 of the country's biggest banks to adjust their balance sheets, cutting the odds they would run into the kind of funding crunch that crippled Bear Stearns and Lehman Brothers in 2008. But critics say it could also crimp banks' profits by forcing them to devote more resources to low-return investments or higher-cost sources of cash, and could disrupt markets by forcing banks to curb their trading in certain types of instruments or pull back from certain types of loans.

The rule is being crafted by three agencies: the Federal Reserve , the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp . and the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency . The FDIC will release details of the liquidity proposal at its board meeting Tuesday, along with voting on an unrelated postcrisis rule to rein in incentive-compensation on Wall Street. That "http://www.wsj.com/articles/new-rules-curbing-wall-street-pay-announced-1461247600" is being prepared by six regulators and was released last week by one of them, the National Credit Union Administration .

The core element of the new regulation is the  net stable funding ratio," or NSFR. This new requirement would force banks to show they have sufficient  stable funding" to help them endure a year of extreme duress. The idea is to encourage banks to rely more on sources such as core deposits and longer-term funding from small businesses, and less on short-term wholesale funding which includes instruments such as repurchase agreements that help to provide another way for large banks to get cash.

The question banks are waiting to see answered is whether the U.S. version of the NSFR will be similar to one suggested by global regulators two years ago or whether U.S. officials will follow their pattern of  gold-plating" international rules by imposing stricter requirements. Banks also fear that regulators won't just give them numerical benchmarks to meet, but issue detailed rules prescribing for them what kinds of transactions are needed to meet those benchmarks.

Critics of the coming rule say they worry that while it might make banks safer, it could undermine the smooth functioning of different parts of the financial system by forcing banks to pull back from sectors where they have traditionally played a major role essentially shifting potential liquidity problems from banks to the broader financial system.

In addition to worries within the industry,  there is significant fear among even global regulators that maybe this is a problematic result in terms of undermining market liquidity," said Karen Shaw Petrou, a managing partner of advisory firm Federal Financial Analytics Inc .

Some industry observers also warn about a  chilling effect" on long-term lending such as aircraft, shipping and project finance, according to a memo by the law firm Shearman & Sterling assessing the possible impact of the new rule. The regulation would effectively raise banks' costs of keeping such longer-term assets on their books to offset more expensive longer-term liabilities, according to the memo.

The one-year stable-funding requirement was completed in 2014 by the Basel Committee , and is one of the remaining pieces for U.S. regulators to complete their version of that global framework. These regulations are designed to make banks less likely to face distress to avert a potential collapse. The Basel proposals aren't binding, but come in the form of recommendations for regulators to carry out in their own economies.

Many of the rules are aimed at forcing banks to maintain more capital on their books to ensure they have a buffer to protect against losses from risks they take. Those have existed for years, though they have been strengthened since the crisis.

A new layer of postcrisis protections is aimed at ensuring banks have sufficient liquidity to protect against a sudden funding crunch, a modern version of a bank run, even if their underlying balance sheets are healthy. As the financial crisis began to unfold in 2007,  Many banks despite meeting the existing capital requirements experienced difficulties because they did not manage their liquidity," according to a "http://www.bis.org/bcbs/publ/d295.pdf", explaining the rationale behind it.

The first such regulation in place, completed by three main American bank regulators in September 2014, is called the  liquidity coverage ratio." That requirement calls on large banks to hold highly liquid assets such as central bank reserves, and government and corporate debt, which can be converted into cash quickly to cover a firm's obligations over a 30-day period.

The new regulation to be proposed Tuesday is aimed at addressing stresses that could emerge in a bank's balance sheet if a financial crisis lasts longer than a month.

The NSFR regulation will apply to roughly 30 U.S. banks with $50 billion or more in total consolidated assets on their balance sheets, excluding client money. But, if past practice is any indication, U.S. policy makers will likely impose a more-stringent rule on the eight largest and most complex banks that hold $250 billion or more in assets, including J.P. Morgan Chase & Co . and Bank of America Corp ., while applying a more relaxed standard to banks that have more than $50 billion in assets but aren't internationally active.

A spokesman for J.P. Morgan couldn't immediately be reached, while a spokesman for Bank of America declined to comment.

 This process should result in a regulation that reduces the probability of banks coming under short-term funding pressures," Fed governor Daniel Tarullo said in 2014 speech previewing the liquidity rules.  Maintaining more stable funding, such as retail deposits and term funding with maturities of greater than six months, will help avoid the spiral of fire sales of illiquid assets that deplete capital and exacerbate market stress."

Write to Donna Borak at "mailto:donna.borak@wsj.com"
