Centre Lane invests $5 million to $50 million in North American companies with revenue of $20 million to $500 million.

Gamut Capital Management, New York

Gamut Capital Management is expected to receive more commitments than it targeted for its debut fund, said people familiar with the matter. The firm, launched by former Apollo Global Management partners Jordan Zaken and Stan Parker, is seeking $750 million for Gamut Investment Fund I LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The debut fund is expected to gather $800 million to $900 million for its final close, the people said. Gamut will invest across a range of industries, including agriculture, chemicals, energy, technology, financial services, transportation and mining. The firm will aim for control or influential minority equity positions and can make debt investments. The fund will focus on deals that call for checks of $40 million to $125 million, but could invest $200 million to $300 million with the help of co-investors.

GP Interests, San Francisco

Former Morgan Stanley banker Mark Bradley is trying to raise as much as $1 billion for a new fund that acquires minority positions in private equity firms, said people familiar with the matter. The fund, GP Interests, is targeting stakes of 25% or less in a number of firms, the people said, and intends to pursue an initial public offering in about four years. Mr. Bradley is the former head of a group within Morgan Stanley's investment bank that handled deals with private equity firms and was a founder of San Francisco-based investment-banking firm DBO Partners LLC. He tried to raise a smaller version of the fund four years ago but put those efforts on hold as he launched DBO.

InstarAGF Asset Management, Toronto

InstarAGF Asset Management, a joint venture between investment management firm AGF Management Ltd. and alternative asset management firm Instar Group Inc., held a first close on 372 million Canadian dollars for InstarAGF Essential Infrastructure Fund. The fund, which is targeting C$750million, focuses on midsize North American infrastructure assets. About $135 million of the capital raised by the fund has been committed to two investments: the passenger terminal at Billy Bishop Toronto City Airport; and a 30-megawatt wind power development project in British Columbia. The fund is expected to hold a final close by the end of the year. InstarAGF is an independent asset manager focused on the infrastructure sector and other alternative real asset categories such as private equity, real estate, venture and mezzanine capital.

Main Post Partners, San Francisco

Main Post Partners, a consumer-oriented firm founded by former members of Weston Presidio, said it wrapped up its debut fund at $410 million, including a $10 million commitment from the firm. Main Post exceeded the $250 million target for Main Post Growth Capital LP. Investors backing the fund include Constitution Capital Partners; the US Select Private Opportunities Fund, a fund-of-funds family formed by Australia's Dixon Advisory Group; and Cordish Cos., a family-owned holding company. Main Post invests $25 million to $75 million in companies with $25 million to $250 million in revenue and $5 million to $25 million in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization. The firm prefers to be the first institutional investor in a company and expects the company's founders or management to reinvest a portion of their equity back into the newly recapitalized company.

Mainsail Partners, San Francisco

Mainsail Partners said it closed its fourth fund at $384 million within about two months of fundraising. Mainsail Partners IV LP hit its $375 million hard cap for limited partner commitments, and the firm contributed an additional $9 million, said Mainsail Managing Partner Jason Payne. The firm's previous fund in the series closed at $216 million in 2012. That fund is almost fully invested, and Mainsail expects to begin investing its fourth fund in the next quarter, Mr. Payne said. The firm backs North American businesses in the software, technology services and health-care sectors that have become successful with no outside capital. The firm looks for companies with $5 million to $25 million in revenue and generally makes equity investments within the same range, Mr. Payne said.

Morgan Stanley Infrastructure, New York

Morgan Stanley Infrastructure said it collected $3.6 billion for North Haven Infrastructure Partners II LP, plus another $2.2 billion in co-investment allocations from investors the firm can tap for deals. The latest fund will only pursue energy, utilities and transportation asset investments in countries in the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development, while the first fund in the series also looked at developing countries, said Markus Hottenrott, chief investment officer for Morgan Stanley Infrastructure. The firm invested roughly 60% of the first fund in energy and utility assets and 40% in transportation, a ratio the firm expects to remain roughly consistent for the second. The firm so far has made two investments from the fund: Eureka Hunter Holdings LLC, which operates natural gas pipelines in the Appalachian region; and Ital Gas Storage, which operates a gas storage plant in the Lombardy region of Italy. Its first fund in the series closed on $4 billion in 2008.

MTS Health Investors, New York

Health-care services-focused investor MTS Health Investors raised at least $155 million toward a $300 million offering amount for MTS Health Investors IV LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm closed its third fund in 2013. MTS invests in companies with enterprise values of $25 million to $300 million and earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of $3 million or more.

Peloton Equity, Greenwich, Conn.

Peloton Equity, a growth capital firm spun out from health care-focused investor Ferrer Freeman & Co., raised at least $48.1 million for its debut fund, Peloton Equity I LP. The firm backs companies with annual revenue of $20 million to $200 million and earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of up to $10 million. Peloton in 2014 made its first investment in digital marketing firm HealthPlanOne LLC.

Peterson Partners, Salt Lake City

Peterson Partners is seeking $200 million for Peterson Partners VIII LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm didn't disclose any capital commitments at the time of the filing. The firm's previous fund closed on more than $140 million in 2013. Its sixth fund raised $55 million in 2011. Through its private equity arm, the firm invests $2 million to $20 million in the consumer, business services, transportation and industrial sectors. The firm has separate venture capital and real estate strategies.

Pfingsten Partners, Chicago

Pfingsten Partners expects to wrap up Pfingsten Partners Fund V LP at $382 million, ahead of a $350 million target but below the $525 million the firm raised for its fourth fund in 2008. The firm raised about 43% of the fund from high-net-worth individuals and family offices, said founder and Senior Managing Director Thomas Bagley. Pfingsten has yet to back any deals out of Fund V but expects to announce the fund's first deal in the next few months, Mr. Bagley said.

Prelude Partners, Irvine, Calif.

Prelude Partners said it closed its first fund, Prelude Partners Fund LLC, which will invest in founder-owned businesses with annual revenue of $10 million to $50 million and earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of at least $2 million. The firm didn't reveal the amount of the final close. The firm aims to deploy the fund's capital within one to two years. Prelude invests in service-oriented areas such as the health-care, insurance, education and legal sectors.

Silver Oak Services Partners, Evanston, Ill.

Silver Oak Services Partners is seeking $300 million for its third fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The offering amount for Silver Oak Services Partners III LP and affiliate Silver Oak Services Partners III-A LP is larger than the $206 million the firm raised for its second fund in 2013. Silver Oak's marketing efforts may benefit from the sales in the last 12 months of health-care supply chain company NDC Inc. to Court Square Capital Partners and corporate travel management company Direct Travel Inc. to Abry Partners.

Tailwater Capital, Dallas

Energy-focused Tailwater Capital said it raised $218 million for Tailwater E&P Opportunity Fund II LP, which will take minority stakes in oil and gas exploration and production companies. The fund is the fourth the firm has raised since it was founded in 2013. The previous fund in the series closed with $121 million in 2014. The other funds, Tailwater Energy Fund I and II, focus on midstream oil and gas investments. The second fund in that series closed on $650 million in 2014.

Veronis Suhler Stevenson, New York

Veronis Suhler Stevenson has raised almost $200 million toward a $300 million target for VSS Structured Capital III LP, according to a person familiar with the matter. The firm closed its previous fund in the series on $312 million in 2009. The firm, which previously backed media companies, has shifted its focus to health-care information technology, educational technology, outsourced technology-enabled business services and informational services. Veronis Suhler Stevenson rethought its investment approach after some media holdings were challenged in a digital era that toppled traditional revenue models, the person added.

Vista Equity Partners, Austin, Texas

Vista Equity Partners set a $10 billion upper limit for its sixth flagship buyout fund, said people familiar with the matter. Vista on March 11 held an initial closing for the new fund, securing more than the $5.78 billion it raised for its fifth fund in 2014, said one of the people familiar with the fundraising. Vista Equity Partners Fund VI LP has investor demand in excess of the fund's $10 billion hard cap. Although Vista has targeted $8 billion for the fund, until February it hadn't set an upper limit on the fund's size. The firm also expects to close on $2 billion for Vista Foundation Fund III LP, according to a document released by the Arkansas Teacher Retirement System, which disclosed a $30 million commitment to the fund. The Illinois Teachers' Retirement System Board of Trustees also committed $100 million to the fund, according to a spokesperson. The small and midcap vehicle will invest in software companies valued at $100 million to $500 million, according to the document. The second fund in the series raised $1.1 billion in 2013.

Waud Capital Partners, Chicago

Waud Capital Partners hit the $1.06 billion hard cap for its fourth fund, Waud Capital Partners IV LP. Limited partners contributed $900 million, and Waud and its affiliates contributed $156 million, according to the firm, which hasn't started investing from the fund. Waud's previous vehicle closed with $487 million in 2011. In line with previous funds, the firm's latest will invest $50 million to $100 million   including for add-on deals   in lower-midmarket companies, with a focus on health-care services, as well as business and technology services.

XPND Capital, Montreal

XPND Capital said it closed its second fund, XPNDCroissance, at 75 million Canadian dollars. The fund was backed by several Quebec-based institutional investors, including Fonds de solidarit FTQ, Fondaction CSN, Investissement Qubec and Caisse de dpt et placement du Qubec. XPNDCroissance has one portfolio company: Taxelco, a company that aims to invest in and consolidate the taxi industry in Montreal. The firm launched its first fund, XPND1, in 2012.

The firm's previous fund in the series closed on about $1.5 billion in 2013, Private Equity Analyst previously reported, citing two investors in the fund. Yorktown generally backs the midstream and manufacturing segments of the energy industry, including the exploration, production, transportation, and marketing sectors, according to a document from the Regents of the University of Michigan, in which the university said it committed $15 million to Yorktown Energy Partners X LP in 2013.

Fundamental Advisors, a firm focused on distressed municipal investing, raised at least $316.1 million toward a $1 billion offering amount for Fundamental Partners III LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. China's Old Guard Get Tech Assist

By Brian Spegele and Alyssa Abkowitz 
822 words
25 April 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

BEIJING -- China's domestic technology firms are linking arms with unlikely new partners: struggling state-owned enterprises.

Alibaba Group Holding Ltd., Tencent Holdings Ltd. and Baidu Inc. are among the Internet companies teaming up with and in some cases even taking stakes in state-controlled companies that Beijing is pressing to modernize.

Inefficient state enterprises are an increasing burden on China's slowing economy, and Beijing has encouraged them to learn from the country's technology pioneers -- pushing a new industrial strategy of boosting the country's mammoth manufacturing sector with Web-driven efficiency and other gains. In one tie-up being closely watched as a potential model for collaborations between the state and private sectors, a venture-capital unit of Alibaba is investing about 300 million yuan ($46 million) in the e-commerce arm of Minmetals Development.

The flagship unit of state-owned China Minmetals Corp. is looking to Alibaba's Taobao platform as a model for a fledgling steel-sales site.

Minmetals Development, which lost around 4 billion yuan ($618 million) last year as commodities demand plunged, called the project "an important testing ground for Minmetals to deepen state-owned enterprise reform."

For Alibaba, the deal is a way into a struggling but still hugely important part of the Chinese economy, offering new sales platforms and potential new revenue. The company said it hopes its technology "can empower a wide range of industries and businesses at home and abroad."

The market for upgrading state-owned enterprises' technology is huge, industry forecasts show. Forrester Research projects China's government and businesses could buy $147 billion in tech goods and services this year, up from $124 billion in 2014.

"As digital transformation becomes the new imperative for Chinese organizations, I think we will see more of these SOE-and-tech-company partnerships," said Frank Liu, an analyst at Forrester.

China's tech pioneers, launched in the years after China's market-oriented reforms of the 1980s, unleashed a long-suppressed entrepreneurial spirit.

Companies such as Alibaba and search-engine company Baidu are a contrast with the inherently political state-owned enterprises, guided by government priorities.

But as their profits sag, the state enterprises "need to find other ways to generate new business and new revenue," said Edward Tse, CEO of Chinese consultancy Gao Feng Advisory Co. "The old model doesn't work anymore."

Having seen online marketplaces like Alibaba's reshape how goods are sold in China, they are trying to copy the Internet giant's nimbleness in hopes of similar sales success.

China's biggest oil refiner, China Petrochemical Corp., known as Sinopec, last week launched an e-commerce platform that it dubbed China's "Taobao of industry," after the Alibaba platform. To get the site -- which links oil-and-gas and other suppliers with manufacturers -- up and running, Sinopec enlisted Alibaba's help on cloud-computing functions including big-data analysis and storage.

"Sinopec wants to borrow Alibaba's successful experience in e-commerce," the refiner said.

The companies haven't disclosed financial terms, but Sinopec says it is eager to work more with Alibaba in fields from big-data analysis to information security.

Such partnerships let companies like Alibaba show Beijing they can help it achieve top goals. And state-owned enterprises or even government agencies can serve as labs for testing new technology -- as when the regional Guizhou Traffic Bureau in southwest China used Alibaba's cloud computing to build a transportation-data platform to monitor traffic and check drivers' safety records. "The public-cloud service is still an emerging market in China, so Alibaba Cloud needs a good case study for other potential customers," said Forrester's Mr. Liu.

In November, Baidu announced it would team up with China Citic Bank to launch a bank that could use Baidu's location and behavior data to help evaluate customers' credit profiles and fraud risk, complementing Citic's own traditional risk assessment. Under the name Baixin Bank, it will have a registered capital of 2 billion yuan, with Citic Bank as majority shareholder. A direct-banking license and application are pending. In another case, Nasdaq-listed e-commerce site JD.com took a 12.5% stake in an e-commerce unit of Shanghai Pharmaceuticals Holding Co. -- controlled by the city's municipal government -- for around 150 million yuan.

According to the annual report of Shanghai Pharmaceuticals, the two are cooperating on a business-to-business platform for retail pharmacies and small and midsize medical institutions. A license approval is still in the works.

Mr. Tse of Gao Feng Advisory called technology companies forces of disruption.

"They are in many cases redesigning business models and how business ought to be done. It affects the full range of other companies, [state-owned enterprises] included."

---

Kersten Zhang contributed to this article.

COMPANIES
Microsoft chief clicks into industrial internet; Convergence of the IT and manufacturing sectors sees rush of venture capital as companies vie to be in at the start

By Richard Waters in San Francisco 
1,547 words
23 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
10
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

When Satya Nadella takes to the stage in Germany on Sunday for his first appearance at Hannover Messe, one of the main events in the manufacturing industry's calendar, it will signal a significant change for Microsoft.

The chief executive of the technology company admits that in past years "I wouldn't have had much to talk about - whereas now I think I have something to say".

He will not be pitching Microsoft's traditional money-earners like Office, Windows and server software, or even its newer cloud computing services. Instead, says Mr Nadella: "We are now talking about the core." This means the data being thrown off by cars, aircraft engines, elevators and other industrial products, supplying the raw material to turn dumb manufactured goods into what he calls "systems of intelligence".

Microsoft's bid to put itself at the centre of the "industrial internet of things " echoes a wider move among software companies. It suggests that the convergence of the IT and industrial worlds may finally be picking up momentum, as companies from two very different worlds fight for the upper hand.

"It's winner-takes-all," says Bill Ruh, chief digital officer at General Electric, which is also racing to stake out its position as a supplier of the foundational software to run the industrial internet . "A cloud-based platform to connect machines and build applications into it and have global scale . . . you're only going to have a few of those."

The data gathered from connected devices will create a new "digital feedback loop" that will make it possible to apply the same data science that has been developed in other fields, says Mr Nadella. This, he adds, will lead to the same "malleability and analytical power and predictive power in industrial equipment that you had in software products for the mobile web".

The result could be better controls, lower costs and new ways of generating revenue by turning hardware sales into service opportunities.

With more of the value in industrial products shifting from hardware to software, it is no surprise that many industrial companies are reconsidering their software strategies. According to GE, the industrial internet as a whole will be a $225bn market in terms of annual revenues by 2020 - dwarfing the expected $170bn for the consumer internet of things, which has attracted more public attention, and bigger even than the enterprise cloud computing market, which is predicted to hit $206bn.

Of the new industrial software market, GE estimates that $100bn will go to a small handful of companies that provide the central platforms for the industrial internet - the software that collects and aggregates data, acts as the foundation for higher-level applications and creates shop windows for developers to reach an audience in the industrial world, much like smartphone app stores.

Until now, few companies have seen the business value in the data they could collect, says Jim Tully, an analyst at the research group Gartner. But he adds that asset-intensive companies such as power utilities, miners and manufacturers are finally starting to wake up to the potential. Rolls-Royce is typical of manufacturers that believe access to different types of data are about to open up new business opportunities. The British aircraft engine maker has been collecting information about the performance of its engines for nearly two decades to help with maintenance.

Now, says Tom Palmer, senior vice-president of services, the company is pulling in other types of data about, for example, the weather. Among the goals is to help optimise flight plans for its airline customers to minimise fuel use, one of their biggest costs.

The spread of new, more flexible cloud technologies has also made it easier and faster for manufacturers to introduce new applications. GE's slow emergence as a software company highlights the problems of the old tech world. Before the cloud, building each application from scratch involved a slow and laborious process, says Mr Ruh, since GE could not call up component services it had already written for other applications.

The US industrial group has been learning from best practice in the cloud computing industry in other ways as well. It has taken to copying consumer internet companies, says Mr Ruh, emulating everything from their datacentre technologies to their sheer speed of operations. "Amazon is one we've learnt from and watched," he says.

With the industrial internet market starting to take shape, a gold rush mentality has taken hold in the software world. Venture capital investment in companies involved in the industrial internet of things jumped 76 per cent last year, to more than $1bn, according to CB Insights, the research firm.

"It's like eight years ago, when [cloud computing] hit," says Isaac Brown, an analyst at Lux Research. "Everyone said, 'Give me some cloud' - but didn't know what it meant."

One surprise has been the large number of start-ups vying to become platform companies rather than application providers - a risky bet given the tendency for platform software markets to consolidate. Entrepreneurs drawn to the market reject the idea that the giant software and manufacturing companies have any inherent advantage. "In most parts of the tech landscape, the entrepreneurial approach is a great formula," says Brad Keywell, a co-founder of deals site Groupon and a backer of Uptake, a two-year-old platform start-up. "Entrepreneurial speed is very important."

But a shake-out seems inevitable. Other new software markets created by the rise of mobile and cloud computing have already concentrated on a very small number of winners, pointing the way to what could happen in the internet of things, says Mr Ruh. He points to smartphone software, where Google and Apple rule, and cloud computing, where Amazon Web Services and Microsoft's Azure have the strongest positions, as evidence that there are only likely to be two or three winners.

GE is hoping to follow in Amazon's footsteps , copying the ecommerce group's approach of opening up the technology developed to support its own businesses for use by other companies. "We didn't take it to market until we'd built it for ourselves," says Mr Ruh.

However, GE has yet to announce it has sold its Predix software platform to any customers that compete with its core manufacturing business, and rivals claim it will be hard for the company to establish itself as a service provider to the wider manufacturing industry. Cloud computing companies, meanwhile, have been moving steadily into the industrial internet. They may lack the deep sectoral experience of a GE, but their general-purpose systems for collecting, managing and analysing data can be adapted for the purpose, says Mr Tully. Amazon announced a push last autumn, while Mr Nadella's appearance in Hannover this weekend caps a series of steps by Microsoft.

It is shaping up to be a complex new world, where software and industrial companies are likely to find themselves allies one moment and rivals the next.

Mr Nadella rejects the suggestion that Microsoft and GE will be head-to-head competitors. But he adds, of the manufacturing companies: "Some of them maybe have broader ecosystem agendas." Even narrow applications, if successful, can provide a springboard that puts companies at the centre of a new emerging market, he says, reflecting the restless evolution of the tech world.

"One person's platform is the other's person's app," he says. "That has played out in consumer internet, and it will play out in the industrial internet."

Forging links

How machinery is being connected to the web

As with most new technologies, it is the consumer uses of the so-called internet of things that have attracted the most attention. But industrial and other business applications are likely to end up being a far bigger market.

The falling price of sensors and the rise of web connectivity have made it easy to connect machinery and other objects to the internet.

Collecting the data thrown off by all the new connected objects should make it possible to monitor them more closely, optimise their performance and foresee problems. It could also support new business ideas, like charging for hardware on a subscription basis depending on how much it is used.

The industrial internet is being assembled out of four different parts, each of which has attracted a wave of investment.

One involves the billions of products and devices that will one day be connected to the internet, with companies like Electric Imp, Particle and Arrayent creating platforms to manage this process, says Isaac Brown at Lux Research.

A second focus is connectivity. Groups such as Jasper Technologies have assembled networks for handling the machine-to-machine communications on which the industrial internet will rely.

The third area of investment is in software platforms that collate data and act as a development platform for applications.Some companies also sell higher-level services like analytics to make sense of all the data being collected.

The final element consists of software applications and services, many created for specific industries.

Richard Waters

The Intelligent Investor: Is Your Stockbroker Great or Mediocre?

By Jason Zweig 
817 words
23 April 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright  2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Most people expect the food in a three-star restaurant to be tastier than one-star grub and a two-thumbs-up movie to be better than a flick that got a solitary upward-pointing thumb. Good luck, however, finding a handy way to rank stockbrokers.

That needs to change, because new research shows the most valuable information about brokers emerges only when you can compare them and their firms industrywide.

The Financial Industry Regulatory Authority, which oversees how investments are sold, maintains BrokerCheck, a database and website that provides information on nearly 1.27 million current and former brokers. A study by Finra last fall found that BrokerCheck data could reliably predict which brokers are most likely to harm their clients.

And so could you, if you had open access to all the data Finra collects on BrokerCheck. But, contends a new report, the regulator keeps such a tight hold that the service doesn't tell investors what they need to know.

The vast majority of brokers are hardworking, honest folks who have no customer complaints on their records.

But those who have been active since before 2000, and those whose colleagues have a history of misconduct, are much more likely to generate complaints from customers, according to the new analysis.

Those insights emerge only from analyzing oceans of data on brokers and their firms -- an absurdly impractical task for ordinary investors looking up BrokerCheck records one at a time.

How much should you worry if a broker settled a complaint for $25,000? Are four arbitrations in 27 years a lot or a few? Have 13 out of 14 of the other employees in your broker's office had complaints lodged against them? There isn't a way outsiders with conventional computing power can tell. Nor can investors readily figure out which firms have the most employees with marks on their records.

And that matters -- a lot -- because taking advantage of clients seems to be contagious.

Brokers whose colleagues have spotty track records end up harming investors much more often, the new report says.

An unrelated recent study, which looked at some 150,000 brokers at nearly 1,000 firms, found that in the wake of a merger between firms, the average broker becomes more than a third more likely to incur customer complaints if his or her new brokerage colleagues have a history of misconduct.

Yet another analysis, released last month by economists at the University of Minnesota and the University of Chicago, found that brokers with a history of complaints were snapped up by other firms rather than being driven out of the industry.

So, before hiring a broker, you should know the disciplinary record of his or her colleagues. Even a report from Finra itself last fall drew a similar conclusion.

The latest study was conducted by Securities Litigation & Consulting Group, a research firm in Fairfax, Va. Part of its business is providing expert-witness testimony in arbitration proceedings against brokers and their firms. Still, "my incentives don't change the arithmetic," says SLCG founder Craig McCann, a former economist at the Securities and Exchange Commission.

The study bases its analysis on Finra's own standards for judging whether investors were harmed; it replicates several of the regulator's findings from last fall almost exactly. Finra's chief economist, Jonathan Sokobin, says the SLCG report "essentially validated our results."

SLCG pinged the SEC's website about 10 million times to determine identification numbers, then downloaded data from BrokerCheck on 1.2 million brokers, including nearly 450,000 registered before the year 2000 -- who weren't included in Finra's analysis last year.

Such longer-serving brokers are up to nine times more likely to trigger complaints from investors, SLCG found. That isn't just because somebody is bound to get annoyed at them over the course of a long career. In 2014, for instance, these veterans were only 39% of all brokers but generated 72% of the cases in which investors were harmed.

Finra long has restricted access to the raw data on BrokerCheck on grounds of privacy and the potential for misinterpretation.

But if independent firms could analyze the disclosures, as researchers like Morningstar and Lipper long have done for mutual funds, they could produce evaluations based on the risk factors that matter.

The possibility of making the data more widely available externally is "something that we're pursuing internally," says Robert Colby, Finra's chief legal officer. "We recognize that third parties could potentially provide valuable incremental information to investors, and we are carefully considering this issue."

Finra should do more than consider it. In the age of Big Data, it is time to liberate the BrokerCheck files. Only when information is set free can it turn into insight.

Markets
Why Bank Trade Group Looks to Fed for Hires; Transcript: Greg Baer, head of Clearing House Association, on agenda for capital requirements, stress tests, anti-money-laundering compliance, financial technology and other issues

By Ryan Tracy 
6,666 words
22 April 2016
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WSJ Pro Financial Regulation sat down recently with Greg Baer, the new president of the Clearing House Association a New York-based trade group of large U.S. banks, including the units of some big foreign banks "http://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-banks-new-weapon-economists-1461348432" on capital requirements, stress tests, anti-money-laundering compliance, financial technology and other issues.

Here is a transcript of the conversation, edited for clarity:

RYAN TRACY: Thanks for having me. How long have you been in the job now, six months?

GREG BAER: Since October.

MR. TRACY: And you've made some big hires recently, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-banks-new-weapon-economists-1461348432". Tell me about what the thinking was behind bringing those people in and where you see the Clearing House going in terms of where it belongs in the policy conversation and how it's trying to influence that.

MR. BAER: Right. I mean, first, I think it's a tribute to the credibility of the organization I inherited that they were willing to come here, and that the Clearing House is seen as a place where you can do credible, meaningful research. And so, you know, it's a benefit just that they were willing to come here and lend their expertise. But, you know, I think with Bill [Nelson] and Francisco [Covas] both coming out of Fed Monetary Affairs, there's no question they are very serious economists, and very thoughtful ones, and very, I think, respected ones.

And that completely complements, I think, what we're trying to do here, which is to bring more analysis and data to the question of regulation from the industry side. There has been a sea change &postcrisis, where more and more regulation is done by economists in Washington or Basel or Brussels. They do that based on their understanding of markets and economics, and we need to have people who can talk to them about those subjects.

MR. TRACY: What are some of the specific things that you would consider were decisions made in that vein?

MR. BAER: Well, I mean, when you think about the decisions that are really moving the needle for the banks, particularly the larger banks that are our members, I think there's an excessive focus on particular parts of Dodd-Frank when really the elephant in the room is the Basel capital rules, Basel liquidity rules and CCAR [the Federal Reserve's Comprehensive Capital Analysis and Review, also known as stress tests]. And those, you know, whether you are a regional bank, a foreign bank operating in the United States or a GSIB [globally systemically important bank] &those have major impact on your balance sheet and your profitability. And those are really subjects that I think most policy makers in Washington don't even understand certainly don't understand how they work and may not even understand that they exist.

You know, the potential impact of the "http://www.bis.org/bcbs/publ/d352.htm" that was just completed in Basel, I think most policy makers in Washington don't even know it happened. But, you know its potential impacts on market liquidity and financial stability are significant.

MR. TRACY: What are those?

MR. BAER: Well, I mean, I think there's a growing realization although it's obviously a very complicated and controversial topic that there is a whole lot less market liquidity across a variety of asset classes. And, you know, the causes of that I think are varied. There's no question that one of those causes is capital liquidity regulation. And so as you see declining liquidity you know, you saw this week, I think, that the FSB [Financial Stability Board] now, as a result of that, wants to enhance real regulation of the buy side, asset managers. I would hope that they would have instead thought about whether sell-side regulation may have gone too far in terms of trading liquidity for the system, you know, which has two potential downsides: One, in a crisis, you want liquidity in financial markets; and, two, to the extent that you have corporations trying to issue debt, that debt will issue on better terms of you believe that there's eventually going to be a liquid market for that, and people will pay more for it.

So I think both of those consequences probably haven't gotten well, I think there's been a fair amount of study of current market liquidity and the potential for problems in stress. I think the sort of larger, you know, economic-growth implications of having illiquid capital markets probably haven't been much addressed in the U.S. I think, oddly, they've been more examined in Europe than they have in the U.S.

MR. TRACY: And so, when you one brings up this cost-benefit idea to U.S. regulators, you hear so let me try to sort of summarize their response. I mean, I think, first of all, they say the benefits of preventing another financial crisis are very difficult to measure, and so, you know, if we did a cost-benefit analysis, it wouldn't it would be biased, in a sense, because the benefits would be really hard to quantify. And if you make us do that for every rule, then maybe we don't have a lot of these rules, even though we think they are really important. So, given that you're advocating for more cost-benefit analysis, what's your response to that concern?

MR. BAER: Well, I mean, any cost-benefit analysis has to be done at the margin, so what's the marginal benefit and what's the marginal cost. For example, there's no question that, you know, short-term wholesale funding was a major problem in the crisis and brought down the large investment banks. So the question isn't, "Should we do something to reduce reliance on short-term wholesale funding?" You know, the question is, "The next step we take, is that is its marginal benefit greater than its marginal cost?"

So consider, for example, the regulators are, you know, I think supposed to put out for comment fairly soon the net stable funding ratio, or the NSFR. You know, if that were the only thing being done, well, that would be a very important rule and would have a lot of benefit. But it's coming on the heels of the liquidity coverage ratio and a lot of capital regulation and, you know, the Fed's CLAR [Comprehensive Liquidity Assessment and Review] process and CCAR. And so then you ask yourself what's the marginal benefit of this, and then what's the marginal cost. And I think you can look at that across a whole spectrum of rules. I think the net I mean, the Fundamental Review of the Trading Book is another example where, you know, the potential for that to further drain liquidity on top of Basel you know, CCAR, liquidity rules, declining repo market it's hard to see a large marginal benefit for even higher capital requirements on trading assets, but it's pretty easy to see that there's a marginal cost.

MR. TRACY: And so should the long-term benefits of these be how should those be treated, in other words? If you're by suggesting looking at marginal costs and benefits, are you saying we shouldn't think about those long-term benefits, or what 

MR. BAER: Oh, no, no. I no, no, I think you think absolutely about long term. I mean, part of the long term is and, you know, it's funny; even when I used to serve in the Treasury Department a long time ago, everyone always talked about how the United States benefited from the most liquid capital markets in the world, but you now hear people proposing blithely, you know, further measures that would significantly reduce the liquidity of those markets, which is already significantly reduced. So when people talk about extremely high leverage ratios, you can't have a liquid, you know, government securities repo market, you know, if you have high leverage ratios, because they punish, you know, riskless assets that are necessary to the functioning of those markets.

So this is absolutely about the long-term marginal costs and long-term marginal benefits. And, you know, again, some of that is on the what does the next financial crisis look like long term, and part of it is what is the impact on economic growth medium-long-term.

MR. TRACY: On market liquidity, would you let me just play devil's advocate again. Would you guys acknowledge that there are other things going on that are affecting market liquidity in addition to regulation?

MR. BAER: I would readily acknowledge there are multiple other factors, some potentially larger than regulation, that are going on. You know, clearly, coming out of the crisis, banks, absent regulation, would have much-diminished risk appetite in these areas. They've significantly expanded and fortified the models they use to measure market risks, which has resulted in a diminished risk appetite. You also have, you know, certainly a very odd macro environment, which is having impacts that I think our economists could probably explain better than I could. But I think there's no question that regulation has had a role as well.

I mean and I think the key understanding is that the only thing that regulators and policy makers can control is that regulatory impact. They can't change the banks' risk appetite. They can't, in the short term, change the macroeconomic environment and where interest rates are. So if your concern is we may have overshot a bit and there may not be as much market liquidity as we would like, the only thing you can really change is the regulation.

MR. TRACY: Tell me about the so you mentioned hiring these two gentlemen and kind of more broadly trying to talk to the regulators more with quantitative analysis and economist to economist, or however you want to put it.

MR. BAER: Right.

MR. TRACY: What, broadly, is the goal, though? I mean, so, for example, do you want a lower leverage ratio in 10 or 15 years? I mean, what's the endgame?

MR. BAER: I think there's a big-picture endgame and then there's a sort of more micro-endgame.

I mean, the big-picture endgame, I think there are really two policy imperatives, you know, before you could get smaller-scale improvements in the world. The first is people have to understand the sort of revolutionary changes in law and balance sheet that have occurred postcrisis that really mean that the large banks are now resolvable and not too big to fail. I mean, I think the market is convinced of that, if you look at spreads and even what the rating agencies are saying now, but I don't know that the body politic is convinced of that. So I think we need to continue to be to work to demonstrate how much has changed, both legally and financially, you know, in that area.

The second really is and this is what we were talking about is there needs to be a larger understanding of the costs of regulation as opposed to the benefits. And that is, as I think you noted earlier, very hard work because it's &a noisy market environment and it's a political environment where people are not really very eager to hear that. So I think, you know, we need to be really good about doing analysis that is compelling and credible and can let regulators and other policy makers understand that there are trade-offs here.

And then, to the micro side, you know, we certainly we need to have ready for the next administration and for policy makers globally, you know, some ideas about, if you wanted to enhance economic growth and make the system more stable for the next financial crisis, is there low-hanging fruit where you could have a de minimis impact on bank safety and soundness, but a true benefit for, you know, either economic growth or financial stability, or both.

MR. TRACY: And does that mean lower capital requirements?

MR. BAER: I think in some areas. But it certainly means halting the march towards ever-higher capital requirements, which is where we are now.

MR. TRACY: OK.

MR. BAER: We are in we are at a place of accelerating capital requirements. And I think when you look, you know, at the relatively objective measures of bank capital, it's hard to argue that there [is] not a lot of bank capital. And then, if you think about a TLAC [total loss-absorbing capacity] type rule or you think about loss absorbency more generally, which includes long-term debt, you know, I think the largest banks under the Fed's TLAC proposal are going to end up with $1.5 trillion of loss absorbency. That's a lot of loss absorbency. And so it's very easy to say, oh, the banks should have more capital, and we're going to produce analysis to show that well, I think the Fed actually, if you look at its CCAR scenario, it shows just how much capital that is. So we need to convince people that that's a pretty good measure for, you know, whether this is enough capital, and it is. And then we need to put the onus on those saying we want more to say why, or to answer the question why.

MR. TRACY: On CCAR, the Fed is engaged in a process right now of looking at potentially, you know, do we need to recalibrate parts of this process. This is the process where, you know, [Fed governor Daniel] Tarullo's had meetings with the industry, meetings with analysts, meetings with academics, not sort of as part of their annual process, but more broadly, and we're expecting something on that later this year. Do you have advice for that? What is what do you think your members want to see out of potential changes to CCAR?

MR. BAER: Well, I would start by first looking at what CCAR is right now in terms of the scenarios that are being mandated for the banks. It already assumes, for example, the unemployment rate goes from 5% to 10% over seven quarters, which is far worse than the financial crisis. GDP drop is more sudden and as bad or worse than the financial crisis. The blowout in spreads is worse than the financial crisis. There are there's a market shock and counterparty failure scenario for the trading banks that is akin to the financial crisis. And then, you know, most significantly, it's, after all of this happens, under CCAR, you still have to be at relatively high capital levels not just survive CCAR; you have to be at 4.5% tier 1 common equity, and then there are a series of other hurdles. And you have to do that all assuming that you're continuing to make share repurchases and pay dividends out exactly as planned, and that your balance sheet doesn't shrink in any of the problem areas. So that's an extraordinarily difficult test, and I think results in an extraordinarily high capital requirement, which is now why it has become the binding constraint for almost all, if not all the large banks.

And so, when you look at that, I think you just have to ask yourself the question, "Why isn't that enough capital?" And, you know, you can say we want to add this surcharge to that or that buffer to that, but it's all just more capital. And, you know, to the extent that it's hitting trading businesses, it's all just that much less market liquidity. And so I think it's less important to talk about the labels and the theory, and more just to ask the question, "Why isn't this enough capital?" And I think they've constructed a highly stressful, one might even say worst-case-scenario test that should give people a lot more comfort than it appears to be giving them at the moment.

MR. TRACY: We've reported that "http://www.wsj.com/articles/washington-strips-new-york-feds-power-1425526210" much more centralized decision-making, especially for the biggest firms. Governor Tarullo has been, I would say, at the center of that. What's your relationship with him and the regulators right now? And do you feel like you get a fair hearing when you bring up things like this?

MR. BAER: (Laughs.) Let me think about this one.

MR. TRACY: Yeah. Fair enough.

(Pause.)

MR. BAER: I would say, you know, I think we absolutely get a fair hearing. I used to work at the Federal Reserve. I think it's a terrific institution. I mean, I have respect for all of the regulators, and I know they have a very difficult job to do. And, unlike when I was there, they have to do it in an increasingly politicized environment. And so I do not envy them their task.

I also don't envy them their task in that the complexity of regulation, I think, is still growing. You know, when you look at the there have been multiple issuances from Basel even in the last couple of weeks (again, which most people in the United States don't even know are occurring) around, for example, operational risk. And so I think it's just difficult for them to keep track of everything that's going on in what's become a very complex and difficult area.

So, you know, we don't think of ourselves as the opposition to them. We believe our role is to keep them as informed as we possibly can about what's going on in markets, what's going on in lending, what the results of regulation are, and to provide analysis that I think we hope will give them comfort that what they have done to date has been successful and has prevented at least, you know, certainly the type of financial crisis we saw last time, but really any financial crisis where the largest banks or the larger banks are, you know, either a cause or an accelerant.

My concern as you look to the next potential financial crisis is, have we established the largest banks as a Maginot Line, where they will, like their antecedent, you know, survive quite well and yet a whole crisis, you know, goes around them &.That wasn't very well put.

MR. TRACY: I understand the metaphor, though, yeah.

MR. BAER: And, yeah, I think that should that should be the concern at this point, is where are the risks going and how do you feel about where they are.

MR. TRACY: [The Clearing House] led the TLAC comment letter in which you asked for the Fed to do away with the stand-alone long-term-debt requirement. Why did you guys I mean, that's something that, you know, the Fed obviously was aware that the industry wanted. There has been some "http://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-chamber-touts-new-engagement-on-financial-regulation-1458085772". There's been sort of reaction to that of, you know, this what you're asking for is kind of going at the heart of the rule. Why did you guys decide to ask for such a significant change there?

MR. BAER: First, we don't believe at all that it's the heart of the rule.

MR. TRACY: OK.

MR. BAER: It's actually not included in the FSB term sheet to which they are purportedly adhering. We think it's actually an unnecessary sidecar to the rule.

MR. TRACY: OK.

MR. BAER: You know, the goal of TLAC is to have a set amount of loss absorbency, to allow, you know, either no failure by a large institution or its successful resolution with no cost to taxpayers. As I think most people who've ever been involved with regulation would acknowledge, at the margin you would always rather have more equity than more debt.

Here's how I'd put it. If you assume a TLAC requirement of 20% 

MR. TRACY: Right.

MR. BAER:  which isn't exactly what it is, but let's just assume if you have a bank that wants to run with 20% equity versus a bank that wants to run with 10% equity and 10% debt, you would always take the 20% equity because it's better for them not to fail at all than for them to fail and go through a resolution process. So it just seems counterintuitive to say we want debt rather than equity. And it's also counterintuitive to think that, even if you're meeting with equity, we want you to go out and leverage yourself more by issuing more debt.

MR. TRACY: Fair enough.

So, speaking of Maginot Lines, let's talk about AML and BSA, anti-money-laundering and the Bank Secrecy Act. There's been, obviously, a lot of enforcement in this area over the past few years. You experienced some of that when you were at J.P. Morgan. What's your assessment of where we are at between the extremes of not doing enough and doing too much on, you know, anti-money-laundering and terrorist finance?

MR. BAER: I think it's really two issues, although I think the problems are similar.

On the sanctions side, you're obviously seeing a lot of so-called de-risking, which is the polite term for banks getting rid of their clients or their correspondents. I think and obviously, they do that under pressure from regulators and because of fear that if they make a mistake, no matter how unintentional, the potential size of the fine is astronomical. And if you assume the potential for a hundred-million or billion-dollar fine for some group of relationships in a given country, it's very hard to figure what you price at to make that worth your while. So, instead, you go home. I think we're seeing that globally.

And I think there is now a growing number of people and not just bankers, but people involved in national security, international development, even intelligence policy who are asking, is it a good thing that U.S. banks, and actually European banks as well, are retreating from developing countries around the world because of fears of enforcement actions in the sanctions area? It's another great example of cost-benefit analysis. And I think, at least in the sanctions area, the costs are now being elevated and again, not by bankers, but by people who are sort of the ones who are truly affected by this. So I think there you're already having a useful dialogue, which I think we would try to attempt to foster or continue.

On the domestic side, you know, it's a little more tricky in the sense that less of it, I think, is public. But it really comes down to, you know, what's the threshold for filing a suspicious activity report. Is it, are you creating more hay, or are you helping them to find needles? And has the enforcement and regulatory environment been conducive to banks incentivizing banks to look hard for cases that are very worthwhile, or are they incentivized to just keep filing more and more SARs?

Again, I think I have now spoken to a wide range of people in law enforcement and intelligence and people who are expert at mining data to find bad guys, and I don't I rarely speak to somebody who doesn't use the word "broken" in describing the system, and I have not spoken to anybody who think that the system's working literally no one.

MR. TRACY: Wow. And why? Why is that the assessment?

MR. BAER: I think it fundamentally comes down to the Bank Secrecy Act. And you could even go back to why it's called the Bank Secrecy Act, which sort of is an indicator of how old it is. You know, it was written for a world decades ago, and not a world where you have big data and the ability to run algorithms. And it's one of my members put it wonderfully to me. You know, because we a lot of times we compare sort of how cyber is working versus how AML is working. And I think in cyber there's been wonderful cooperation, most of it unpublicized, between the banks and those in the intelligence and law-enforcement community. And there you don't file a SAR every time somebody pings your website; you deal with it as adults with a common goal, and you don't do defensive things solely for regulatory or enforcement purposes.

And someone very smart put it to me as the difference between AML and cyber is with cyber our goal is to solve a problem, and with AML our goal is to demonstrate compliance with a law to the regulators. And that tends to breed a very different regime.

MR. TRACY: And what's been the practical impact? I mean, banks are spending a lot of money on both. Does it look different, in practice, between how that money gets spent?

MR. BAER: I don't think there's any question that much more of the money is wasted on AML than on cyber. To the banks, it really isn't about the money. A lot of these banks are in New York and, you know, they want to help catch the bad guys. And what really frustrates them is not the money, but the fact that they are less focused on catching bad guys and working with, in a lot of cases, their former colleagues in law enforcement or national security, but rather that they are establishing audit trails. And that's probably not the best way to catch bad people.

MR. TRACY: So why is the money wasted? Or how?

MR. BAER: I think it's wasted to the extent that you are producing data that will never be used to identify a serious wrongdoer.

I think this is just an area where we think all well-intentioned people can conclude that there's a need for a serious change and not incremental change. And I think that's what we're going to hope to foster, you know, but really allowing the sort of end users of this information to lead the way because that's what's really important.

MR. TRACY: And that would have to be legislative. I mean, is that what you have in mind when you talk about change, that is that the goal?

MR. BAER: I think, you know, ideally legislative, but I think there are a lot of other things that could be done.

MR. TRACY: OK. Yeah, not the easiest hurdle to get a bill passed these days.

So, fintech [financial technology], let's talk about that. Do you think the playing field is level right now in terms of the rules that apply to some of these nonbank entities and marketplace lending or whatever it is?

How much of a priority is it for you to bring this up with regulators about, you know, what rules do and don't apply in that space right now? And if it is a priority, what specifically do you want to see?

MR. BAER: I mean, I think our members, you know, just they want consumers to have equal protections regardless of whether they're dealing with a bank or a nonbank performing banklike services. It's obviously a very competitive area, and competition's a good thing. And I don't think anyone wants to stifle competition. But I do think that, you know, getting back to your cyber issue, I mean, there's serious risks here. And the question is, who owns those risks, who manages those risks, and who ultimately bears liability in the case of those risks? And I think we have a way to go before, you know, consumers understand that, and where the management of the risk equals the is in the same place as the potential liability.

MR. TRACY: OK.

MR. BAER: What you hear most frequently from or at least what I hear most frequently from banks is concerns that they will ultimately have to make consumers whole for events that occurred outside their walls and over which they had no control and no ability to manage, and that's a very difficult position.

MR. TRACY: And can you be more specific about the concerns they have about where the rules may not be equal, or?

MR. BAER: Well, one we're looking at currently is about data aggregation.

MR. TRACY: OK.

MR. BAER: You know, where in some cases data aggregators work cooperatively with the bank, and there's an understanding of the data flows and there are protections and there's transparency to the customer. But I think there are other cases where the bank is even unaware that the data is being scraped and the customer is probably unaware of what his or her rights are in the event that there is, for example, a cyber event or something like that.

MR. TRACY: What's your assessment, broadly, of where the too-big-to-fail debate is? I mean, we've had some campaign rhetoric on both sides about that. Obviously, we know y'all think that a ton has been done to address that. But what's your assessment of where the debate is?

MR. BAER: If you think about a series of concentric circles with the core being knowledge and the outer circles being ignorance, I think the closer you get to the center, the more sanguine you are about too big to fail. I think the TLAC proposal &is a perfect example. I mean, there's no reason for the Federal Reserve to require the largest banks to hold, you know, over $1.5 trillion of total loss absorbency except to effectuate a resolution that will not require taxpayer support or create financial instability. I'd like to think they will say that when they finalize their rule. But I think you hear people debating too big to fail who don't even know that that's happening, or don't care.

So it's I mean, it's quite frustrating. But I do think, among informed regulators, there's a whole lot more comfort than there was. And then, you know, again, to the extent that you look at what's the market's view, I mean, holders of long-term debt of large banks clearly believe that they are at extraordinary risk in the event of failure, and now the rating agencies are following along. So, I mean, that they could always be wrong, but you know, markets tend to be pretty smart. And so I would hope that that's a comfort to regulators and others that, you know and it certainly means that you're not getting any, you know, unfair competitive advantage as a result of that. But I think that's pretty good evidence that there's been real change and that, you know, debtholders are at risk. Which means, of course, that at the levels of loss absorbency that the Fed is going to require, it's pretty difficult to imagine a scenario where that isn't sufficient to cover the losses.

MR. TRACY: So you feel like empirically there's the evidence is on your side at this point, in other words?

MR. BAER: I think the market perception is evidence. But you also have analysis in the sense that those who have those who say that, you know, Title I and Title II [of Dodd-Frank] somehow don't work never really seem to explain why. You have to believe that the levels of loss absorbency wouldn't be sufficient, which I think analytically is very difficult to do, or you'd have to believe that the regulators would violate the law and protect the debtholders even when they're prohibited from doing so. And nobody really explains how or why that would happen. It's very difficult to imagine it happening.

So, yeah, I think we're ready with our analysis and evidence, but the other side and maybe that's a good sign does not seem to come forward with analysis and evidence on the other side.

MR. TRACY: Let's talk more about Basel. How do you kind of broadly view what's going on? And which direction is it going? Is it going in the direction that you guys are concerned about, or is it is it neutral?

MR. BAER: I mean, I think one great debate that's going on at Basel and with, you know, U.S. regulators participating, but again, outside of U.S. view is really the question of, you know, what should be the binding constraint for bank capital and what is the relative role of what are the relative roles of bank internal models of assessing risk, regulator-created standardized models creating one governmental view of risk, and stress tests like CCAR although there really aren't any other stress tests like CCAR outside the United States.

And that's why you are seeing a series of proposals around standardized approaches to measuring various types of risks. I think there's a very vigorous debate going on around, you know, what their role is relative to internal models. I think in some corners there is a deep distrust for any bank internal model. I think that distrust is unwarranted, at least for U.S. banks, in the sense that the rigor around those models is extraordinary, and their review both internally and by the regulators is extraordinarily robust. But there are certainly countries that participate in Basel who don't believe any bank model could ever be right. Then there are on the other hand, there are people who view who believe that the cost of running those models and going through all that review is too high, and you're better off just going with some standardized model and saving yourself the trouble. And that's a really, really important debate to have because I think, ultimately, it has real implications for credit allocation and economic growth, which, again, have not really received the attention they deserve.

So, again, that's why I think we're going to try to be more engaged in that international debate, because that will eventually become a U.S. debate.

MR. TRACY: OK. What's been the impact of the you mentioned how CCAR is different from other countries. One way I understand that's very different is you have this rejection or approval of the capital plan in CCAR. What's been the impact of that just mentally on the way banks think about the regulators, I mean, and is that a good thing or a bad thing?

MR. BAER: I think certainly vis--vis investors it's a very difficult thing because it's very important to be able to tell your investors what your capital requirement's going to be. And with CCAR, with the scenarios changing every year, with perhaps the model changing every year but we don't know, and potentially with the amount of capital you have to have at the end of it changing, it's very difficult to tell investors here's how much capital we have to have. And so there's no question that that is a difficult thing for the banks, and then a difficult thing ultimately for the economy because the economy tends to do well when banks are able to do good projections and gather investors and lend money and support markets. So that's certainly a cost of a CCAR process.

On the other hand, I mean, I think we and I think most of the banks support the notion of stress testing as the best way to assess bank capital. And so, certainly in theory and that sounds like damning with faint praise, but a little more than theory the idea of using a stress test as a binding constraint does not seem to be a bad one. I think the greater frustration is, again, with, as we talked about earlier, you know, the severity and stringency of CCAR is extraordinary, and yet, no one seems to be or not no one many people seem to be either ignorant of it or understating what it means to have passed that test.

MR. TRACY: What's your reaction to "http://www.wsj.com/articles/feds-new-bank-critic-keeps-heat-on-1459720148"? What does it say about the broader debate here?

MR. BAER: I think debate is always good. I think, you know, my frustration with "http://www.wsj.com/articles/minneapolis-feds-kashkari-says-must-consider-options-for-ending-too-big-to-fail-1459783192" is that it takes as a given or a premise something that I don't believe to be true, which is that too big to fail still exists, notwithstanding the myriad policy actions that we've been discussing, and that we now need to get about the business of breaking up the banks or imposing wildly higher capital requirements.

From what I understand, I don't think there are going to be the types of proposals that lend themselves to the data and analysis that we want to engage in, you know, debating policy issues. So I think we'd welcome the chance to debate on an analytical and data basis the whole series of questions that are not even on the table there because they've been conclusively presumed against the banking industry.

MR. TRACY: How are you enjoying this job? What are you what's your reaction, having moved and started doing this public-sector work?

MR. BAER: It's the issues that have consumed me for quite some time, and working with a great group of people, and having the resources to really, you know, explore the right answers. You know, it's a terrific place to be.

Plus we also have a payments company. And with our real-time payments initiative, that's become, you know, a very exciting endeavor. So on some level it feels like just working at a startup fintech company as well as working at a large bank, you know, association. So, you know, on both sides, it's a terrifically exciting place to be.

MR. TRACY: And what is what should our readers know about that payments company work?

MR. BAER: Well, I mean, we're developing a clearing and settlement system that will allow for real-time payments, which ultimately I think will have economic benefits, consumer-customer benefits, although it's more on a wholesale side at this point. But you've seen systems like that have been developed in the U.K., Singapore, other countries. You can argue we're overdue in the United States. But we're certainly making every effort to get this done as quickly as possible.

MR. TRACY: What's the goal for it? What's the timeline on that?

MR. BAER: OK. We're going to begin a pilot program Q1 of '17, so we'll be testing with the banks.

MR. TRACY: OK. Well, thank you so much.

Threat Intelligence Company Anomali Raises $30M

By Cat Zakrzewski 
249 words
21 April 2016
13:34
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Anomali Inc., formerly known as Threatstream, has raised new funding as it rebrands and looks to expand its new threat intelligence offerings.

The company grabbed $30 million in a recent Series C round led by Institutional Venture Partners. Previous investors from General Catalyst Partners, Paladin Capital Group and GV, formerly Google Ventures, also participated in the round.

The company announced its rebranding as Anomali in February at the RSA Conference, one of the security industry's largest annual events. With the new name, the company now offers a product that focuses on small businesses as well as a new enterprise product that provides breach analytics.

The company has also continued to offer its original product, ThreatStream, which is now in its sixth iteration. The tool enables businesses to share threat information with others they have identified as trusted peers.

At the RSA Conference where the company announced its rebranding and new products, many of the attendees said they were growing fatigued with threat intelligence services, a part of the cybersecurity industry that became overheated in recent years.

 It doesn't feel crowded to us because we are in a particular vertical that is more technology focused," said Anomali Chief Executive Hugh Njemanze.

Mr. Njemanze said Anomali's platform sits above most threat intelligence tools, and that the company's platform makes them usable for enterprises.

The company has raised more than $56 million in funding to date.

Pro Private Markets
Fraud Prevention Company Forter Secures $32M Series C

By Cat Zakrzewski 
278 words
21 April 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Investors are betting Forter Ltd. can help retailers limit fraudulent charges online.

The company has closed $32 million in Series C funding led by Scale Venture Partners, with previous investors Sequoia Capital and New Enterprise Associates participating.

Fraud liability falls on the retailer or merchant. Forter Chief Executive Michael Reitblat said companies lose billions every year in fraudulent purchases, and even more revenue in charges they stop because their existing fraud-detection tools generate false positives.

The company takes a fully automated approach using machine learning to detect fraud in purchases. Its clients include retailers such as Delivery.com, Heels.com and Jomashop.com.

Mr. Reitblat said Forter covers any fraudulent charges that make it through its system.

He said companies that use Forter tend to see between a 5% and 10% increase in revenue. The company's primary market is U.S. retailers, and he said Forter also enables companies explore opportunities in Asia, a market some avoided because of the high instances of fraudulent purchases.

The Forter team consists of former PayPal Inc. executives and former officers from the Israeli Cyber Command. Its business operations are based in San Francisco, while research and development is in Tel Aviv.

As the company expands, it faces competition from another fraud detection platform, Signifyd Inc. That startup announced it "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00120160225ec2pi2wto" earlier this year. Mr. Reitblat said he is confident his fully automated solution can compete with Signifyd's, which relies on a combination of machine and human intelligence.

With the new round, Forter added Scale Venture Partners' Rory O'Driscoll to its board.

EMC Misses Expectations but Says Dell Deal Remains on Track; Company says merger to close by October, cites an excess of unfilled orders for results below views

By Robert McMillan and Lisa Beilfuss 
946 words
20 April 2016
22:44
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

It's been a bumpy road but Dell Inc.'s blockbuster acquisition of EMC Inc. is on track to reach its next major milestone, a vote by EMC shareholders, within the next few months.

The deal, now valued at $60 billion, $7 billion less than it originally was worth, would have Dell Inc. take over storage company EMC by October. EMC chief Joe Tucci on Wednesday reaffirmed that all systems are go.

 We expect the transaction to happen on the original terms and within the originally announced time frame," Mr. Tucci said during a conference call with analysts.  Integration planning has accelerated &, the leadership team has been established and we've received the vast majority of antitrust approvals required."

Financial analysts also believe that the deal will be approved.  Given the way stocks are going right now, there's a good chance the deal will go through," said Abhey Lamba, managing director with Mizuho Securities USA Inc.

Dell's parent company, Denali Holding Inc., is completing a proxy filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission, and the shareholder vote is expected six weeks after that. That pushes the vote back to June. Dell and EMC had hoped the shareholder vote would take place by early May.

A series of missteps since the deal was announced have hammered the share price of VMware, a publicly traded EMC subsidiary in which the parent owns an 81% stake. The problems lowered the payout that EMC shareholders could expect if the deal went through. In October, when the acquisition was announced, Dell's offer, partially pegged to the price of VMware stock, was worth more than $33 per share. On Wednesday, it had fallen to nearly $30 a share due to the slide in VMware's value, while EMC shares traded just above $26

Shares of VMware have dropped 25% since the deal was announced, but they were up 13% on Wednesday on news of a stock buyback, as well as quarterly results that suggested the merger plan hadn't hurt the company's financial prospects as much as some financial analysts had feared.

Dell's big bet comes at a time when incumbent technology giants are struggling. On Tuesday, Intel said it "http://www.wsj.com/articles/intel-to-cut-12-000-jobs-1461097009", about 11% of its workforce. IBM on Monday reported its 16th-straight quarterly decline in revenue.

And the former Hewlett-Packard Co. has spun off its printer and personal computer businesses in a divestiture that stands in contrast to Dell's bid to consolidate diverse IT products and services under one umbrella.

EMC's revenues have missed analyst expectations for six straight quarters as its core businesses have shrunk. Sales in 2015 of its high-end storage gear dropped 14% while its midrange business was down 18%, according to the industry research firm Gartner Inc. The two businesses accounted for $3.9 billion in revenue. The company's flash storage business offset some of those losses, growing by 122% to $1 billion.

 The spending environment continues to be challenging," said EMC Chief Financial Officer Denis Cashman,  as customers focus more on transformative IT projects while also minimizing transactional spends."

Tying up with privately held Dell will give EMC breathing room to focus on longer-term opportunities, Mr. Tucci said.

 There's an advantage to being private," said Patrick Moorhead, president of Moor Insights & Strategy. He pointed to Dell's rising market share and ability to operate out of the spotlight, disrupting competition without having to telegraph moves and without the quarterly scrutiny of investors and Wall Street analysts.

 What Dell has shown is they can handle the complexity where HP said they couldn't," Mr. Moorhead said. Whereas HP determined that its businesses were better off divided between two entities, the combination of EMC and Dell  makes sense," he said.

HP said there's a difference. Dell and EMC are looking to  get larger, lever up their balance sheet, and double-down on mostly legacy technology," an HP Enterprise spokeswoman said via email. HP by contrast is looking to focus on new technology, while  de-levering the balance sheet," she said.

EMC reported first-quarter profit of $268 million, or 14 cents a share, up from $252 million, or 13 cents, a year earlier. Excluding the expense of stock-based compensation and restructuring, among other items, earnings per share were 31 cents, flat from the year-ago period.

Related

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/eu-approves-dells-takeover-of-emc-1456755339"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/emc-begins-selling-novel-data-storage-system-1456743605"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/emc-profit-declines-as-merger-costs-dollar-weigh-on-results-1453900237"

Revenue slipped 2.5% to $5.48 billion. Analysts projected 33 cents in adjusted per-share profit on $5.63 billion in sales, according to Thomson Reuters.

EMC attributed the disappointing results to an excess of unfulfilled orders at the end of the quarter. Unshipped storage-product orders totaled roughly $75 million due to the timing of bookings within the quarter, according to Mr. Cashman.

Sales in the VMware business rose a better-than-expected 5% from a year earlier, and the company backed its earlier sales guidance for the year. In EMC's Pivotal segment, which offers cloud and big data software by subscription, revenue soared 56% from last year's quarter.

Those gains were offset by a decline in the company's information infrastructure business, its biggest, where sales declined 5.9%.
