Pro Private Markets
Imaging Advantage Teams Up With MIT Members on Artificial Intelligence 

By Amy Or 
681 words
20 April 2016
14:35
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Imaging Advantage LLC, a cloud-based imaging company backed by Goldman Sachs Group Inc., among others, is hoping to introduce artificial intelligence in the reading of X-rays.

The company, which also counts private-equity firms Brightwood Capital Advisors and CRG as investors, teamed up with faculty members from the Massachusetts Institute of Technology and Harvard Medical School's teaching hospital, Massachusetts General Hospital, to develop a machine learning initiative named Singularity Healthcare to take a first look at digital X-rays.

Naseer Hashim, founder and chief executive of Imaging Advantage, said the idea behind Singularity is that technology will examine X-rays and highlight potential problematic areas of injury and disease before sending the images to one of the 500 board-certified radiologists connected to Imaging Advantage's cloud-based network.

 The whole idea of deep learning is that the machine is becoming better looking at analogies," said Mr. Hashim.  If it detects a potential problem area in one patient, it will also have the knowledge, hopefully, of many more similar indications."

He added Singularity will continuously learn from Imaging Advantage's expanding database of 7 billion images.

 The machine will read what radiologists determine in other cases and come up with a potential response on what that is," he said.

Founded in 2007, Imaging Advantage, of Santa Monica, Calif., received Goldman capital in 2010 and again in 2012, for a total of $40 million. Goldman invested through its direct private investing operations offered through the New York firm's merchant banking division.

The capital injection allowed Imaging Advantage to provide radiology services to hospitals, and assist with practice management, day and night final teleradiology, and specialized radiology billing.

New York firm Brightwood, which targets the lower midmarket, joined Goldman as an Imaging Advantage investor in 2012. CRG, a Houston-based, health care-focused investment firm, pledged $250 million in equity and debt to the company in 2015.

There has been a push to introduce technology in health care. Although electronic medical records have become commonplace for the past couple of years, health care lags behind other industries in the deployment of analytics, often takes on an assisting role in facilitating physicians in back-office functions and hasn't played that big of a part in treatment.

The Singularity project to be launched later this quarter is still far from allowing machines to take over the diagnosis-making process, but it is the first step to let technology aid physicians and clinicians in the more commoditized part of their job.

According to the Government Accountability Office, the U.S. handled 30 million imaging services in 2010 (the latest data available), three-quarters of which were X-rays, ultrasound and other standard imaging.

In Imaging Advantage's experience, according to Mr. Hashim, 50% of all radiology tests are X-rays.

 This technology, once we establish its advocacy, I believe radiologists will gravitate toward it," Mr. Hashim said.  Radiologists don't like reading plain film. It is the lowest reimbursement modality. Given a choice, they'd rather focus on MRI and CT."

Mr. Hashim said adding artificial intelligence to the reading of X-rays will hopefully aid the faster turnaround of tests and potentially help eliminate errors or misses on X-ray reading.

 Even a small percentage improvement in the highest use modality is incredible," Mr. Hashim said.

Singularity is named after the hypothetical event in which artificial intelligence surpasses human control. However, Mr. Hashim said the technology isn't set up to take over the jobs of radiologists.

 I don't believe the market will be ready to have nonboard certified radiologists reading exams from a patient perspective," Mr. Hashim said.  I don't think insurance companies will support it. You are looking into the distant future, even if the technology is perfect, before radiologists come out of the equation."

"http://www.imagingadvantage.com"

Finance Watch; A roundup of news items about business and finance

641 words

The Wall Street Journal Online

TD AMERITRADE

Profit Rises 8.5%

TD Ameritrade Holding Corp. said its quarterly profit climbed 8.5% as revenue at the brokerage rose and client trading activity increased.

Interest-rate-sensitive assets, which include spread-based assets and money-market mutual funds, grew by 11% to a record $112 billion, with the firm pointing to organic growth and changing investor sentiment in a volatile market environment.

For the fiscal second quarter ended March 31, TD Ameritrade reported earnings of $205 million, compared with $189 million a year earlier. On a per-share basis, earnings rose to 38 cents from 35 cents a year earlier, meeting analysts' expectations. Revenue increased to $846 million from $803 million, below analysts' projections of $856 million, according to Thomson Reuters.

TD's average daily trades rose 6.8% from a year earlier to 509,120.

The brokerage brought in $14 billion in net new client assets in the quarter, bringing total client assets to $711 billion, which the company said was a record.

The firm was also able to control expenses, which edged down 0.8% from the prior year.

Joshua Jamerson

REAL ESTATE

Grosvenor Warns Boom Is Ending

A major U.K. landlord warned that the boom in global real estate is ending.

London-based Grosvenor Group, which manages investments on behalf of the Duke of Westminster, said in its annual report that years of rising property values could be set to reverse. While it isn't possible to predict when the market will turn,  it is only a matter of time," said Nicholas Scarles, group finance director, in the report.

Real-estate investment surged after the 2008 financial crisis, pushing values of commercial and residential property to records in cities around the world. With interest rates low, property returns looked attractive compared with other assets.

Stock-market volatility, low oil prices and political uncertainty have damped demand. High-end housing markets in cities like London and New York have softened in the past year. Commercial-property transaction volumes totaled $1.2 trillion in 2015, down 2% from 2014, according to data provider Real Capital Analytics.

Grosvenor continues to  expect and plan for a slowdown, particularly in high-end commercial and residential property," Mr. Scarles said. Other historic landlords in Britain have also been preparing for a downturn.

For Grosvenor, preparation includes selling assets and pursuing development opportunities expected to mature during the next market upturn, he said.

In addition to high property values,  there is a risk that sustained low oil prices could lead to sovereign wealth funds reducing investment in high-end commercial and residential property in London and elsewhere," he said.  All of this points to a correction in the near future."

Grosvenor manages a 6.7 billion ($8.42 billion) property portfolio spanning Hong Kong to San Francisco.

The jewel in Grosvenor's crown is a 300-acre plot of central London that has been in the family since 1677. Once a marshy pasture, it now encompasses the Mayfair and Belgravia neighborhoods, home to hedge funds and embassies.

Prices of luxury housing in these areas have fallen in the past year.  In the U.K., the top end of the residential market in London has passed its peak, due in part to the recent changes to stamp duty, along with the strength of sterling during 2015," said Mark Preston, Grosvenor's chief executive, in the report.

In 2015, the total return for Grosvenor was 9%, down from 13% in 2014. Pretax profit was 526.6 million, down 23% from 681.8 million in 2014.

 While we have seen performance above the cycle average in each of the years since the financial crisis, we anticipate the next few years will bring a more challenging environment," Mr. Scarles said.

Art Patnaude

Tracy s Take: FSOC s Asset-Management  Update ; The Wall Street Journal s financial regulation newsletter for Monday, April 18, 2016. 

1,925 words

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO

Tracy's Take: FSOC's Asset-Management  Update'

Chinese Lender's Woes Expose Its Global Tentacles

EU 's Mogherini Pushes Closer Banking Ties With Iran

Federal Reserve Corrects Letter to Morgan Stanley on Living Will

SEC Coders Open Doors for Enforcement

FSOC's Asset-Management  Update' What to Watch

When a panel of top regulators meets at 5 p.m. Monday to lay out their views on asset managers, they won't take drastic action.

But they will, according to a public notice, issue an  update regarding ... ongoing work" on the sector that could, nonetheless, have a big impact on the regulatory environment for the $50-trillion-plus industry led by BlackRock Inc ., Vanguard Group , and others. Watch for suggestions about blocking certain types of high-risk mutual funds to retail investors, or curbing hedge fund borrowing. While not binding, the report will influence rulemaking in the coming months.

The approach to be unveiled by the Financial Stability Oversight Council  a 10-headed Hydra created by the 2010 Dodd-Frank Act illustrates the panel's evolution as it struggles to define its role and powers over the nonbanking portion of the financial sector that fueled the 2008 crisis.

Early on, FSOC took a heavy-handed approach, formally designating three big insurers, along with General Electric Co .'s financing arm, as  systemically important" and thus subject to extensive new oversight. That prompted a backlash, culminating in a judge last month nullifying that designation for MetLife Inc .

After mulling doing the same to asset managers, FSOC changed course. They've spent four years studying how to steer that industry, opting for a lighter touch. Today's  update" will be the culmination of that work.

The suggestions won't automatically turn into regulation. Yet they will carry the influence that comes with the signatures of Treasury Secretary Jacob Lew , the FSOC chairman, Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen , an FSOC member, and eight other senior officials on the council.

Today's report will cover four areas the council laid out for its inquiry into asset managers: liquidity and redemption risk, leverage, operational risk, and resolution (regulatory parlance for failure or bankruptcy).

Officials have talked more publicly about the first two, indicating those are priorities. In the other two areas, regulators may simply call for more study.

Specifically, Treasury officials have questioned whether certain types of mutual funds ought to be available to retail investors, given their liquidity or cash needs especially those funds that offer daily withdrawals while investing in hard-to-sell assets. On Thursday, Antonio Weiss, counselor to Mr. Lew, pointed "https://www.treasury.gov/press-center/press-releases/Pages/jl0422.aspx" to the Third Avenue Focused Credit Fund's decision to suspend investor redemptions in December. Don't be surprised if FSOC encourages the Securities and Exchange Commission to curb such funds.

FSOC could also call for more action on leverage, or borrowing, associated with securities lending, an activity where funds loan out securities they own. Fed governor Daniel Tarullo"https://www.federalreserve.gov/newsevents/speech/tarullo20151117a.htm" about investment funds taking on  bank-like" risks.

Regulators may also recommend more to rein in borrowing by hedge funds. Analyst "http://capalphadc.com/downloads/2016/4/hedge_funds_and_asset_managers_beware_the_spurned_fsoc.pdf" that the minutes of January's FSOC meeting were the first in at least 18 months to mention hedge funds, as part of a discussion about how some aren't subject to borrowing limits.

Monday's  update" could be much more than simply that.

 Artificial intelligence -focused Numerai raises $1.5m

Robin Wigglesworth, US markets editor 
569 words
18 April 2016
08:49
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A hedge fund focused on artificial intelligence has raised $1.5m from a group of investors led by a founder of Renaissance Technologies, one of the world s biggest money managers, underscoring the growing interest in applying cutting-edge technology to markets.

"https://numer.ai/"was founded by South African mathematician Richard Craib last year and uses a monthly  tournament  for data scientists to develop models using AI fields such as  machine learning  to predict stock market movements, with the best predictions winning money from the company.

Unlike Quantopian, a similar embryonic  crowdfunded  hedge fund, San Francisco-based Numerai provides data scientists and computer programmers with encrypted financial data.

They then use the data to independently develop their own models scouring for patterns and submitting their results to the hedge fund, which then synthesises the best ones into stock market trades. "https://www.quantopian.com/"members submit completed trading algorithms.

 It s just a clean data challenge. We manage all the irritating things like the fund structure and the trading,  Mr Craib said.  They can build whatever model they want and simply submit their predictions. 

The Numerai investment group was led by "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Howard_L._Morgan", a former computer science professor and one of the founders of Renaissance Technologies, the immensely lucrative but secretive science-powered hedge fund.

 Richard has a new take on how to use crowdsourced artificial intelligence in a very innovative way,  Mr Morgan said.  The homomorphically encrypted data means it s just abstract data. It could be weather data or pharma data, it just happens to be financial data. 

Some of the investors   who include Naval Ravikant, a co-founder of AngelList and a seed investor in Uber, and Playfair Capital, a London-based venture capital group specialising in artificial intelligence   also put an undisclosed sum into Numerai s hedge fund, which otherwise remains closed to outside investors.

 We have so much to do on the technology side and on the tournament design, so I want to focus on that for now,  Mr Craib said. But with typical Silicon Valley understatement he added that eventually  I want to manage all the money in the world .

Numerai has also appointed "http://diamandis.com/", the chairman of the X Prize Foundation and the Singularity Universe, to its advisory board. He will join Vitaly Shmatikov and Arvind Narayana, two machine learning experts at Cornell and Princeton Universities respectively, Kaggle board member Ash Fontana, and Norman Packard, a chaos theory physicist and founder of "http://www.newyorker.com/magazine/1999/04/26/black-box", a cutting-edge hedge fund in the 1990s.

The hedge fund, which was started in October last year, initially traded only using Mr Craib s own model, but now mostly uses the predictions produced by the contestants at its tournaments, which started running in December.

 My model was doing very well, which is why I felt confident I could start a hedge fund, but within 10 days of the first tournament I had an even better model,  he said.  We now have 80 people with better models than me, which is depressing, but it s good for the fund. 

The current leader on Numerai s tournament board is a user called  "https://numer.ai/ai/ncvsai" , who works in genomics and biostatistics, according to Mr Craib. He has earned $5,563 since starting to submit predictions earlier this year, but as the hedge fund grows Numerai intends to ramp up the rewards.

COMPANIES
Man Group steps up investment in AI; Financial services

By Robin Wigglesworth in New York 
468 words
18 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
14
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

AHL hedge fund says results of 'machine learning' show promise

Man Group's computer-powered AHL hedge fund plans to ratchet up its investment in " machine learning ", a hot new area of artificial intelligence that is garnering increasing attention in the investment industry.

AHL, which manages more than $19bn, is known for investment strategies that follow trends and its two main funds have returned an average of 12-13 per cent annually since the mid-1990s.

But the success of its machine learning experiments in recent years led the company to plough more money into the field, and it is now the single biggest investment area at AHL, according to Sandy Rattray , chief executive.

"It's very powerful, and could unleash a very important period for quantitative investing," he said.

AHL has been researching machine learning - a field of artificial intelligence where dynamic algorithms pore over vast data sets for patterns - for five years, and has been using the technique in trading for the last three years. The results have been encouraging, according to executives at the hedge fund.

A machine learning strategy helped one of AHL's funds swing from a narrow loss to a narrow gain in August last year, when markets were convulsed by concerns over China, by autonomously buying and selling stock at vital junctures in the turmoil. Many traders initially stood on the sidelines, unable to quantify rapidly-changing data.

"It learnt to buy the dip . . . when we showed it a lot of data," said Nick Granger , co-head of research at AHL. "No one taught it to do this. It responds to the market environment, which makes it much more dynamic."

Machine learning is an increasingly popular niche of AI that is being used for everything from generating film recommendations for Netflix to Google's translation service.

The AI field has been around for some time, but increasingly fast computers allow fund managers to use machine learning on an accelerating data deluge.

Some experts are more sceptical over the utility of machine learning to trade financial markets, pointing out that financial data are immensely complex, chaotic and more "noisy" than other industries. The concern is that it has become a faddish concept in quantitative investment.

Mr Rattray concedes that machine learning has become a buzzword, but says AHL's results using the AI technique shows it has immense promise.

The success of AHL - its four main vehicles returned between 3.9 and 7.6 per cent in the first quarter, despite the turbulent markets - helped keep Man Group's overall funds under management relatively steady at $78.6bn, as net inflows of $1.3bn into its "quant" funds offset outflows from its more traditional hedge funds.

Man Group, one of the world's biggest hedge-fund managers, reported higher client inflows during a choppy first quarter for investment firms, led by its computer-driven funds.

The London-based firm posted $500 million of new client money, compared with withdrawals of $1.3 billion a year earlier, although it sustained $700 million of investment losses during the period.

Man's shares rose more than 5% on Friday in London trading.

The first quarter "has been a difficult one across the industry for both flows and performance. Against this background, Man did well to see flat assets under management and [$0.5 billion] inflows, we think," said Bank of America Merrill Lynch analyst Philip Middleton.

Man -- which has been trying to turn itself around under Chief Executive Manny Roman after years of client withdrawals -- has been boosted by the revival of its computer-driven hedge fund unit AHL.

The flagship AHL Diversified fund rose nearly 34% in 2014, ranking it as one of the world's best-performing hedge funds. It lost money last year and is up 3% this year.

Funds including one that trades niche markets, such as German power or emerging-market interest-rate derivatives, and one that uses newer investment styles such as machine learning, also have made money.

That has helped lure back clients, with a net $1.3 billion flowing into AHL and other computer-driven hedge funds during the first quarter, reflecting renewed demand across the hedge-fund industry for such funds.

Man said that 71% of AHL's open-ended funds were at their so-called high-water mark -- the point at which they can start to earn lucrative performance fees.

"We believe that Man's efforts to diversify its product range and broaden its distribution network have resulted in net inflows during a volatile and uncertain [first quarter]," said Peter Lenardos, an analyst at RBC Capital Markets who had forecast net outflows of $400 million during the quarter.

Investment losses and more than $1 billion of withdrawals at its discretionary unit GLG helped pushed Man's overall funds under management down to $78.6 billion from $78.7 billion at the end of last year.

"The ongoing uncertainty in the markets remains challenging and, accordingly, the risk appetite of our clients has the potential to affect flows," Mr. Roman, the chief executive, said in a statement.

In February, the firm reported that profit last year had fallen 17%, hit by lower performance fees and higher compensation costs.

New York Fed's New GDP Report Diverges From Atlanta's

By Katy Burne, Ben Eisen and Paul Vigna 
907 words
15 April 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
7
English
(Copyright (c) 2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

The race to provide credible real-time data on U.S. economic growth is pitting the Federal Reserve Bank of New York against its sibling in Atlanta, to the puzzlement of some traders.

The New York Fed said Tuesday that on Friday it would begin issuing a report tracking U.S. gross-domestic-product growth. In doing so, the bank is indirectly facing off with the Federal Reserve Bank of Atlanta, which has published its own regularly updated GDP estimate, GDPNow, since July 2014.

Adding to the intrigue, the rival measures offer vastly diverging views of how the economy is doing. An initial estimate from the New York Fed's FRBNY Staff Nowcast called for tepid first-quarter growth at 1.1%, but the Atlanta Fed measure is showing a near stall at 0.1%, according to the bank's website. Final official figures won't be released until late June.

The divergence is confounding traders, who have used the Atlanta Fed reading to gauge the health of the economy and predict whether the Fed will move rates up or down. Some said the New York Fed's announcement about its new tool caught the market by surprise.

"I have been getting a lot of questions from clients," said Torsten Slok, chief international economist at Deutsche Bank AG. "They want to know which one they should look at."

The tussle underscores the rising investor demand for real-time snapshots on GDP. Growth has been uneven since the financial crisis and major market indicators such as oil prices, interest rates and inflation expectations recently have shown an unusual level of volatility, limiting their usefulness to traders and analysts seeking to divine the direction of the world's largest economy.

The limits were most visible in the market's U.S. recession scare of January and February. The Dow industrials fell as much as 10% during that episode before recovering. The index was up 2.8% for the year as of Wednesday.

While U.S. economic data are widely viewed by many investors as the most reliable in the world, the reports typically are released with a lag, reducing their value to investors who are eager to get an edge on rivals in making and hedging directional bets.

GDP trackers are rapidly becoming a preferred tool for measuring the health of the economy among everyone from bond and currency traders to global economists. The demand for speedy data has led private companies to offer their own GDP estimates, although those have had a harder time getting traction with traders since the Fed issued its own. Most Wall Street banks, and firms including Moody's Analytics, have GDP models.

Robin Anderson, a senior economist with Principal Global Investors in Des Moines, Iowa, says she often receives questions from people inside her firm about GDPNow, and she looks at it regularly as an input in her forecasts.

"It's a nice way to see what the current data releases that go into GDP are telling us about where GDP is tracking," she said.

The Atlanta Fed's tracker has expanded its following in recent months, as its first-quarter growth estimates fell from 2.3% in mid-March. Since then, search interest on Google for the term "GDPNow" has grown more than threefold.

That said, some caution not to read too much into GDP trackers because of their large swings. Since they push out an estimate before all of the inputs are finalized, the trackers warn that there is less accuracy at the beginning of the tracking period than the end.

The Atlanta Fed's GDP tracker grew out of growth forecasts that were prepared for the Fed's Federal Open Market Committee, which meets eight times a year. Because final GDP data are released only four times a year, the Fed Board staff started preparing their own advance forecasts. In doing so, they developed a model that they say is more accurate than simple statistical models.

GDPNow takes 13 subcomponents that go into GDP and constantly recalculates the forecast as new data are supplied. The use of the subcomponents is crucial; many models don't take this "bottom-up" approach and develop estimates for those subcomponents and so can miss granular changes in the economy, researchers said.

The New York Fed's models seek to capture the most important macroeconomic data and digest it as news headlines, "mimicking the way markets work," according to the New York Fed.

Both seek to take the mix of past results, newly reported data and forecast data and through their models produce an overall rate of change, expressed as a percentage.

The Atlanta Fed's tracker is updated the same day that major new data arrive, often within hours. The New York Fed will release its new estimates every Friday, with the exception of Fridays during its blackout periods around Fed policy meetings.

New York's Nowcasting tool was in the works for more than a year and is based on a similar methodology to Atlanta's, a person familiar with the matter said.

Mr. Slok said he told clients to take an average of the two Fed numbers until both have longer track records. "Over time, we will figure out which one is better," he said.

COMPANIES
Facebook hopes bots will send positive message; Technology: Artificial intelligence 

By Hannah Kuchler in San Francisco 
893 words
14 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
14
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Social network urges groups to use new platform on Messenger and help transform industries

Facebook wants businesses to use bots to communicate with customers on its Messenger service, in the hope that making its artificial intelligence tools available will transform industries.

The largest social network launched the Messenger bot platform on Tuesday, with initial partners including Bank of America, online travel service Expedia, Burger King, the fast-food chain, and news organisations such as CNN and Business Insider.

David Marcus , head of Messenger, said bots - AI-powered systems that respond to human requests - had the potential to become a "new paradigm" that would create new companies, in the same way that apps, sensors and GPS services led to the rise of ridehailing apps such as Uber.

"Traditionally in history when things become a lot easier for people, then it transformed industries," he told the Financial Times at F8, Facebook's annual developer conference in San Francisco. "Maybe what we're starting to build today will enable companies to build experiences of tomorrow that are a lot easier to use."

The move comes just weeks after Microsoft chief executive Satya Nadella outlined plans to put chatbots at the centre of its future efforts, declaring "bots are the new apps". This month, Kik, a Canadian chat app, opened a "bot shop" for apps, where users can find services run by brands such as make-up retailer Sephora.

Facebook Messenger's 900m monthly users will be able to chat with automated systems to perform simple tasks such as ask for information and submit orders. They will also be able to communicate with bots run by news organisations, asking for the latest headlines on particular topics, for example.

The social network is betting that people are tiring of downloading a new app for functions they do not use every day and find surfing the mobile web clunky.

By deploying bots within Facebook Messenger, people will be able to communicate with companies without having to remember extra usernames and passwords, and with the context of their previous interactions recalled by the software.

Developers seeking to create more sophisticated bots with a wider range of functions can access Facebook's Bot Engine, the same technology it is using to develop M, its virtual personal assistant and the group's long-term bet on AI.

Mr Marcus, a former chief executive of online payments company PayPal, said about 80 per cent of time on smartphones is spent in apps, but about 80 per cent of that time is spent in just five apps. Facebook dominates the rankings, and Facebook Messenger was the fastest-growing app in the US last year.

The company is also launching a trial to allow marketers to send sponsored messages to existing customers to reengage with them.

Mr Marcus said Facebook was being "very careful" and setting rules on what advertisers are allowed to do because Messenger is mainly used for one-on-one or small group conversations and is a private space. The company says it does not want to clog it up with corporate mailshots.

"Facebook is controlling the quantity and quality because we want to ensure that what happened to email will never happen to Messenger," he said.

The new plans are the first signs of the company aiming to develop a revenue stream for Messenger, which Facebook has funded with sales from advertising in the main app's news feed. Brands will be able to pay to promote their bot in Messenger in the news feed, so people can find it and begin a conversation. Facebook will generate revenue from sponsored messages but the company says it does not intend to take a cut of any transactions made through the Messenger app.

Industries such as news and music have already been disrupted by social media, which has in many cases disintermediated their relationship with customers. If the new bots are successful, more industries including retail and online travel could become increasingly reliant on Facebook Messenger.

But Mr Marcus said companies were attracted by Messenger's huge audience.

He gave the example of an airline executive who would be pleased to reach just 10 per cent of his passengers. Messenger could enable the company to reach about 80 per cent without as much investment in its own app.

"The reach and richness of the experience is extremely appealing to businesses," Mr Marcus said.

Brian Blau, an analyst at technology research firm Gartner, said businesses were nowhere near as technologically advanced as Facebook is pushing them to be, with some just making the move from paper to digital or engaging in their first experiments with mobile.

"There are some brands on the edge that like to use the latest technology and have no problem," Mr Blau said, adding these were often action sports, drinks or travel brands.

But "99.9 per cent of the rest of the businesses on the planet are all behind".

As groups try to keep up with the pace of change in social media, they have to rely on platforms such as Facebook because they cannot build their own AI or virtual reality, Mr Blau said. "It is a risky situation for businesses to be in but then, what choice do they have?"

DigitalGenius Ltd. wants to use artificial intelligence to enhance the work that human customer-service agents do.

From Twitter to chatbots, the platforms that companies use to interact with their customers have ballooned in recent years.

 Artificial intelligence by itself won't take over the customer-service world," DigitalGenius Chief Strategy Officer Mikhail Naumov said.

That is why he wants to use artificial intelligence to help human customer-service representatives respond more quickly and accurately to complaints. Using old customer-service records, the company trained a deep-learning algorithm to accurately respond to emails, social media and mobile messages from customers.

The company announced it raised $4.1 million in funding from Salesforce Ventures, as well as BloombergBeta, NovelTmT, Singularity Investment and Spider Capital. Previous investors including Lerer-Hippeau Ventures, Lowercase Capital, Metamorphic Ventures and RRE also returned for the round.

He said DigitalGenius integrates with Zendesk and Salesforce to help answer questions and complaints that come to customer service agents. If it can answer the question a customer poses with a certain degree of confidence, it produces a fast answer. If it is below a certain confidence level, it gives the answer to a human representative who can approve and personalize it.

Because DigitalGenius uses a machine-learning technique called deep learning, the algorithm gets smarter as the customer-service representatives use it more.

 The artificial-intelligence trainer is the customer-service representative," Mr. Naumov said.

The company, which is based in London and New York, has worked with BMW, Unilever and Panasonic. DigitalGenius also works with an airline and telephone company.

Mr. Naumov said the service won't eliminate existing jobs because the number of customer service is increasing so dramatically. He said the  human touch" is essential to the service.

Onfido Background Checks wants to help fintech companies and on-demand startups verify identities and complete background checks.

The company has raised $25 million to expand operations in the U.S. from Crunchfund, Indivest Partners and Wellington Partners. Onfido wants to bring online the lengthy background check process that usually occurs in-person.

Onfido is currently working with companies like Handy and TaskRabbit to help them verify the identities of their workers. The company uses machine learning to carry out its verifications, so the product becomes better at spotting fraud the more checks that it does.

As it pushes into the U.S., Onfido will face competition from Checkr, a company that also conducts background screenings online. Checkr announced it raised $40 million in funding in March. The company has secured many clients in the sharing economy, including Instacart, GrubHub Holdings Inc. and Uber Technologies Inc.

Investors are increasingly scrutinizing the valuations of private companies, and some are skeptical about the viability of many consumer on-demand companies.

 Changes in valuations do not take away from growth and individuals coming on to their platforms," said Onfido Chief Executive Husayn Kassai.

Mr. Kassai said the company is focusing on expanding to fintech companies as well as on-demand services and believes this diversity will allow the company to pull through any headwinds in the sharing economy.

Checkr said it was pushing farther into peer-to-peer commerce and marketplace business. It counts Warby Parker and Zenefits as clients.

Mr. Kassai said Onfido sets itself apart from traditional background checks because of its machine-learning algorithms.

 Typically with background checking companies, the more checks they do, they become worse because they use human processes," he said.  For us, the more we do the better we become."

The rise of the sharing economy has put background processes in the spotlight of regulatory decisions. Some advocates believe background checks on drivers should require an in-person check with a fingerprint, but Mr. Kassai disagrees.

 We can remotely and robustly identify individuals," Mr. Kassai said.  We're doing a lot of work to show how ours is superior."

The Property Report
Italian Property IPO Set to Debut

By Art Patnaude 
725 words
13 April 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright  2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Italy's main stock exchange is on the cusp of welcoming its first new real-estate company in a decade.

Milan-based Coima Res plans to raise 215 million euros ($245.1 million) from U.S. and European investors in an initial public offering this month. It would be the first property IPO on the FTSE MIB since 2006, according to data-provider Dealogic.

Seeking to jump-start a stagnant sector for real-estate investment trusts has been challenging, with investors fretting about volatile markets and the general lack of transparency in Italian property.

Coima reduced the target amount last week from 300 million euros in part because of such concerns. It postponed the IPO in January because of market volatility stemming from investors' worries about slowing economic growth.

Compared with January, the "general environment in the market is more volatile now, and more of a challenge," said Manfredi Catella, Coima's chief executive. "We're fully aware we're opening the market in Italy for the REIT industry."

Investor sentiment toward Italy has deteriorated significantly from a positive start to the year. In March, Italian and Swiss stocks were the least favored among European fund managers, according to a Bank of America Merrill Lynch survey. The FTSE MIB is down around 17% from the start of January in highly volatile trading.

Adding to worries, last week Italy's government cut its already cautious growth forecasts for 2016 and 2017 to 1.2% and 1.4%, respectively, from 1.6% for both years.

Coima's fraught journey to the Milan bourse follows failures of other Italian real-estate firms. Two Italian property groups withdrew plans to go public last year -- one of them twice, in July and again in October, Dealogic data show. Other firms' plans for IPOs never materialized.

Meanwhile, demand for Italian commercial real estate is booming as international investors hunt for returns amid low interest rates. Commercial property transaction volumes in Italy rose 66% in 2015 from 2014, according to Real Capital Analytics.

The $9.7 billion of deals last year was just 3.4% of the European total, the data show. A lack of transparency and low deal flow are limits totransactions, analysts said.

U.S. private-equity firms like Blackstone LLP have been very active in Italy. But longer-term investors, like pension funds and sovereign-wealth funds, have largely struggled to find adequate large-scale acquisitions of properties like office buildings or shopping malls, Mr. Catella said.

Options also are slim for investors wanting to buy shares in Italian property firms, with only two major REITs listed in Milan: Beni Stabili SIIQ and Immobiliare Grande Distribuzione SIIQ.

A successful IPO from Coima is "potentially very positive for the REIT sector" both in Italy and Europe, said Peter Papadakos, managing director at real-estate firm Green Street Advisors. "It could be taken as a sign that the market for IPOs is more open than we thought," he said.

The listed real-estate sector in Europe is relatively small. According to Green Street, the market capitalization of European REITs is just 15.4% of the global total, compared with 48.4% in the U.S. and 30.8% in the Asia-Pacific region.

But markets where REITs were almost nonexistent just a few years ago -- such as Germany, Spain and Ireland -- have been growing. German REITs made up more than 19% of the European listed property market in March, compared with 3.7% at the start of 2010, Green Street data show. REITs also have sprung up in Spain and Ireland after governments eased regulations.

The Italian government softened its REIT regulation in late 2014. Analysts said Italy could be next to take off, although they cautioned that major challenges still exist.

Coima Res is being launched by Coima, a property firm set up by the Catella family in 1974. In 2007, Coima formed a business with Texas-based property investor Hines to build the 28-building Porta Nuova, one of the biggest redevelopment projects in Milan. Mr. Catella bought the majority interest from Hines's Italian fund-management business last September.

Porta Nuova was sold to sovereign-wealth fund the Qatar Investment Authority in two deals in 2013 and 2015.

The Economist explains; What bots are

626 words
12 April 2016
News Analysis from Economist.com
ECOCOM
English
(c) The Economist Newspaper Limited, London 2016. All rights reserved 

As the app economy matures, a new platform emerges on smartphones

 I PROPOSE, if and when found, to take him by his beastly neck, shake him till he froths, and pull him inside out and make him swallow himself." P.G. Wodehouse thought up this treatment to punish a rival in love. But his words are also, according to Benedict Evans of Andreessen Horowitz, a venture-capital firm, a pretty good description of what is happening in the world of software for smartphones, which is constantly bundled, unbundled and rebundled. The latest product of this digital sausage-making process are  bots", or  chatbots", to be precise, which will feature prominently at Facebook's developer conference on April 12th. What are they? And will they, as some predict, be the new apps?

Bots date back to the dawn of the internet age. Early users of the network could already send short messages to computers, which would reply in a programmed way. One of the first bots was a service which suggested drinks to its users, called Bartender. AOL's Instant Messenger, a once popular messaging service, has long offered them, including SmarterChild, an early version of a virtual assistant, which was retired in 2008. Now a new generation of bots has begun to emerge. Digit, for instance, allows users to interact with their bank accounts and find ways to save money. MeeKan sets up meetings for users of Slack, a popular corporate-messaging service. Pana, an online travel agency, is an example of a bot which relies on both automation and humans to turn text messages into bookings. In June Telegram, a messaging app with Russian origins and more than 100m users, launched a bot platform and a  bot store". It now counts thousands of bots, such as news alerts from media organisations, or feeds that link to football videos or porn.

If bots are again multiplying, it is thanks to several developments. As services based on artificial intelligence improve, they need a way to talk to real people and bots are an option (which is why they are also called  conversational interfaces"). Download numbers are still growing, but the app economy is clearly maturing: a quarter of all apps are abandoned after a single use. Only instant messaging bucks the trend: more than 2.5 billion people now have at least one messaging app installed, meaning that they are used to a text-based interface. Users should also find bots smoother to use than apps: installation takes seconds; switching between bots does not involve tapping on another app icon. And both Facebook and Microsoft have an interest in pushing bots: most smartphones are powered by operating systems controlled either by Apple or Google; the bot market, by contrast, is unconquered territory.

Whether bots will become the new apps is an entirely different question, however. Much will depend on  killer bots" hugely popular services that work best in the form of bots and turn using them into a daily habit. Toby Coppel of Mosaic Ventures, a venture-capital firm, sees health care as a promising market. Bots could deal with routine ailments and send difficult ones to a doctor. Ted Livingston, the founder of Kik, a messaging app which has launched a "bot shop", expects  instant interaction" to dominate. He predicts businesses won't just have phone numbers and web pages, but bots too. Restaurants could take orders via instant message as some do already in China. Yet the most successful  killer bot" may be of another sort: a personal bot (let's call him  botler"), who automatically deals with the digital deluge generated by other bots.

Politics
U.S. Clarifies Role of Lead Auditors in Corporate Financial Reports; Public Company Accounting Oversight Board wants parties in group audits identified and supervised

By Tatyana Shumsky 
509 words
12 April 2016
22:47
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Auditors who contract other accountants or accounting firms to review a company's financial reports would need to increase supervision of the work they delegate under a proposal from the government's audit-industry regulator.

The proposal, issued on Tuesday by the Public Company Accounting Oversight Board, clarifies the lead auditor's responsibilities for planning, supervising and evaluating the work of other auditors. The new rules also would require the lead auditor to disclose which portion of the financial statement was audited by other parties and identify those auditors.

 Investors depend on the lead auditor to provide assurance that there are no material misstatements in audited financial statements or material weaknesses in internal control, no matter where those misstatements or weaknesses may reside," PCAOB Chairman James R. Doty said.

These group audits have become more common in recent years, as companies with international operations require accounting audits that span the globe. About 80% of Fortune 500 audits performed by so-called U.S. global network firms, which have affiliates around the world, involved other auditors, according to a PCAOB analysis of data from Audit Analytics and Standard & Poor's.

PCAOB inspections show that the other contracted auditors on average reviewed financial information covering between a third and half of the total assets and revenue of the audited company.

The proposed rule aims to buttress the lead auditor's ability to detect or prevent flaws in the work of other auditors.

It will require greater communication between the lead auditor and other auditors, access to other auditors' work papers and calls for the lead auditor to supervise the areas of greatest risk.

 It just makes sense for the lead auditor to take responsibility for the areas that pose higher risks," said Ken Bertsch, executive director of the Council of Institutional Investors, an organization of pension funds and other asset owners that focuses on corporate governance. Mr. Bertsch said his organization is still reviewing the proposal, but is broadly supportive of the PCAOB's effort.

PricewaterhouseCoopers LLP, one of the Big Four accounting firms,  is supportive of the PCAOB in undertaking their project to strengthen the supervision of other auditors," said Mike Gallagher, managing partner audit quality at the firm.

The PCAOB move comes in response to regulator and investor concerns about material misstatements in such audits, particularly those in jurisdictions with less stringent financial reporting standards.

 These are large, complicated audits that span the globe and our oversight activities have shown that we really do need better oversight of auditors in this area," said Jeanette M. Franzel, a PCAOB member, at the board's meeting on Tuesday.

The board is accepting public comments on this proposal through July 29, including those regarding the amount of time auditors would need before it becomes effective upon approval by the Securities and Exchange Commission.

Business
Three Stubborn Types of Mistakes Dog Financial Reporting; Errors involving debt and equity, cash flows or taxes accounted for over half last year s revisions and restatements

By Tatyana Shumsky 
979 words
12 April 2016
01:47
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

An internal auditor at Bank of America Corp., routinely sifting through the bank's financial reports last summer, noticed something awry with its cash-flow statement.

More checking confirmed that the bank had misclassified $3.4 billion in noncash transactions as investments, rather than funds from day-to-day activities over the previous six quarters, said a person familiar with the matter.

As a result, Bank of America joined Nike Inc. and Alphabet Inc. as one the 663 U.S. companies that filed financial revisions or restatements last year, according to research firm Audit Analytics.

While that number is down 60% from a decade ago, three types of accounting missteps remain stubbornly common,  a trend some auditors say suggests that finance chiefs are struggling with complex rules, rather than trying to mislead.

Over half of last year's corrections involved debt and equity, cash flows or taxes. The frequency of these errors has more than doubled since 2002, when the Sarbanes-Oxley corporate-governance law was enacted, partly to increase managerial accountability.

 These areas are complex, and they happen late in the financial-reporting process, late in the audit process, so it actually makes sense that there are mistakes in these areas," said Trent Gazzaway, a national managing partner at Grant Thornton LLP.  I think that you were always going to see these three issues very high up."

In last year's third-quarter earnings statement, Bank of America reported that correcting its mistake had no impact on its income or balance sheet, an example of how the severity of accounting errors has declined in recent years.

Restatements which correct mistakes serious enough to make a company's previous financial reports unreliable accounted for just 24% of last year's corrections, down from 68% in 2005. By contrast, a revision involves a small correction to financial statements that passes the regulatory tests for having no material impact on the company's account of its performance.

The decline in restatements at least partly reflects tougher regulations and enforcement, which encourage more vigilance.  Better internal controls mean you will have fewer mistakes," said Don Whalen, director of research at Audit Analytics.  When they do a make a mistake, they seem to find it faster."

Errors in accounting for debt and equity often crop up because companies are increasingly dealing with complicated financial contracts and instruments. Two months before private-equity firm KKR & Co. took it public, payment- technology company First Data Corp. received a letter from the Securities and Exchange Commission asking questions about the company's treatment of certain debt.

First Data said that when it refinanced debt with borrowings of similar maturities, it had long classified the debt as modified, rather than extinguished. But company executives decided that approach was wrong. The reversal resulted in a $56 million loss from the debt extinguishment for 2013. And it led to a further $79 million loss in 2014. The changes were treated as immaterial, warranting only a revision, and not a restatement.

 There are many estimates made in accounting, which are vetted throughout a reasonable range of expectations," said Thomas Gibson, former chief accounting officer of Accretive Health Inc., a Chicago-based provider of support services for physicians. The company restated its 2011 figures at the end of 2014, and filed its 2012 and 2013 financials late. Mr. Gibson said he spent eight months on that effort.

Accountants have a leg-up in compiling cash-flow statements because they know from the outset a company's cash position at the start and the end of a reporting period. Still, cash-flow errors are the No. 2 source of financial revisions and restatements.

Nike said it miscategorized $312 million in foreign-currency balances held by some of its foreign subsidiaries for the six months ended Nov. 30, 2014. Nike erroneously recorded these figures as the results of swings in foreign-exchange rates, rather than cash from operations.

Nike revised its fiscal 2013 and 2014 financial statements, but said the error had no bearing on its net income.

 The company has well-established internal review procedures that identified these immaterial misclassifications and corrected them," a Nike spokesman said.

Tax errors are the third-most-common cause of financial restatements. Experts say complex tax codes are to blame.

Taxes contributed to a $711 million revision by Alphabet in 2015 the year's biggest. In last year's second quarter the tech giant found it had for years incorrectly apportioned sales to its business entities outside the U.S. That meant it booked lower taxes than it should have.

Alphabet, then known as Google, retroactively raised its income-tax expense for 2008 through the first quarter of 2015. It also reclassified certain revenues by attributing them to its U.S. business, and not its  rest of world" operations.

Despite the overall size of the revision, the mistake wasn't considered material because its impact was diluted when spread out over the period, the company said. The error didn't affect the company's consolidated revenue.

But dollar amounts aren't everything. Valeant Pharmaceuticals International Inc. has been dogged by revenue-recognition troubles that appear to center on just $58 million, a sum it erroneously booked in 2014.  Some investors and short sellers have questioned Valeant's business model and accounting practices, driving down the company's share price.  

The company recently told investors and regulators it will need to restate its financial results for 2014 and the first quarter of 2015, for which previous reports aren't reliable.

A Valeant spokesman declined to comment.

Maxwell Murphy and Richard Teitelbaum contributed to this article.

Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Livongo Health, which has created a digital-health platform for managing diabetes and other chronic diseases, has secured $44.5 million in a Series C financing that should enable it to turn profitable next year.

New investors included Cowen Private Investments, Merck Global Health Innovation Fund, Sapphire Ventures, Wanxiang America Corp. and Zaffre Investments. Existing investors Draper Fisher Jurvetson, General Catalyst Partners, Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers and 7wire Ventures also participated. Livongo raised a $20 million Series B round last year and a $10 million Series A financing in 2014.

Livongo clients include self-insured companies that aim to lower costs tied to diabetes, which affects 29.1 million Americans, according to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention.

Livongo's system includes a cellular glucose meter that transmits blood sugar readings to the company's cloud. Livongo uses analytics to process the data and provide guidance to patients. It also employs certified diabetes educators who provide coaching and instruct users on what to do when their blood sugar rises too high or low.

Someone with a very high or low blood sugar reading would be contacted by a Livongo diabetes educator within 60 to 90 seconds, according to Chief Executive Glen Tullman. The company's system also connects diabetes patients to their care team, which can include medical professionals, family and friends.

Livongo, whose customers include employers like Lowe's Companies Inc. and Office Depot Inc., finished last year with 5,000 people using its system. That figure now approaches 10,000, and by year-end Livongo expects to have 40,000 users, Mr. Tullman said. The Mountain View, Calif., company expects to turn profitable in mid-2017, he said. He declined to discuss its revenue.

This financing will enable Livongo to improve its ability to provide personalized information patients and to expand its offerings. The company is looking at ways to help patients manage other chronic conditions, such as hypertension and depression, according to Mr. Tullman.

Other venture-backed companies with mobile technologies for diabetes patients include Telcare Inc., whose cellular-enabled blood glucose meter tracks glucose readings and connects with the patient's care team.

Merck Global Health Innovation Fund will gain a seat on the Livongo board. The representative joining the board has yet to be disclosed.

Pro Private Markets
Mixpanel CEO s Unicorn Dreams Take a Back Seat to Cutting Costs

By Patience Haggin 
1,058 words
11 April 2016
16:04
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Eighteen months ago, Suhail Doshi vowed that Mixpanel Inc., the app analytics startup he co-founded, would be valued at $1 billion, making it what Silicon Valley calls a unicorn.

He nearly made it, securing a $65 million investment from Andreessen Horowitz that valued the company at $865 million.

But Mixpanel's efforts to fuel rapid growth cost the startup, which is now struggling to regain its stature in a highly competitive industry. Its story reflects those of other startups seduced by a period of highflying valuations in a venture industry that is now cooling as investors write down investments and slow their deal pace.

To move forward, the 27-year-old chief executive of Mixpanel said he needs to forget about growth or fundraising and instead get the company's spending under control.

Mr. Doshi and co-founder Tim Trefren dropped out of Arizona State University in 2009 and wrote software to help websites track users' visits, clicks and conversions. Amid the recession they raised $12 million in seed and Series A funding from venture-capital firms Andreessen Horowitz and Sequoia Capital, as well as an elite list of angel investors. The company spent almost nothing on marketing but caught on with developers. It reached profitability in 2012.

In the fall 2014 Mixpanel had $22 million in the bank and didn't need more money. But Mr. Doshi raised more anyway, telling The Wall Street Journal he wanted to  re-up the risk in the startup."

Andreessen Horowitz wouldn't give him the billion-dollar valuation he sought, but the near unicorn status stake came in higher than any of the half-dozen venture firms Mr. Doshi pitched. Sequoia Capital didn't participate. A person with knowledge of the matter said the firm had a conflict due to another investment.

In a recent interview, Mr. Doshi said Mixpanel  could have pushed our investors" to value it at $1 billion in 2014.  We really kind of just realized that at the end of the day, that was just ego getting in the way of things." At that time, there were 79 private companies valued at $1 billion or more by venture-capital firms world-wide, according to The Wall Street Journal and Dow Jones VentureSource.

Like many unicorn hopefuls, Mixpanel sacrificed profitability for growth. During 2015 and 2016, Mixpanel planned to triple its sales staff, open offices abroad, acquire machine-learning startups and develop new analytics products for sales and finance, according to the pitch deck it used to raise the Series B.

The growth made Mr. Doshi, whose only prior professional experience was a summer internship as a software engineer at Slide, the very involved CEO of 200-plus employees. More experience joined the helm when Matt Cooley became chief revenue officer in summer 2014.

Mr. Doshi spent six months persuading Mr. Cooley to leave his position as vice president of world-wide sales at New Relic Inc., an enterprise analytics platform then valued over $1 billion and months away from an IPO. Mr. Cooley, who was well-liked by staff, took over sales hiring and added about 60 positions. In April the company opened an office of about 20 in New York's SoHo.

At Mixpanel's founding, it occupied a lucrative niche. Freemium pricing put it within reach of startups that couldn't afford legacy tools like Adobe's Omniture. But Mixpanel's position slid once competitors began offering the company's core usage metrics at steep discounts, according to former employees.

 There's kind of a basic part of analytics that's been commoditized," said Spenser Skates, chief executive of Mixpanel competitor Amplitude Analytics Inc., which, in the words of one former Mixpanel employee,  definitely tried to cut the legs out from under us." Clients such as education startup Coursera left for competitors, former employees said. Mr. Doshi declined to comment on particular customers, but said Mixpanel had also taken customers from lower-priced competitors.

Mixpanel repeatedly missed sales targets and failed to hit its 2015 revenue goal, according to former employees. Yet Mixpanel nearly met its ambitious hiring goals. It doubled its employees, reaching about 240 in early January 2016. Mr. Doshi declined to comment on the company's revenue or targets.

As mutual funds wrote down the value of their shares in highly valued startups such as Dropbox in the fall of 2015, industry experts predicted that venture capitalists would tighten their purse strings. Around the start of the year, Mr. Doshi said he spoke with a banker  to determine how much risk we could take." He declined to provide details but said the conversation persuaded him to set aside growth and reassess spending.

In January Mixpanel laid off 18 employees, primarily in sales. Mr. Doshi said the company cut its weekly catered lunch budget by $2,000 and shelved plans for acquisitions, international offices and new analytics products for sales and finance.

Mr. Cooley left Mixpanel in early March. Mr. Doshi declined to discuss the circumstances of his departure. Mr. Cooley confirmed he was no longer with Mixpanel but declined to explain why.

Mr. Doshi said he has no plans to raise funding. He declined to discuss the company's burn rate or how much it had in the bank.

At $865 million, its valuation in its 2014 financing, Mixpanel is one of the highest-valued among comparable analytics startups. Industry experts say Mixpanel may soon find itself in the same position as cloud-storage companies that won high valuations before Google and Amazon drove down prices. Dropbox, valued at $10 billion in 2014, has seen mutual funds write down their shares in the company. Box went public last year to a strong debut, then saw its market capitalization fall by about half. A similar fate may await some of the 146 private companies valued at $1 billion or more, experts say.

The cuts have hit Mixpanel's competitors as well: Localytics laid off 37 people, or 15% of its staff, in January. Optimizely laid off 40, or 10% of its staff, in March.

Still, Mr. Doshi said the company is still growing. He said most of the cuts have  had to do with how to do more with less."

Tech Firms Help Get Women On Track

By Georgia Wells 
913 words
11 April 2016
14:42
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

As tech firms compete in an escalating battle for talent, some are targeting what they consider an undervalued source: women returning to the workforce.

Companies including International Business Machines Corp., Google parent Alphabet Inc. and PayPal Holdings Inc., as well as smaller startups, are using internships and other programs to help women get back to work and up to speed on the latest technology they need to be competitive in the workplace.

 The war for talent is so extreme that we're seeing CEOs sitting around, saying,  Who have we not gone after? Maybe we need to find women who are at home with kids?'   says Valerie Frederickson, CEO of human-resource executive search firm Frederickson Pribula Li.

Interning helped Lisa Stephens, a former IBM engineer, land a job after taking time off to raise her two sons for 20 years. To prepare for re-entering the workforce, she enrolled in an IT management Master's program.  The whole idea of software had changed very much since I learned it in the  80s," Ms. Stephens says.

When she applied for jobs at tech companies, however, none called her back. So Ms. Stephens completed a paid internship for women returning to the workforce at email-marketing firm Return Path Inc. near Denver. After 14 weeks of coding exercises, she was hired full time as a software engineer at Return Path.

The tech industry has a shortage of qualified job candidates. The unemployment rate in Silicon Valley is less than 4%, lower than the California and national rates, according to the Silicon Valley Institute for Regional Studies.

 Every company today is dealing with how to bring in good talent," says John Donahoe, chairman of the board of PayPal. Hiring women returning to the workforce  is a source of competitive advantage."

Companies seeking out women who have been out of the workplace for an extended time say they are easier to hire because there is less competition for them.

Amy Pressman, co-founder of customer-relationship management startup Medallia Inc., found it difficult to compete for job candidates during her company's early days five years ago. She reached out to women after learning that one of her classmates from Stanford's Graduate School of Business had a hard time finding work after taking a six-year hiatus to raise children.

 We made a conscious decision to focus on hiring people others were ignoring," Ms. Pressman says.

Many women face hurdles in returning to work after stepping away for an extended period.

Nearly 90% attempt to resume their careers, but only 40% land full-time jobs, according to the Center for Talent Innovation, a nonprofit research organization focused on minority groups in the workplace. About 25% of women who attempt to resume their careers take part-time jobs, and roughly 10% become self-employed, the Center said.

The programs that companies such as Google have piloted to help women engineers return to work is in response to the skills gap the women may face. Due to the fast-changing nature of the tech industry, the gap can be greater than in other sectors.

 You can't just take a 10-year pause, come back and be fully effective," says Diane Flynn, chief marketing officer for startup accelerator GSVlabs, a portfolio company of investment firm Global Silicon Valley.

Last year, she started the firm's Reboot Career Accelerator for Women, an eight-week program that teaches skills such as design thinking, shared calendars and personal branding. IBM provided initial funding, and Google and Facebook have invited Reboot's students to visit their corporate campuses. Employees from Apple Inc. and LinkedIn Corp. have assisted with lessons.

Tech lags behind other industries in welcoming women back. In financial services, Goldman Sachs Group Inc., J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. and Morgan Stanley formed midcareer re-entry programs years ago. Consulting firm McKinsey & Co. contacts former female employees to see whether they are interested in returning.

By targeting this population, tech companies also are trying to fix a weakness in the industry: the under-representation of women. Tech companies have among the lowest participation of women of any sector, according to data from LeanIn.Org and McKinsey.

IBM's initiative to hire women returning to work began in 2012 as it developed new businesses including artificial intelligence, cloud-computing, security and data analytics. The company was facing a shortage of candidates amid competition from a newer generation of tech companies.

 We realized we had an enormous opportunity to tap the supply of female talent," says IBM human resources Vice President Lindsay-Rae McIntyre.

IBM created an alumni network to connect on social media with former employees who had stopped working. Dozens of women from the network have now rejoined IBM. That network helped Mary Kolbenschlag, a computer program manager, return to work a year after she left IBM in 2014 to care for her husband who was severely injured in a bicycle accident.

In the summer of 2015, one of her former IBM mentors offered her a role with a division that matched her prior work experience. She didn't know his outreach was part of the initiative to win back women who had left IBM.  If my mentor hadn't called me, I don't think I would have returned to IBM," Ms. Kolbenschlag says.

Japan Tech Hunts for Restart Button; There s hope that entrepreneurs can rekindle the economy but it can be a hard place to get a startup going

By Alexander Martin

20:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

TOKYO Stacks of cardboard boxes serve as makeshift partitions at Mistletoe Inc.'s new office in Tokyo's posh Aoyama district, where startups gather to work on their latest projects.

The do-it-yourself vibe a far cry from the stuffiness typical of Japanese corporate offices is something founder Taizo Son, serial entrepreneur and youngest brother of SoftBank Group Corp. founder Masayoshi Son, wants to see more of.

 Japan has the talent and funds but lacks the necessary ecosystem to create its own Silicon Valley, so that's what we're trying to provide," said Mr. Son, 43, who describes Mistletoe as a program to cofound new businesses.

The nation that created the Walkman and the bullet train before China even had a tech industry now lags behind as Chinese Internet startups like Alibaba Group Holding Ltd. become global powerhouses. With its "http://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10000872396390444840104577551972061864692" Japan is counting on entrepreneurs to rekindle its hobbling economy.

The government is pledging to fund startups, top universities have launched incubators and venture funds to transform their wealth of knowledge into innovation and even Japan's oldest and largest conglomerates, such as the Mitsubishi and Mitsui groups, are looking to nurture entrepreneurs.

Some startups have reached prominence in recent years. Euglena Co., a "http://blogs.wsj.com/speakeasy/2013/07/25/is-this-the-future-of-food/" that launched in 2005 and went public in 2012, has a market capitalization of more than $1 billion. Mercari Inc., operator of a peer-to-peer shopping app launched in 2013, raised around $75 million last month, valuing it at over $1 billion.

But venture-capital investments in Japan last year totaled just $629 million, according to AVCJ Research, compared with $59.1 billion in the U.S., according to the National Venture Capital Association.

There are still relatively few people willing to risk choosing entrepreneurship over a job at a big company. Even as the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/toshiba-expects-biggest-ever-yearly-loss-1454567056" and "http://www.wsj.com/articles/sharp-in-talks-over-panel-business-after-hefty-loss-1446205138" show there is no security in size, there remains a deeply ingrained reverence toward large corporations.

Take Yoshiyuki Taguchi, a 22-year-old intern at Slush Asia, an offshoot of a popular "http://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052702303289904579197803449054732" While he wanted to continue with Slush after graduation, his family insisted he join a  listed, famous" company. After arguing for a month, he took a job with a major human resources firm.

Antti Sonninen, chief executive of Slush Asia, said Japan needs more entrepreneurs-turned-investors to serve as role models.

 Those guys can give the best advice on how to build the next-generation, world-changing companies," he said.

That is what Mr. Son is attempting to do through Mistletoe, which supports startup-education programs and pitch events where entrepreneurs try to sell their ideas to investors while working with ventures to find solutions to problems in areas from transportation to Japan's aging population.

Mr. Son calls himself part of the first generation of Japanese entrepreneurs that launched startups during the dot-com boom of the late 1990s. Fundraising then was a nightmare, he says, with significantly less money available for entrepreneurs and banks reluctant to lend to unknown businesses.

A lot has changed since then. Today's entrepreneurs can point to the success of some of those unknown businesses the likes of e-commerce company Rakuten Inc. and mobile Internet operation DeNA Corp. have become household names in Japan, if not world-wide.

The number of companies going public reached an eight-year high last year at 98, around two-thirds of which listed on the Tokyo Stock Exchange's Mothers market for high-growth startups, according to the Japan Exchange Group.

Mr. Son's startup successes include Yahoo Japan, which he helped launch in 1996 while still a student at the University of Tokyo, and which now runs Japan's largest portal site, and Gungho Online Entertainment Inc., which produces top-grossing videogames like Puzzle & Dragons.

His mission now is to relay his decades of experience to a new generation of entrepreneurs. While his older brother has led telecommunications and Internet giant SoftBank in big, bold overseas bets including the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-profit-nearly-triples-1438842764" Corp. in 2013 and a recent "http://www.wsj.com/articles/softbanks-investment-in-india-could-exceed-10-billion-1452927757" and other parts of Asia the younger Mr. Son appears dedicated to energizing his local startup scene from the ground up.

Mistletoe's 1,300-square-meter  startup studio," as he calls it, offers participants workspace and the assistance of a staff that includes experts in fields ranging from artificial intelligence to industrial design.

 It can be hard to attract the necessary talent to launch a startup, so we work with them and provide advice," he said.

Tenants include Fove Inc., which is developing an eye-tracking virtual reality headset. Mistletoe also invests directly in about 20 startups, including Fomm Corp., which is making affordable compact electric vehicles that can move on water aimed at flood-prone markets like Thailand and Indonesia.

The next step would be a successful product release.

 There's a lot of support in Japan available for launching startups, but what's important is to establish structures for businesses to commercialize innovation," said Atsuko Nomura, senior researcher at the Japan Research Institute.  That's where large companies could come in, but we're not seeing enough of that."

With the advent of the "http://blogs.wsj.com/digits/tag/internet-of-things/" a network of devices connected to the Internet Mr. Son says there is big potential for Japan, a country that has traditionally excelled in producing hardware.

 Japan's large, established companies are still home to very powerful, innovative technologies, but they are risk-averse compared with startups that have nothing to lose," he said.  The way for Japan to develop its own unique ecosystem is to have them both coexist and cooperate, and we'd like to be that bridge."

INSIDE LONDON
Let's all dress up as our favourite bitcoin characters

By Paul Murphy 
709 words
9 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFTA
London Ed1
21
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

We had news this week that Circle Internet Financial, a US start-up, was hooking up with Barclays in the UK to allow users of its mobile app to transfer money between them. Circle will take a sterling amount from your bank account, turn it into bitcoin, send it to your nominated recipient and then turn it back into sterling.

This is the latest advance in the company's impressive mission to "change the world economy". Which begs the question: for the worse?

Logical thinkers might assume that the most efficient way to transfer sterling to someone else over the internet is to send them sterling direct using any existing service rather than faff about with a stupid crypto-currency that takes 1,000 times longer to clear through the payment system and burns holes in the ozone layer in the process.

Existing online payment transfer systems might be clunky, but do we need to co-opt a comic, energy-burning software platform promoted by techno-utopians who want to abolish central governments?

But that would be anti-bitcoin thinking - and an affront to some of the most intelligent people in the country, such as the government's chief scientific adviser. Sir Mark Walport told us this year that systems based on bitcoin's distributed public ledger, the blockchain, might well save the National Health Service and would certainly help with a host of other public service functions , from issuing passports to collecting taxes. Somehow.

A piece of bitcoin news we did not get this week was the real identity of the person known as Satoshi Nakamoto, the pseudonymous bitcoin inventor.

As Izabella Kaminska revealed on FT Alphaville , our financial blog, advisers purporting to work for Craig Wright, an Australian tech entrepreneur, who was named as the real Satoshi last year, have been trying to get London media organisations to sign up to embargoes ahead of a so-called Big Reveal in the UK, this week or next.

Mr Wright, it has been said, is about to perform a crypto-miracle, proving his credentials - which fuelled another wave of bitcoin debate online over whether or not he is the real deal.

Should we worry that all this wingnuttery is increasingly entering the mainstream? Perhaps it is just a financial version of "cosplay", a game enjoyed by many in which adults dress up as their favourite fictional characters. Cosplay apparently offers therapeutic help and a creative outlet to those working with dreams and memories.

And something similar is taking place with bitcoin. It allows financial minds to roam free in a techno-nirvana, slaying mighty beasts such as the Bank of England along the way.

Jeremy Allaire and Sean Neville, Circle's founders, present themselves as the financial revolutionaries of our day, declaring: "Let's change money together!"

But cosplay enthusiasts pay their way, at least bringing their costumes to the game, whereas the machinery underlying bitcoin and the energy burnt to maintain the network seem to be paid for by an endless supply of venture capital.

Meanwhile, there is the persistent idea that anything bitcoin-related somehow avoids a cost to the end consumer. Circle's services are free. It says there are no fees for sending money, even if you are turning pounds into dollars (via bitcoin) or the other way round. Can that really be the case? Are they farming users' data? Gouging through FX rates? Burning VC capital as they gun for market share? What?

It is an established fact in the modern world that if something is offered for free, the recipient is the product.

No matter. Here is what Circle sign-ups are told: "How exactly are we doing this? Unlike existing systems that are closed and proprietary, we use open internet standards and protocols, including the blockchain (allowing us to offer this service for free). We also foster innovative approaches to risk, including our AI risk engine, which runs on cutting-edge algorithms that continuously keep your money safe."

So, an artificial intelligence risk engine, running cutting-edge algorithms. That's OK then - so long as everyone involved is just playing fantasy finance.

paul.murphy@ft.com
