NATIONAL NEWS
Banks tap into the needs of millennials; Going mobile: App generation

By Emma Dunkley 
791 words
9 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
03
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Lenders use selfies and thumbprints as battle for younger customers heats up

On the bus to work at 7.30am, most commuters are half asleep, but 26-year-old Henrietta Hunter is doing her banking over her mobile phone.

As messages from friends come through, she can transfer cash to them with a couple of taps on her banking app. "I do not want to waste time queueing at a branch on my weekend," says the production manager from Stockwell, south London.

She is not alone. Accenture, the consultancy, polled 3,000 consumers and found 41 per cent of 18- to 24-year-olds now check their balance on their phones. "In 10 years' time banking will be unrecognisable," says Warren Mead at KPMG, another consultancy. The lines between banking and the digital world are blurring and will soon disappear altogether, he predicts. Banks are under pressure to cater for their younger customers by developing their own mobile services and face fierce competition from a range of technology companies, from nimble start-ups to giants such as PayPal.

A survey last month of 200 senior bank executives by technology company Temenos showed 57 per cent believed more money would flow via digital businesses than traditional retail banks by 2020. This week saw the launch of the UK's first digital-only lender , Atom Bank, which has no branches and boasts customers can log into their accounts by taking a "selfie".

"Look how quickly a 20-year-old takes a selfie of themselves - there is a familiarity with apps that thrive on the quick capture of information, images in particular," says Edward Twiddy, the bank's chief operations officer.

Tandem Bank, another digital start-up that has won a banking licence, promises to be mobile-based and is trying to build a community of "co-founders" who pitch ideas about how to improve banking. Ricky Knox, at Tandem, says there are "massive differences in behaviour" in the millennial generation because it has grown up with smartphones.

A survey by Tandem of 2,000 people aged between 26 and 36 showed that nearly a third would switch banks simply on the strength of a mobile app.

Payment methods are also changing. Some pub chains are now allowing customers to buy their drinks by taking a thumbprint on a mobile app powered by MasterCard. More than 3m customers are also now using Pingit, an app by Barclays that lets people "ping" money to a mobile phone number.

Maceo Frederick, a 25-year-old trainee accountant, says he rarely uses cash machines, never visits branches, and sends money through Pingit. He adds he would switch banks to whoever had the best digital service.

Banks are experimenting with voice technology and artificial intelligence.

Earlier this month Santander UK created a voice-based service , similar to iPhone's Siri, that allows customers to command their phone to tell them their payments history. The bank will soon allow people to command their phone to make a payment.

Royal Bank of Scotland is using an artificial intelligence program, called Luvo, to talk to customers with "personality" and help them with queries.

Branches still have a role to play, even though they are in decline. Research by consultancy firm CACI forecasts that the number of branch visits by current account customers is expected to fall to 268m by 2020, while mobile app usage is on track to more than double to 2.3bn.

For Rosie Shimell, 27, a political researcher from Dulwich, branches are still a vital part of banking services. "I like that my bank has high street branches, mainly because if you have a problem, it can be easier to pop in and speak to someone rather than wait to get through to a call-centre." She also puts more faith in traditional lenders.

Mr Frederick says: "I would rather stick to the high street banks than a new digital bank or technology company because I'm already comfortable with them, I have no problems and if I moved, I would not know what to ex-pect, I would be starting from scratch."

Financial Times
FTFT

Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A young basketball executive who tried to apply the number-crunching techniques of Wall Street to the game but fell flat on his face has delivered a rambling resignation letter of more than 7,000 words seen as one of the epic executive parting shots of all time.

Sam Hinkie, a 38-year-old product of Stanford business school, had been hired in 2013 as general manager of the Philadelphia 76ers basketball team by owners including leading private equity executives as Blackstone's David Blitzer and Apollo's Josh Harris as well as Will Smith, the Philadelphia rapper turned Hollywood actor.

Mr Blitzer and Mr Harris have fared little better in the English Premier League. After spending 100m for 36 per cent of Crystal Palace when the football club was sixth in the table last December, the club has since recorded just one win.

The Philadelphia 76ers had hoped Mr Hinkie would be another Billy Beane, the analytics-minded baseball executive immortalised in the Michael Lewis book Moneyball for unearthing undervalued players by analysing performance metrics. However, the 76ers are on track for one of the worst seasons in National Basketball Association history.

Mr Hinkie reflected on their dire 10-68 result in a resignation letter this week that runs more than five times the length of the 1776 Declaration of Independence and includes quotes from Abraham Lincoln and Warren Buffett.

His farewell has already become one of the most intensely parsed resignation letters since Greg Smith's 2012 dispatch for The New York Times titled "Why I Am Leaving Goldman Sachs". Modelled on the letters sent to investors by fund managers and market sages, Mr Hinkie's missive to "the equity partners of Philadelphia 76ers, LP" compared his decision to step down to that of a 38-year-old Mr Buffett to shut down his investment fund in May 1969.

"It pains me that . . . I would go at the end of a particularly down year in the standings," he wrote. "But the fact is - and a young Buffett said it much better than I ever could - 'I am not attuned to this environment, and I don't want to spoil a decent record by trying to play a game I don't understand just so I can go out a hero.' "

The blogosphere responded with derision. One scribe on the sports site Deadspin said that the letter was "a giant blinking warning sign to any future employer".

Pro Private Markets
Autonomous Delivery Vehicle Company Dispatch Drives $2M in Seed Funding

By Cat Zakrzewski 
482 words
7 April 2016
13:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Dispatch wants its autonomous vehicles to make on-demand delivery available to the masses.

The company announced on Wednesday it has grabbed $2 million in seed financing led by Andreessen Horowitz. This is the second investment Andreessen Horowitz partner Chris Dixon made this week in autonomous vehicles, following the investment in Comma.ai he announced Sunday.

 I'm really excited about autonomous vehicles in general," Mr. Dixon said.  Machine learning and deep learning is only recently at the point where you can really make a high-quality, highly autonomous, safe vehicle. It's going to open up all sorts of vehicles."

Dispatch develops a vehicle it nicknamed  Carry" to travel on sidewalks and pedestrian areas. The robot, which looks like a large cooler on wheels, can carry up to 100 pounds and travels at the same speed humans walk. It has four compartments that users unlock with their smartphones when it arrives.

Users can track Carry as it travels to them, and it is programmed to move at the speed people around it are walking. The Dispatch team says that Carry has never bumped into anyone, and even if it did, it would be negligible due to the vehicle's slow speeds.

The founders, Stav Braun, Sonia Jin and Uriah Baalke, are former computer vision researchers from Massachusetts Institute of Technology and University of Pennsylvania.

The Dispatch founders said they hope the vehicles will mitigate the cost of delivery because it can save labor costs by bringing packages right to a customer's door. It does face competition in the sector from Starship Technologies, a  ground drone," which two Skype co-founders created.

Right now, Dispatch is still being tested. The company has two Carry vehicles that are making deliveries on the California State University Monterey Bay campus and the Menlo College campus.

Dispatch's funding comes as autonomous vehicles are in the spotlight. General Motors Co. purchased Cruise Automation Inc., a startup that developed a system for applying autonomous-driving software to existing vehicles. The Wall Street Journal reported the deal is valued at more than $1 billion.

Larger companies such as Alphabet Inc. and Uber Technologies Inc. are heavily investing in autonomous automobiles. The Dispatch team thinks its focus on delivering items will separate it from competitors focused on transporting humans.

 I don't think the big tech companies are working on sidewalk drones," Mr. Dixon said.

Mr. Dixon thinks even as large public tech companies take a capital intensive approach to autonomous vehicles, there's room for startups. He compared startups like Dispatch to the navigation app Waze, which managed to collect better data about traffic than Google by involving regular users.

 As you collect more data, the systems get smarter," Mr. Dixon said.  A smarter startup has a shot."

Virtual agents and chat bots may be the next thing in software, but getting an artificial intelligence product into the market can be an expensive and technically challenging undertaking.

After two years of development by dozens of data scientists and software engineers, New York startup X.ai Inc. says that 100% of the responses of its scheduling assistant are machine-generated. Humans are still involved in teaching the algorithms to understand what users are asking, said Dennis Mortensen, the startup's co-founder and chief executive.

The goal is to get X.ai's assistant, named Amy or Andrew, to run entirely on its own. Then the company could aim for big clients such as banks.

To get there, the pre-revenue startup has raised a $23 million Series B round, Mr. Mortensen told VentureWire. Two Sigma Ventures, the venture arm of hedge fund Two Sigma Investments, which uses data science approaches in investing, led the round.

The deal brings X.ai's funding to $34.5 million. Its post-money valuation is about $100 million.

The New York startup says its productivity tool could free up humans from the email ping pong around setting up meetings. Without any downloads, users copy X.ai's virtual assistant on an email they send to contacts with whom they'd like to meet. The AI taps the customer's calendar to find the best time and place.

X.ai's investors are making a bet that businesses with office workers would be willing to trust AI with their calendars, as well as with maintaining polite conversation on their behalf with clients, bosses and colleagues.

 This whole round was scheduled on Amy," Mr. Mortensen said, adding that the AI was sensitive to the power dynamics of a CEO fundraising from investors.

DCM Ventures and Work-Bench Ventures are new investors, joining returning backers IA Ventures, FirstMark Capital, SoftBank Capital - SBNY, Lerer Hippeau Ventures, Crunchfund, and Pritzker Group Venture Capital.

 This will get us to cash-flow positive," Mr. Mortensen said, adding that X.ai plans to start charging businesses in September. Close to 30,000 people signed up for X.ai's waiting list for its beta version in February.

Text- and voice-based bots are a natural evolution in software, according to Julie Ask, principal analyst at Forrester Research Inc., pointing to such products as Amazon Inc.'s Echo.

 As a consumer I don't want to hop in and out of different apps," Ms. Ask said. But bot adoption, Ms. Ask said,  is not going to happen as fast as everybody wants."

Other virtual scheduling assistants come from Clara Labs, Julie Desk and Zirtual, each using automation to various degrees. Zirtual ran out of funding and was acquired by Startup.co last year.

MARKETS - BACK SECTION
LSE combination spawns derivatives doubts; Analysis: Capital markets

By Philip Stafford 
686 words
6 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
22
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Deutsche Brse deal will bring post-trade changes, but the outlook for fees is unclear

Deutsche Brse's $20bn combination with London Stock Exchange promises an "industry-defining combination" but there are doubts that traders and investors relying on derivatives in a post-crisis world will see the benefits.

The deal will bring together London, Europe's leading financial hub, and Frankfurt, home of the European Central Bank, into a behemoth able to trade in equities and futures, benchmarks, index and data analytics.

But it is in the world of risk management, or post-trade as it is known, where the new exchange promises the biggest changes. A combination offers clearing of futures and swaps and collateral management on a scale that no exchange operator in the world can match.

For some it is an indication of how global markets are changing rapidly, overturned by the impact of capital rules on banks and the growth of exchange traded funds and other forms of passive investing.

Anthony Perrotta, a former broker and head of research at Tabb, the US capital markets consultancy, says capital rules are the "single biggest issue" affecting over-the-counter markets.

Bourses are in an arms race "establishing true verticals - pre-trade information flow, execution, clearing, and settlement - in the market", he says.

"I think exchanges are recognising the need for expanding their roles as intermediaries by utilising scale and services to connect asset owners."

The deal is expected to improve the efficiencies of the exchanges to the tune of at least  ¬450m over three years. But how much will it benefit markets?

In an interview last week Xavier Rolet , chief executive of the LSE, said a combined group would be a model for the future as it sought to provide services for customers that trade globally, such as Fidelity, BlackRock and Pimco. The combined settlement assets would offer collateral management immediately after the market close, he added.

"Would they accept their business siloing - you know, that you have to deal with these guys here, with those guys there? There's hundreds of billions of dollars or pounds or euros of margin that is posted and a whole bunch of silos around the world - inefficiently."

Among participants in futures markets there are concerns that a deal will lead to higher trading fees in an already costly industry. It is a charge to which the LSE is sensitive.

"Our American futures friends have kept on increasing fees every year - in 2009, 2010, when everybody was on their back bleeding to death," said Mr Rolet. "Total cost of trading is too high, so it's going to change."

But clearing is a mandatory part of derivatives trading and will always require costly margin to offset risk. That has led others to question whether the exchanges can really promise more savings for traders.

"Regulatory capital isn't margin," says one executive at a high-speed trading company.

Few banks and fund managers are willing to go on the record backing the combination.

"It might make European ETF listings a bit easier, but overall, for us, it's largely a non-event," says one fund management executive.

The new exchange promises banks and investors savings of billions from the related costs of trading in OTC markets as new Basel regulations kick in. New rules force banks trading in derivatives to post large amounts of collateral and margin at central clearing houses as insurance to back their deals. The main customers, the investment banks, are also being hit with heavy capital charges on those deals.

Netting the margin that traders post to back both futures and swaps derivatives - called cross-margining - is a more efficient use of capital, the exchanges argue.

Michael Spencer , chief executive of interdealer broker ICAP, is a fan of the deal, which he says "offers the best margin-netting opportunities".

Deutsche Brse's dominant position in longer-dated bond contracts cleared on Eurex's futures markets can be matched against LCH's dominant position in euro rate clearing, he says.

COMPANIES
JPMorgan uses online forums to hire talent; Banks

By Laura Noonan, Investment Banking Correspondent 
489 words
6 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
17
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Financial groups become creative in their quest to attract the right personnel

Investment banks are turning to "talent communities" and machine learning as they become increasingly creative in their quest to attract the right personnel to an industry that has lost some of its lustre since the financial crisis.

JPMorgan, the world's biggest investment bank, is creating online talent forums of prospective employees in an attempt to attract tech workers, Jim Cochran , the bank's head of global recruiting, told the Financial Times.

Goldman Sachs is using machine learning to screen the hundreds of thousands of applications it still gets each year for those that meet the bank's basic suitability thresholds, an executive said.

Top MBA graduates are now 40 per cent less likely to pick banking as a career than they were in 2008, while banks are also struggling to lure the tech-savvy experts they need to future proof their businesses away from more attractive Silicon Valley companies.

Mr Cochran said JPMorgan is using online talent forums to show a select group of in-demand professionals "why it's just as interesting to work for a financial services firm" as it is for a tech company so that they can get people "over the hump" about making the switch between industries. The portal is similar to closed groups on networking website LinkedIn, but JPMorgan is in talks with "front-end providers" who will sell it a special interface for the communities.

The members of the talent communities include people who have previously had interviews with the bank and decided not to join, as well as other industry experts. The material on the forum includes information about the work the bank is doing, as well as personal messages from senior managers on what it is like to work there and discussions around industry topics.

"We're creating a pipeline, a community around a topic, so that recruiters can pick [from the community]," Mr Cochran said.

An engineer who spent more than a decade at a top tier bank before moving to a large technology company said it would take more than talent communities to convince people like him to move to financial services. Banks treat engineering as a "cost centre", whereas technology companies had engineering at the heart of their business, he said.

Mr Cochran insisted the issue was not a shortage of CVs coming into his bank, which gets 40-50 applications for every job it advertises. "There are a lot of rsums coming in, we want the people that are right for JPMorgan," he said.

Goldman is also dealing with an overflowing inbox, attracting 313,000 applications globally last year for 9,700 positions. Despite the financial crash, top-tier banks such as Goldman continue to attract a large number of applicants who see banking as a lucrative career.

MARKETS & INVESTING
Freelance quant fund beats S&P 500; Equities

By Robin Wigglesworth, US Markets Editor 
512 words
6 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
Asia Ed1
20
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Quantopian put $100,000 monthly kitty at disposal of winning strategies

A "crowdsourced" hedge fund that uses the trading algorithms of a handful of freelance programmers notched up a 1.93 per cent net gain in its first full quarter - more than doubling the gain of the US stock market.

The platform, which is run by Quantopian, based in Boston, allows programmers, mathematicians and data scientists to design and test stock trading algorithms.

Every month whoever comes up with the best-performing strategy is given access to a $100,000 trading kitty. The winner is allowed to keep any profit over the next six months.

Last year the number of registered "quants", or quantitative traders, swelled to nearly 60,000 when Quantopian started an internal hedge fund and seeded it with capital. The fund uses the best algorithms and gives 10 per cent of the proceeds to their authors.

Quantitative investing - the use of complex mathematical models, supercomputers and sometimes artificial intelligence to identify patterns in the swelling sea of data - has been around for several decades.

But it is increasingly popular thanks to the enviable returns achieved by heavyweights such as Renaissance Technologies, DE Shaw and Two Sigma.

Amid a shortage of talent - many computer scientists prefer to work in Silicon Valley - hedge funds have offered competitions and established alliances with universities.

Quantopian gives its members access to data and coding tools so they can trade as a sideline or from home. The returns are net of fees paid to the authors, an assumed 2 per cent management fee and the Quantopian's 10 per cent performance fee - mimicking the "two and 20" hedge fund fee structure.

The S&P 500 rose 0.8 per cent during the first quarter.

John Fawcett , Quantopian's founder and chief executive, said it was important to "stay sober" regarding the early, unaudited results on trading the initial $500,000 of capital, but said the initial results were encouraging. "The number of algos and the size need to be ramped up in the coming months to achieve our objectives," he said.

The plan was to open the fund to outside investors once its record had been established, Mr Fawcett added.

Quantopian's initial foray used strategies for the US stock market designed by eight members, including a mechanical engineer in Australia and a US software developer at an internet search company. The intention is to use 20 to 30 algorithms at a time eventually.

This year it plans to increase the fund's firepower to $1m of capital and to enhance its leverage to three times capital from close to one times in its first quarter. In time it foresees leverage of six times capital.

Quantopian expects to have more than 120,000 people on its platform by the end of the year.

While enthusiasm over " DIY quants " is growing, sceptics warn that freelance algo traders will over time not do much better than day traders.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
'There are going to be lots of dead unicorns'; THE BIG PICTURE

By Attracta Mooney 
1,567 words
4 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
06
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Many fund managers have had to write down their investments in billion-dollar start-ups, says Attracta Mooney

Unicorns, as billion-dollar start-ups have been dubbed, were one of the hottest topics of 2015.

Asset managers, including Fidelity Management and Research, BlackRock and T Rowe Price, piled into private start-ups last year to take advantage of the boom in technology companies.

But one year on, most fund houses appear to have taken a vow of silence when it comes to their investments in the billion-dollar fledglings.

Few of the 25 or more asset managers and venture capitalists contacted would talk on the record about unicorns. They were even less happy to discuss the valuations of the start-ups they flocked to last year.

Many fund managers were forced to write down their investments in start-ups in the final three months of 2015, including those in Dropbox, the file storage website, Snapchat, the disappearing-photo app, and MongoDB, a database.

The writedowns have raised big questions about the soaring valuations of many young tech companies and whether asset managers rushed into an area where they had little understanding of the true value of what they were investing in.

There are also fears that a tech bubble is emerging and that large-scale failures are likely, leading to so-called unicorpses - unicorns that die.

"There are going to be lots of dead unicorns," said Marc Benioff, founder and chief executive of Salesforce, the cloud computing company, at the World Economic Forum in Davos earlier this year.

Regulators are also worried. Last week, Mary Jo White, the chairwoman of the Securities and Exchange Commission, said the US financial watchdog was concerned about the "eye-popping" valuations of some unlisted start-ups and the consequences for investors of the market overheating.

In this climate, technology start-ups are finding it much more difficult to raise cash. During the final quarter of 2015, so-called crossover investors from public markets, the likes of mutual funds and hedge funds, were involved in 168 deals worth $8.1bn, compared with 223 deals worth $13.5bn during the third quarter, according to CB Insights, a research company.

Overall, however, crossover investments in private technology companies rose 51 per cent last year, to more than $40.9bn across 800 deals, CB Insights data show.

Mutual funds had previously avoided unlisted companies, but in recent years many technology companies decided to delay an initial public offering , instead raising money through so-called late-stage financing rounds. This move is forcing asset managers that want access to the fast-growing tech sector to consider private investments.

Adley Bowden, vice-president of PitchBook, a database of venture capital and merger and acquisition deals, says: "Asset managers have historically invested in unicorns. The key difference now is that these companies would have already [had an IPO] and been publicly traded. But, without a public offering for asset managers to invest in, they have started investing more while the companies are still private."

This has helped drive up the number of unicorns from 39 to 156 globally, with a cumulative valuation of $550bn, according to CB Insights. But 58 tech start-ups suffered "down rounds" since the start of 2015, a situation in which company stock is deemed to be worth less than initially thought.

Some unicorns are suffering large valuation writedowns when they publicly list for the first time, as was the case with Square, which handles credit card payments. Private fundraising in 2014 valued Square at roughly $6bn. Its IPO last year priced it at $2.9bn.

Asset managers would not comment on whether they expected further writedowns of valuations. Instead, they said company valuations were decided by internal committees that operate independently of portfolio managers.

But many have already written down investments in tech start-ups, including BlackRock, which cut its valuation of Dropbox by more than 20 per cent last October.

T Rowe Price also wrote down its investment in Dropbox, as well as several other companies, including Cloudera, a data analytics company, and Evernote, the workplace app that announced staff cuts and office closures last autumn.

Fidelity has additionally revalued Snapchat and MongoDB. Other asset managers are taking similar approaches.

Mr Bowden played down the markdowns to private companies' valuations, suggesting it was "not as big of a deal as the press has played it up to be".

He says: "Mutual funds are used to marking their investments up and down daily with the public markets, and very much view the changes in valuation as something totally natural. It is important to note that these investments are big to the venture world but in most cases a very small drop in the fund managers' overall [assets under management]."

Some of the world's best-known technology experts and investors have urged caution when it comes to unicorns. Bill Gates , the co-founder of Microsoft, the technology company, recently warned that investors need to be more discriminating when investing in technology start-ups.

But even when asset managers try to avoid unicorns, they often end up inadvertently invested in them through the public companies they hold. In some cases, public companies have suffered because of their exposure to unicorns, as happened with PLDT, the listed Filipino telecommunications company.

It was forced to write down an investment in Rocket Internet, the German start-up incubator that invests in unicorns, at the end of 2015, leaving the business reporting its first net loss in 12 years.

Edward Lam, a partner at Somerset Capital Management, says: "The writedowns [of unicorns in public companies] are a concern. Some of these investments may end up coming good but there has been a slightly speculative element to some of them."

Most asset managers contacted for this article would give little away about whether or not they expect to increase or decrease their allocation to unicorns this year.

A spokesperson from BlackRock, which PitchBook estimates has invested in five unicorns, says the fund house invests in privately held companies from "time to time". T Rowe says it is "extremely selective when it comes to investing in these companies".

Neither investment manager provided any details of whether it saw any opportunities in start-up technology companies currently.

Andy Boyd, head of global equity capital markets at Fidelity Management and Research, says the company, which ranks as one of the largest 15 investors in unicorns globally, plans to continue to invest in private start-ups.

"We look for opportunities in all sectors and across many geographies and have been successful in finding opportunities this year, although at a slower pace than in previous years," he says.

Mr Boyd says there is a strong investment case for putting money into private companies. "We have been investing in private companies for over a decade and we feel that in order to understand the competitive position of any company, one should look at all the players in that space, both public and private," he says.

He also downplayed fears about unicorpses. "Any investment comes with risks," he says.

Mark Hawtin, investment director at GAM, the Swiss asset manager, adds: "It is very important to look at the topic of unicorns company by company. There is no black-and-white view. Some unicorns look like great investments and some do not, but it is case by case."

Mr Bowden says mutual fund managers will continue to invest in unicorns, not least because many have a mandate that requires them to invest in high-growth companies.

"We believe this shift in asset managers investing in companies while still private is here to stay, as long as these businesses continue to push out their IPOs," he says.

He adds: "Asset managers won't go away."

Soaring valuations

Year of the unicorn to year of the unicorpse

The word unicorn might be most associated with the mythical creature sporting a large spiral horn, but since 2013 the phrase has developed a different meaning.

Aileen Lee, founder of Cowboy Ventures, a venture capital firm, first used the word in late 2013 to describe private technology companies with a valuation of at least $1bn. Her point was that billion-dollar start-ups were extremely rare, almost mythical.

"It's really hard, and highly unlikely, to build or invest in a billion-dollar company," she wrote in a post on TechCrunch, the website. "The odds are somewhere between catching a foul ball at [a major-league baseball] game and being struck by lightning in one's lifetime."

At the time, she estimated there were just 39 unicorns, including companies such as Airbnb, the accommodation rental website, and Uber, the taxi-hailing app.

But the number of unicorns has increased significantly since then. CB Insights, the research company, estimates there are 155 unicorns globally now.

These include Pinterest, the online scrapbook site valued at $11bn; China's Lufax, an online finance marketplace; Spotify, the Swedish music-streaming service valued at $8.5bn, and HelloFresh, the German ecommerce business that delivers fresh food to consumers.

As the number of billion-dollar tech companies soared, 2015 was branded the year of the unicorn. This year, however, could be the year of the unicorpse - or dead unicorns - amid fears about valuations and the sustainability of business models.

Pro Private Markets
Hedge Fund Analysts Use Deep Learning  to Diagnose Heart s Condition

By Cat Zakrzewski 
470 words
4 April 2016
14:26
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Two quantitative analysts using artificial intelligence in an online data science competition showed they could diagnose heart disease about as accurately as doctors.

Qi Liu and Tencia Lee, hedge fund analysts and self-described  quants," built the winning algorithm in the competition, which could find indicators of heart disease. The online data contest challenged participants to develop machine algorithms that could measure cardiac volumes from MRIs provided by the National Heart, Lung and Blood Institute.

Mr. Liu and Ms. Lee didn't know each other before they won the competition, beating out more than 1,390 algorithms. They met each other in a forum on the Kaggle site, where the competition was hosted over a three-month period.

 We decided to combine our methods," Ms. Lee said.  We decided they were different enough from each other that we could do better than either of us would alone."

Competitors worked on algorithms that could accomplish a manual and slow process that normally is carried out by cardiologists. Usually it takes doctors about 20 minutes to measure cardiac volumes and derive ejection fraction data from an MRI. The algorithms can analyze the images much more quickly.

The data scientists had a set of more than 1,000 MRIs to work with. Ms. Lee said the winning algorithm used a technique called deep learning.

The National Heart, Lung and Blood Institute will now test the algorithm in clinical environments. Ms. Lee said she hopes one day the algorithm can be used by health care professionals, but she said there is a long road of testing and regulatory processes before it gets there.

 I certainly wouldn't trust my doctor with what we just wrote," Ms. Lee said.

Many companies are trying to use artificial intelligence to improve medicine. Investors are increasingly backing startups in the category, and public technology companies have signaled it is a sector they're betting on. Last year International Business Machines Corp. acquired Merge Healthcare Inc. for $1 billion, according to The Wall Street Journal.

The contest, the National Data Science Bowl, is sponsored by the data science startup Kaggle and Booz Allen Hamilton.

This is the second time Kaggle has hosted a National Science Data Bowl. The first competition challenged scientists to come up with algorithms that could measure plankton population to predict global health.

Kaggle Chief Executive Anthony Goldbloom said the winner of the first National Data Bowl now works as a research scientist at Google's DeepMind. He said Kaggle competitions, which crowd source solutions to big data problems, have become a way for data scientists to quantify their skills when applying for jobs.

Mr. Goldbloom said the company is now seeking proposals for the third competition's challenge.

(The following is a roundup of selected companies that have revealed securities sales in recent Form D regulatory filings. These companies are required to file a Form D notification within 15 days after the first sale of securities in an offering, which could indicate fundraising, acquisitions or other securities activity. The filings don't list the recipients of the securities being offered. However, they do list board members, so in cases where new investors have surfaced, we'll report them below.)

Direct Flow Medical Inc., Santa Rosa, Calif., developer of a method to treat heart-valve conditions through a catheter-based procedure instead of surgery, has sold $15.1 million of a $21.5 million round of Series A-1 equity. In 2014, the company received $32 million in equity and debt from investors including PDL BioPharma Inc. and SV Life Sciences. Direct Flow is also backed by EDF Ventures, ePlanet Venture Partners, Flare Capital Partners, New Leaf Venture Partners, Spray Venture Partners and VantagePoint Venture Partners.

Neat Company Inc., Philadelphia, which manages receipts and documents for businesses or individuals, has issued slightly more than half of a $2.1 million round of equity and rights. In 2010, the company raised a $2.75 million round from Edison Partners and MentorTech Ventures.

Notion Data Inc., Portland, Ore., a software-as-a-service analytics provider operating as Notion, has opened a $3 million round of equity. Portland Seed Fund counts the company in its portfolio.

Virtual Power Systems Inc., Santa Clara, Calif., a data center company, has sold $4.2 million of a $10.4 million equity round, and has issued $1.6 million of a $2.1 million round of equity and debt, according to two separate filings. Data Collective, Dolby Family Ventures and Exfinity Venture Partners are investors in the company, according to its website.

THE LONG VIEW
Investors find it hard to know what April Fool's data to believe

By John Authers 
871 words
2 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
18
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Yesterday was a Big Data day if ever there was one. Investors had to sift through the usual raft of April Fool spoofs, and supply manager surveys from around the world, but also - unusually - had the monthly game of the heavily revision-prone US employment data as well.

Data matters more than usual because the Federal Reserve, the US central bank, depends on it, and because there is heightened uncertainty. The quarter just finished featured a sharp sell-off of risk assets, fuelled by fears the US would lapse into recession, and an equally swift recovery. People do not know what to believe.

In the event, the April Fool's data gave neither the Fed nor anyone else much reason to change how they proceed from here. The US unemployment rate ticked up slightly, but only because the numbers in the labour force (and looking for work), also ticked up very slightly. The pattern remains solidly unchanged; employment is improving at the same steady pace at which it has been improving for several years, which is not yet quite fast enough either to make US citizens much happier, or to give them much negotiating power to press their wages upwards.

The fact that the "participation rate" - the proportion of the total adult workforce deemed actively seeking work - has risen for six months in a row after cratering could be seen as a sign that inflation pressures are ready to take over, but not a great one.

In line with this, the University of Michigan's consumer confidence indicator, a much-followed survey, also ticked up slightly, but remains well below the level it hit last year.

The significant change is in the ISM manufacturing survey, based on supply managers' opinions and carried out in all the leading economies. This suggests that the manufacturing sectors of China and the US, the world's two largest economies have returned to health, and are (slightly) expanding again. For many months, they had pointed to recession, a main cause for markets' alarm.

This is probably a direct offshoot of the weaker dollar, to which China's currency is tied. The dollar has weakened 4.1 per cent this year against its biggest trading partners. This may be the result of deliberate decisions by central banks, even though it is not great for the eurozone, which has seen the euro rebound 4.9 per cent this year against the dollar this year. Its manufacturing numbers have weakened a little in consequence.

The weaker dollar also relieved pressure on some emerging markets that had heavy debt denominated in dollars. Emerging markets stocks are 5.5 per cent up from the beginning of the year.

As for other market measures, most described a V-shape, and ended up where they started. US stocks have barely changed. Neither has oil. Crude oil, as measured by the West Texas Intermediate contract, is at this moment very slightly cheaper than it was at the beginning of the year.

Two other changes are significant. First, expectations for profit growth have collapsed. According to Thomson Reuters, forecasts for US profit growth in the first quarter have moved from 2.3 to minus 6.9 per cent this year, while forecasts for the full year have dropped from 7.6 to 2.0 per cent.

And one critical market indicator had a quarter for the ages. Gold, which rose more than 16 per cent in dollar terms, remarkably enjoyed its best quarter since 1986. Gold has been falling very steadily for a long time and is due for a rebound. Some of this is a reaction to the weaker dollar. But still, a move that sharp in gold when other assets moved little, and where the economic situation apparently changed little, has to be treated as an indicator of something.

But what? Taking all the evidence together, it is fair to suggest that the crisis of confidence in central banks' ability to move markets and the economy, widely discussed at the market nadir in February, remains intact. Stocks are unchanged for the year, and below their highs, even though long-term interest rates have dipped significantly, with the 10-year Treasury yield falling from 2.27 to 1.77 per cent this year.

So this is what the data tells us about the year so far and where we stand now. Central banks have succeeded in weakening the dollar, and arresting an incipient recession in manufacturing that greatly threatened the emerging world, at the cost of a fall in their credibility.

The lack of confidence shows itself in collapsing profits forecasts, and in rising gold. Meanwhile, the oil price, which has led other risk markets for almost two years now, has ticked down in the last week. A weaker dollar and the prospect of easier money would usually drive oil prices up, not down, so this is a development to watch closely.

So data-dependency will continue, but the data to watch will change. Over the next few weeks watch the price of oil and earnings numbers from US groups.

john.authers@ft.com

COMPANIES
MetLife ruling shakes US post-crisis laws; Financials: Dodd-Frank challenge

By Barney Jopson in Washington and Alistair Gray in New York 
819 words
1 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFTA
USA Ed1
15
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Court's revocation of insurer's 'systemically important' tag deals big blow to Washington

US finance chiefs often grumble about the tough rules imposed by the Dodd-Frank reforms that followed the 2008 crisis. But most went along with the various strictures, wary of fuelling another bout of anti-Wall Street hostility.

Not Steven Kandarian , chief executive of the insurer MetLife. He argued that the Obama administration had gone too far when it identified his group as a potential threat to the financial system that needed extra supervision.

A federal judge sent ripples through Washington and New York this week by ruling that Mr Kandarian was right and revoking the insurer's designation.

The decision has shaken a pillar of post-crisis regulation, raising questions about how the US handles "too big to fail" institutions and whether other groups will follow MetLife and try to throw off the government's yoke.

GE Capital , which has chafed at its "systemically important financial institution" - or Sifi - tag, filed an official request yesterday to be struck off the government's list, having downsized drastically.

The judge's decision is putting pressure on AIG , whose near-collapse in 2008 fuelled the drive to identify and rein in systemic institutions, to say whether it will try to escape the scrutiny that goes with being on the Sifi list.

Peter Hancock , the insurer's chief executive, told CNBC yesterday: "It certainly opens that opportunity, but I think it's something we want to reserve judgment [on]."

The full impact of US District Judge Rosemary Collyer's ruling is not yet clear. The US Treasury is widely expected to appeal to the higher courts, although so far it has said only that it will "continue to defend [the] designation process vigorously".

Also the reasoning behind her ruling has not been revealed. Ironically for a case in which MetLife accused regulators of lacking transparency, the two parties asked for the details of the judgment to be confidential. MetLife and the Treasury are scheduled to decide with the judge next week how much of the ruling will be released.

"It's intolerable that a case that is so important to the financial stability of the country is almost entirely secret," said Dennis Kelleher of Better Markets, a group that lobbies for tougher financial regulation.

MetLife argued, and Judge Collyer appears to have agreed, that the Financial Stability Oversight Council , a group of regulators that includes representatives of the Treasury and the Federal Reserve , had mishandled the Sifi decision. The council had made unsubstantiated assumptions, failed to take into account the costs of Sifi designation on the group and reached an "arbitrary and capricious" decision, the insurer said.

"It's a profound embarrassment to the FSOC," said Karen Shaw Petrou, managing partner at Federal Financial Analytics , a Washington-based consultancy.

Analysts at UBS said the ruling "seems to validate management's decision to pursue this lawsuit, which we had previously questioned".

Several lawyers said they would need to see the court's full opinion before being able to assess Washington's chances of success in a higher court.

Not surprisingly some of the staunchest Republican opponents of Dodd-Frank in Congress were quick to praise the ruling. "FSOC's perfect storm of secrecy and intimidation has created a shadow regulatory system that concentrates power in Washington at the expense of hardworking Americans," said Scott Garrett.

"I'm pleased to see the judicial branch took a stand for the Constitution."

The other big insurer on the "too big to fail" list is Prudential Financial . Asked about its next move, it said: "We continuously review developments that impact our company, and we are evaluating what is in the best interests of the company and our shareholders."

Banks will be far less affected by the MetLife decision, because the Sifi rules are clearer for them. Dodd-Frank specifically states that any bank with assets of $50bn or more will be considered systemically important.

That cut-off has been hugely unpopular with some midsized institutions that were captured by the designation. Their only hope of escape is a change in the law, and enduring partisan acrimony in Congress makes that unlikely.

So far large asset managers such as Fidelity and BlackRock have got off relatively lightly in the systemic debate. They headed off an early move by regulators to designate specific companies as systemic threats.

Hillary Clinton, the Democratic presidential candidate, has consistently highlighted the stability risks posed by non-banks. Her campaign was quick to criticise the MetLife ruling.

"When Treasury , the SEC, the Fed and other top financial regulators all agree an institution poses systemic risks to our economy, it's deeply concerning when a lower court judge unilaterally rejects their decision," a Clinton spokesman said.

COMPANIES
Fidelity cuts valuations of its Silicon Valley start-ups; Financials

By Leslie Hook in San Francisco 
373 words
1 April 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
14
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Fidelity Investments has cut the valuations on many of the start-ups in which it owns shares, underscoring the adjustment under way in Silicon Valley as investors become more sceptical.

Among Fidelity's heaviest writedowns were enterprise software companies Cloudera and Dropbox, which had the value of their shares cut by 38 per cent and 20 per cent respectively from the end of January to the end of February.

The writedowns also reflect declines in technology shares more broadly, as companies such as Twitter and LinkedIn have seen stock prices sink.

Enterprise software companies that rely on subscription sales have come under particular scrutiny because their business models often rely on heavy marketing to drive growth.

Cloudera, which sells software to manage big data, has delayed listing at a time of volatility in the public markets, even though many analysts expected it might try for an initial public offering last year. There have been no tech IPOs this year, after several went public last year at valuations lower than they earned on the private markets.

Dropbox, known for its photo sharing services, has been trying to move from consumer sales to a model that emphasises enterprise sales. However, it has struggled with several new products.

Fidelity mutual funds also wrote down the value of software companies such as Nutanix, Delphix, Cloudflare and Appirio in monthly holdings reports. The carrying value of these shares is determined by an independent team of analysts.

The value of a handful of companies increased, including Snapchat, the app known for its disappearing messages. In February, Fidelity increased its investment in the company even though Fidelity's own analysts had marked down the value of Snapchat shares last year. Fidelity declined to comment.

The cooling valuations have so far had little impact on capital flows to venture capital groups. Preliminary data from Dow Jones VentureSource show that US venture capital companies have raised more in the first quarter this year than in any quarter since the dotcom bubble.

Huge sums are still flowing to start-ups too, and as valuations come down, some investors are starting to see better opportunities to invest.

Telecom Italia Must Call Renzi

Political connections are always useful in the highly regulated telecom industry. In Italy they are essential.

The new chief executive named by Telecom Italia on Wednesday, Flavio Cattaneo, will have to show he has more traction with Matteo Renzi, Italy's prime minister, than his predecessor. He faces an uphill struggle of Alpine proportions.

Telecom Italia's relationship with the Italian authorities has become increasingly dysfunctional since Mr. Renzi assumed power in 2014. The prime minister attaches great importance to high-speed broadband, but seems to prefer working with energy company Enel, in which his finance ministry holds a 25.5% stake.

Last week, Enel announced a 2.5 billion euros ($2.8 billion) plan to deliver fiber-optic broadband cables direct to homes in 224 Italian municipalities as part of a project to install smart electricity meters. It won't offer telecom services itself, but said it had discussed a letter of intent with mobile operators Vodafone and Wind. The result would be a competitor to Telecom Italia's fixed-line business in urban markets.

This week, meanwhile, Mr. Renzi visited a renewable-power plant run by Enel in Nevada, the first stop on his U.S. tour. It is a "great global company," he commented on Facebook, his favored medium of public communication. "We will continue to make it grow, including through the innovative broadband plans we will present onApril 7."

The government has made 3.5 billion euros of public subsidies available for rolling out high-speed broadband to otherwise unprofitable rural areas. Next week's announcement is expected to include the first of the tenders. It doesn't sound like Telecom Italia will be getting the money.

Much depends on whether the Italian authorities see Vincent Bollore, chairman of Vivendi, the leading shareholder in Telecom Italia, as investor or raider. Mr. Bollore's ties with former Prime Minister Silvio Berlusconi, who in March confirmed his Mediaset pay-TV group was in talks with Vivendi over a content deal, may not be helpful.

Telecom Italia rattled investors in February with poor results anda plan to invest 12 billion euros over three years, delaying much-needed debt reductions. The shares have fallen 18% in the year to date.

Unfortunately for shareholders, the only way to appease the Italian government an unholy trinity of regulator, sponsor and competitor in the company's key home market is likely to involve more investment. Yet Mr. Cattaneo has little choice but to pick up the phone.

-- Stephen Wilmot

Can Bad News Be Good for European Steel?

Is one man's misfortune another man's gain? The decline of the U.K.'s steel industry, with news that Tata Steel plans to shed its entire business in the country, boosted shares elsewhere in the beleaguered European steel sector: notably, ThyssenKrupp's stock jumped 8% Wednesday.

This looks like the triumph of hope over experience. The bull case for ThyssenKrupp is pretty simple: Its capital goods business, which makes elevators, escalators and other engineering components, should be worth far more than the group's five times earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization.

Sell, spin off, joint venture or otherwise ditch the steel business and ThyssenKrupp's valuation should benefit.

No wonder ThyssenKrupp management has been vocal in its wish to shed steel, or otherwise participate in consolidation. But the reality has proved tough in an industry where most companies spent years waiting and wagering that a massively oversupplied market would improve, only to leave themselves in a dire enough state as to have few options when it didn't.

In that regard, Tata's decision regarding its money-losing U.K. operations offers a potential opening. India's Tata is trying to pare back its European operations to its efficient premium flat-steel-products business at Ijmuiden in the Netherlands. That business, which is thought to be profitable, may fit with ThyssenKrupp's flat steelmaking operations, argues Jefferies.

Problems abound: The chances of a quick, neat U.K. sale for Tata look remote, given limited buyers for a struggling asset in need of investment. The company hasn't said anything about its plans for Ijmuiden. It also isn't entirely clear the latter is large enough for a merger and consolidation of ThyssenKrupp's European business, which made about 500 million euros in operating profit in the most recent fiscal year.

Meanwhile, consolidation only really helps if it comes with serious cost savings and, ideally, capacity closures. Closures remain as politically fraught as ever.

But options on recovery are all that a sector living on fumes really has. Rising Chinese export prices have sent shares soaring since February. Any sniff of deal making, or more aggressive protection from policy makers, does the same. Two things are clear: The foundations for a fundamental steel recovery are flimsy at best and nothing in the interim will be easy.

-- Helen Thomas

China Banks

Are Fighting

Bad Loans

Chinese banks eked out small profit gains in 2015, but investors know better than to celebrate. There are just too many risks looming.

Three of China's biggest banks each beat expectations for fourth-quarter earnings on Wednesday. Still, the results were uninspiring. For all of 2015, net profit rose just 0.7% at Bank of China, 0.5% at Industrial & Commercial Bank of China and 0.1% at China Construction Bank.

It probably isn't a coincidence that the results are strikingly similar. The banks have some control over the amount of provisions they take on expected bad loans. It has been clear for a couple of quarters that they are managing their provisioning, with a target of barely positive earnings growth.

This trick is getting hard-er as bad loans surge. At the three institutions that reported earnings Wednesday, nonperforming loans rose by an average 40% in 2015.

In China, banks are required to maintain provisions worth 150% of bad loans, a relatively high ratio by international standards. They are now bumping up against that.

The bull case for the banks hinges on government support. Many analysts expect Chinese regulators to loosen the provision ratio requirement this year, which would allow the banks to set aside less money for bad loans. But that won't halt the rise of bad loans, which is likely closer to its start than its finish.

Chinese banks look cheap, at around 0.7 to 0.8 times book value. But with risks to their loan books rising, the discount seems reasonable.

-- Aaron Back

Shale Is Odd Man Out in Oil Reckoning

The world oil market is starting to resemble the old slapstick gag about the French Foreign Legion. The sergeant needs a volunteer to take a step forward for a dangerous mission. Instead, all of the assembled soldiers, except one, take a step back, volunteering him.

The sucker seems to be U.S.shale. While Russia and many membersofthe Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries may meet next month in Qatar to discuss the idea of an output freeze to support prices, this represents a hollow pledge.

The invited countries represent some three-quarters of global output -- certainly enough to move the needle on supply. But that needle is stuck in the red zone for all but one producer.

Iran, after years of sanctions, is determined to ramp up its output to some four million barrels a day from about 2.9 million in January.

In the face of that commitment, regional rival Saudi Arabia is reluctant to cede market share. And Russia, pumping near post-Soviet records and desperate for income, has taken a similar stance.

With OPEC and its possible partners unwilling to agree to much beyond the status quo, balancing of supply and demand is being done by Western producers with bills to pay and shareholders to please.

Economics might seem to dictate that the first cuts would come from the highest-cost fields such as Canada's oil sands, but that would be wrong. Instead it will occur where lack of investment will show up first: U.S.shale.

A report last week from the U.S. Energy Information Administration highlights why. A surprisingly high 48% of U.S. oil production from the contiguous states came from wells drilled since 2014.

Nearly all are from unconventional formations such asshale. They went from providing 500,000 barrels a day in 2009 to a peak of 4.6 million last May.

Output has been declining since as spending was cut. Based on geology alone, it should have been worse.

Production from ashalewell typically falls by half during the first year in operation and then by another quarter to one-third in the following year. The reason it hasn't is a significant rise in productivity per well -- a modest additional investment squeezed out extra barrels.

That trend is reaching its limits, though.

So are U.S. producers really such patsies? Driven by the imperative of profitability, it is true that they will cede share.

But a large number of uncompleted wells, colloquially referred to as a "fracklog," standsready to come on stream fairly quickly once prices rise by another $20 a barrel or so.

By keeping its financial powder dry, a leaner U.S. shalepatch can storm back soon after prices become more attractive and enjoy the last laugh.

-- Spencer Jakab

Chinese Banks Define Success Down

Chinese banks eked out small profit gains in 2015, but investors know better than to celebrate. There are just too many risks looming.

Three of China's biggest banks each beat expectations for fourth-quarter earnings on Wednesday. Still, the results were uninspiring. For all of 2015, net profit rose just 0.7% at Bank of China, 0.5% at Industrial & Commercial Bank of China and 0.1% at China Construction Bank.

What's in your wallet?

Americans' ability to answer that burning question isn't all that encouraging. It turns out, though, that cluelessness about money varies by geography.

The odds of getting a good answer from Americans is highest in New Hampshire: The residents of the Granite State came out tops in a survey of financial literacy by WalletHub.

The weakest states on that measure were Alaska and Nevada. Washington, D.C., was even worse. Wags might dismiss the residents of those three places as being a bit naive because they have been conditioned to think money comes from oil wells, slot machines or congressional appropriations, respectively. The answer, though, probably has nothing to do with how people make a living.

The odds of incorrect answers to basic financial questions and of financial readiness such as having certain types of accounts or an emergency fund correlated with lower income, younger age and minority status. New Hampshire, which is 94% white and both older and wealthier than most states, had the odds in its favor.

While residents of more financially literate states have earned mild bragging rights, the upshot of the survey is that America remains an unequal place not just by income but how people save and invest it.

Pro Private Markets
Moat Raises $50M to Help Develop Digital Ad Currency; The Web media analytics  company Moat has raised $50 million in new funding as it looks to take on an ambitious goal. It plans to challenge Google , Nielsen, comScore and other digital media companies by developing a standard metric that would serve as the currency for buying and selling digital ads.

By Mike Shields 
964 words
31 March 2016
03:53
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The Web media analytics company Moat has raised $50 million in new funding as it looks to take on an ambitious goal. It plans to challenge Google , Nielsen, comScore and other digital media companies by developing a standard metric that would serve as the currency for buying and selling digital ads.

Founded in 2010, Moat is perhaps best known for being one of the leading companies that track viewability--determining whether online ads can be seen by real people. But the 125-person company, which had previously raised over $17 million, aspires to do much more. According to co-founder and Chief Executive Jonah Goodhart, Moat is working on a new measurement designed to help Web advertisers and publishers gauge whether people pay attention to digital ads and are impacted by them.

The investment round was led by Insight Venture Partners .

The ultimate goal for Moat will be to have advertisers negotiate ad deals using this new metric, rather than relying on the long-standing practice of billing advertisers based on how many ads are delivered to a Web page by  ad serving" software.

Typically, ad serving data comes from Google --the leading ad serving provider in the industry. That data is often used by media companies to track how many ads they deliver and to bill advertisers. In addition, advertisers often use tools from comScore and Nielsen to enhance ad serving data, such as tracking the demographic makeup of the audience that is exposed to an ad campaign.

But as more digital advertising moves away from standard banners on Web pages toward video and native ads on platforms like YouTube , Snapchat and Facebook , Moat sees an opportunity to displace Google and other metrics providers.

Mr. Goodhart said Moat is already well positioned to do this, since it has its own viewability tracking mechanisms plugged into Facebook ,

YouTube and Twitter . Plus, a major advertiser, Unilever , is already transacting ad deals using Moat data.

 Our ambition is to build the currency for digital advertising," he said, while emphasizing that Moat sees companies like Nielsen and Google as potential partners in this effort.

Moat has successfully pivoted before. The company first launched with the idea of building a crowdsourcing tool for online ad creative, which never took off. Over time, the Moat team built a search engine for online ads, then started selling access to advanced analytics to marketers.

In the past few years, as the online ad industry rushed to start only buying ads that are viewable, Moat was in the right place at the right time and moved aggressively to build out viewability tracking tools for both desktops and mobile Web pages.

While he declined to discuss specific figures, Mr. Goodhart said that Moat has been profitable for the past two years and that revenue tripled from 2014 to 2015.

 The first thing I wrote down when we started this company five years ago was, we need to make brand advertising more effective online," said Mr. Goodhart. He and his Moat partner Michael Walrath and previously founded Right Media, an early digital ad exchange that was heavily used by direct-response advertisers. It was sold to Yahoo in 2007.

With Moat, the plan was to cater more to the kinds of marketers who spent heavily on TV, like car, beer and toothpaste brands.  Most stuff is bought offline," he said.  We're trying to connect those dots. If you look at TV, it has a currency [with Nielsen data]. It may be flawed, but the point is, everyone is on the same page."

Surely, most every digital media company would like to see more TV ad budgets come their way. But getting everyone on the same page for a brand new currency won't be easy. Just getting viewability standards set across the industry has been challenging.

Peter Naylor , head of sales for Hulu , said he started working with Moat a few years ago when brands started pushing the viewability concept. That move led to more ad budgets coming Hulu 's way, he said.

So he's impressed with Moat's technology and approach.  They are really in touch with the needs of both sides of the business," he said.  There is an opening there. But [ad] server counting is pretty foundationally sound in the industry."

Megan Pagliuca, global chief executive at Accuen, an agency that specializes in data-driven ad buying, said she's generally on board with Moat's philosophy.  I do think the industry needs to evolve beyond the metrics we have today," she said.  Ad serving data is clearly not enough."

But Ms. Pagliuca said it may be hard for any one company to solve all of the digital ad world's needs. For example, besides tracking whether ads are viewable or command attention, advertisers are looking for help in tracking people across different devices and different media vehicles, she said.

For now, Moat is focused on building out its technology aimed at developing this potential new ad currency, as well as hiring. The plan is to double staff this year, Mr. Goodhart said.  We think we are at an interesting crossroads in the industry," he said.  Where Marketers are saying,  if I can't measure it, I can't buy it.'"

Leslie Hook in San Francisco

Fidelity Investments has cut the valuations on many of the start-ups in which it owns shares, underscoring the adjustment under way in Silicon Valley as investors become more sceptical.

Among Fidelity s heaviest writedowns were enterprise software companies Cloudera and Dropbox, which had the value of their shares cut by 38 per cent and 20 per cent, respectively, from the end of January to the end of February.

The writedowns also reflect declines in technology share prices more broadly, as companies such as Twitter, LinkedIn and Salesforce have seen share prices sink.

Enterprise software companies that rely on subscription sales have come under particular scrutiny because their business models often rely on heavy marketing to drive growth.

Cloudera, which sells software to help companies manage big data, has delayed listing at a time of volatility in the public markets, even though many analysts expected it might try for an initial public offering last year. There have been no tech IPOs this year, after several tech companies went public last year at valuations lower than they earned on the private markets.

Dropbox, known for its photo and document sharing services, has been trying to move from consumer sales to a model that emphasises enterprise sales. However, the company has struggled with several new products that have not performed as well as hoped.

"http://www.ft.com/topics/organisations/Fidelity_Worldwide_Investment_Ltd" mutual funds also wrote down the value of software companies such as Nutanix, Delphix, Cloudflare and Appirio in monthly holdings reports released on Wednesday. The carrying value of these shares is determined by an independent team of analysts who draw on public company share prices, recent share transactions and revenue updates.

The value of a handful of companies increased, including Snapchat, the app known for its disappearing messages.

In February Fidelity increased its investment in Snapchat as part of a follow-on round even though Fidelity s own analysts had marked down the value of Snapchat shares last year. The new investment, at about $30 per share, in effect resets Fidelity s valuation of Snapchat back to its previous levels. Fidelity declined to comment.

Pro Private Markets
Banks Fight Tech Inertia and Fintech Startups; Banks, under pressure to respond to fintech startups circling their markets, are working on many fronts to inject new technology into operations, including opening labs and working with new vendors. But some banks have been slow to respond to competitive threats, hampered by an instinct to preserve legacy technology and existing business models, analysts say.

By Kim S. Nash 
489 words
31 March 2016
04:20
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Banks, under pressure to respond to fintech startups circling their markets, are working on many fronts to inject new technology into operations, including opening labs and working with new vendors. But some banks have been slow to respond to competitive threats, hampered by an instinct to preserve legacy technology and existing business models, analysts say.

Old technology can hobble innovation at some banks, said Rebecca Wettemann, vice president of research at Nucleus Research. That is because core software for processing transactions was built decades ago in some cases, and heavily customized, Ms. Wettemann said.  CIOs are in a tough spot trying to drive innovation without exposing risk to core systems," she said.

To modernize, many banks are bringing in new technology for marketing or consumer-facing situations, like mobile banking, Ms. Wettemenn said.

One reason banks have started innovation labs and embraced working with startups is because a lot of internal technology resources go to maintenance and regulatory compliance, said Rajiv Singh, chief executive of North American wholesale banking at Rabobank Group.  They're tapped out. They're busy dealing with the past," he said.

Others in financial services, including insurance and investment companies, are making bigger strides than banks, according to Boston Consulting Group. In its annual list of the 50 most innovative companies in the world last year, J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. was the only bank included.

Insurers, like fintech startups, are making good use of analytics, said Christophe Duthoit, a senior partner at Boston Consulting. Progressive Corp., for example, is hiring more data scientists as it ramps up experiments with real-time analytics to deliver customized policies, as CIO Journal reported in December. Progressive, Allstate Corp. and other insurance companies are pushing devices and apps to monitor customers' driving habits and tailor pricing accordingly, a new way of sizing up risk.

Banks have to change their mindset from thinking of themselves as transaction processors to exploiters of information, like Facebook Inc. and Amazon.com Inc., Mr. Duthoit said.  If you're better at collecting and exploiting vast amounts of information, then you can make some different offerings to a bunch of people" applying for loans, he said.

Analyzing data to create a complete  digital experience" for customers is difficult because typically at a big bank, information is spread across many different systems and departments, said Michael Raneri, managing director in PricewaterhouseCoopers' Strategy& financial advisory practice. Rather than significant change, Mr. Raneri said,  there's inertia that leads banks to incrementally improve what already exists."

Hedge fund  quants  win heart diagnosis challenge

Richard Waters in San Francisco 
631 words
30 March 2016
15:56
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Two hedge fund  quants  have come up with an algorithm that diagnoses heart disease from MRI images, beating nearly 1,000 other teams in one of the most ambitious competitions in artificial intelligence.

Tencia Lee, who recently left Los Angeles-based Crabel Capital Management to join a robotics start-up, said she and partner Qi Liu, a former employee of hedge fund Two Sigma, had not previously worked before with the winning technology, called deep learning.

Ms Lee and Mr Qi entered the contest in December and created a method that has proved, in early tests, to be as effective as a cardiologist in analysing images of the heart.

 People have been working on this for 15 years   I m amazed what kind of results came out of this competition in three months,  said Andrew Arai, chief of advanced cardiovascular imaging at the National Institutes of Health.

MRIs are used to diagnose heart disease around 1m times a year in the US, Mr Arai said, with cardiologists spending an average of 20 minutes on each image. That could make the algorithm an important addition to a growing field of automated medical imaging, though it faces stringent formal tests before it can be adopted.

The winning entry used a so-called convolutional neural network, a form of deep learning designed to emulate the way vision works in animals.

Ms Lee said that neither of the pair had worked with neural networks before and had taken software from GitHub, an online repository of open-source software, to solve the challenge. The main problem they faced had been to define the problem in a precise enough way, she added. After that, it was a question of feeding examples of heart MRIs into the neural network and letting it work out the solution.

The availability of such software meant that even complex problems were open to being solved by experts with a more general background in data science, Ms Lee said.  We are both very experienced with working with large amounts of data, and having an intuition about where to look for problems,  she said.  It took all my spare time over a period of three months. 

Recent technology advances are  allowing us to do things with images that were not possible three or four years ago , said Anthony Goldbloom, chief executive of Kaggle, which ran the National Science Data Bowl competition with consultancy Booz Allen Hamilton.

The changes include development of specialised chips suited to pattern recognition, called graphical processing units, or GPUs, and big increases in computing capacity, he added.

Open competitions have become an increasingly common way to solve difficult data science problems. Netflix ran one of the most prominent in 2009 with a $1m competition to come up with an algorithm to make film recommendations.

Kaggle, which conducts about 50 competitions a year on behalf of big companies, said the heart imaging challenge was the hardest it had run. The chance of winning instant fame in their field, even more than the prize money, accounted for the large number of people who entered such contests, Mr Goldbloom said.

Sander Dieleman, a PhD student in neural networks who headed a team from the University of Ghent that won last year s National Data Science Bowl, has since been hired by DeepMind, Google s UK-based deep-learning arm whose system recently beat the top human champion at the board game Go.

Ms Lee and Mr Qi entered the competition individually and were placed in the top 10 after an early round. They teamed up after Mr Qi posted a message on Kaggle seeking help.

This story has been updated to clarify that Qi Liu no longer works for Two Sigma
