RISING STAR: Permira s Dipan Patel Shows Knack for Finding Tech s  Hidden Gems 

By Amy Or 
794 words
30 March 2016
19:16
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Dipan Patel has made a name at London-based buyout shop Permira for unlocking the potential of quirky technology businesses.

In 2011, the executive spotted the then-publicly listed Renaissance Learning Inc., and convinced the Permira team to visit the educational assessment and learning analytics company in Wisconsin Rapids, Wis., a city known more for its timber industry than its technology. The visit paved the way for Permira's purchase of the company later that year for $440 million.

 Whenever I brought up Renaissance Learning, people would say,  That quirky Wisconsin business,'" Mr. Patel said.  That really intrigued me, because I could see from customer calls, school penetration and key performance indicators that it was a hidden gem."

The 33-year old Mr. Patel, who was promoted to partner in February, has built a career not only on his ability to find promising tech companies other firms may have overlooked, but by also identifying what those companies need to transform themselves and grow.

Dipan Patel

An Early Start

Mr. Patel, whose father was a serial entrepreneur, learned about investing at a young age around the family dinner table.  I grew up very comfortable with the notion of looking at lots of businesses and opportunities, understood a company's earnings, return, relative return and if our family should put money here or elsewhere," he said.

Career Path

Mr. Patel joined Permira as a London associate in 2009 and relocated to the Menlo Park, Calif., office in 2012. He was promoted to principal in 2014 and to partner in February.

Education

Mr. Patel holds a degree in economics from the University of Cambridge.

Board Positions

He serves as a non-executive director at Ancestry.com LLC, Legalzoom.com Inc. and data integration software provider Informatica Corp.

With Renaissance, for example, Mr. Patel could see the company's technological capability in assessing students' learning progress was weighed down by its quiz and hardware offerings, including its own version of a laptop called NEO.

Brian Ruder, a partner and co-head of Permira's technology team, praised Mr. Patel's foresight in spotting intense competition in hardware offerings from big technology companies, adding that Mr. Patel told his colleagues,  Newsflash, the world is going to iPads, Androids and tablets in the classroom."

Permira restructured Renaissance's business and disposed of its noncore quiz and hardware assets to focus on its core business of school improvement and student assessment software and technology. That shift helped the company grow its earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization by about 50% during the three years the firm owned it. When Permira sold Renaissance in 2014 to San Francisco peer Hellman & Friedman, the firm made more than four times its money.

 It was a team effort but it was Dipan who found it, developed and helped execute it both on the way in and on the way out," Mr. Ruder said.

Mr. Patel has  a unique combination of very good commercial judgment   he knows a good business when he sees it   and the ability to think about the quality of a business in a very quantitative way," said Mr. Ruder, adding that Mr. Patel's British charm works in the U.S.  like warm butter."

Among those charmed was John Suh, chief executive of Legalzoom.com Inc., who described Mr. Patel as having  an extra gear," apart from being analytical.  Too often highly analytic people get trapped in the weeds. Dipan can wallow in the data and still step back and elevate...staying focused on the two or three most difficult or important questions," he said.

Permira paid more than $200 million to take a majority stake in Legalzoom.com in 2014. At the time, the online platform that sells do-it-yourself wills and other legal documents was switching to a subscription-based model, a move that often leads to a dip in revenue during the transition period. Mr. Ruder said the young executive was the  key agent on the board to coach and cajole the management team to accelerate and focus on the transition that the business was just in the early stages of making."

Annual customer renewal rates turned out to be better than expected and became a significant business driver.

 At the time of the investment, we had launched multiple new services with no data on renewals. Management was using a conservative estimate, but there was considerable perceived risk at the time," Mr. Suh said.  Permira made an educated bet borne out of deep analysis...they took a leap of faith and were rewarded when we outperformed."

Pro Private Markets
MapD Locates $10M Series A for Faster Databases; Todd Mostak couldn t have predicted that his time in Cairo as an Arabic-English translator or as a student of Middle East studies at Harvard University would eventually lead him to a startup.

By Cat Zakrzewski 
582 words
30 March 2016
15:41
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Todd Mostak couldn't have predicted that his time in Cairo as an Arabic-English translator or as a student of Middle East studies at Harvard University would eventually lead him to a startup.

But as he was working on his thesis about social media in the Middle East, he realized there were no databases or visualization tools fast enough to do the kind of analysis he needed. So he decided to make his own while taking a class at Massachusetts Institute of Technology.

The result of his path was the GPU-powered database and analytics suite that is now known as MapD Technologies Inc. The startup, founded in 2013, recently closed $10 in Series A funding led by Vanedge Capital, which had participation from Verizon Ventures, Nvidia Corporation and GV, formerly Google Ventures.

Mr. Mostak declined to disclose the company's valuation but said it has raised $12 million to date.

Many data tools today are powered by CPUs, or central processing units. MapD is instead powered by graphics cards, known as GPUs. CPUs only consist of a few cores optimized for sequential serial processing, but GPUs have thousands of smaller, more efficient cores that can handle many tasks simultaneously.

MapD uses these chips to power SQL queries. The tool has a wide variety of applications for large sets of enterprise data. It has been used for social media analytics at companies such as Facebook Inc., business intelligence at companies the likes of Verizon and recently has even been used by government agencies.

Though the buzzword  big data" is often used to describe his startup, Mr. Mostak thinks the company falls into an emerging category. He said the company deals with tens of gigabytes to tens of terabytes of working data.

 Its allows you to be very interactive on it," Mr. Mostak said.  I would say we're  fast data.'"

On its website, MapD shows how its GPU-powered tweetmap can break down 60 million geo-coded tweets. The application shows where people sent the tweets from and what devices people use to send them.

The company offers two products, a database and a visualization platform. The products are sold separately but can be used together.

Mr. Mostak said MapD is only going to get faster, thanks to innovation from GPU manufacturers. Consumer demand for high-definition videogames has fueled development in the sector faster than in CPUs, Mr. Mostak said. Large tech companies investing in virtual reality also have interest in improved GPUs because the better the graphics card, the less likely a person is to get sick. Mr. Mostak said he can now get cards as large as 24 gigabytes but that size will double very soon.

He said such an increase could allow the company to process data up to 200 times faster.

Within the next year, Mr. Mostak said the company will move toward machine learning applications. Mr. Mostak said in the future, MapD could have applications in image classification and natural language.

MapD has about 20 employees, and it recently took its product to market. The company has seven clients, and Mr. Mostak said the recent funding will be used toward hiring and sales.

"http://www.mapd.com/"

 Michaels s Take: Mutual-Fund Sponsors Banked on a Softer SEC; The Wall Street Journal s financial regulation newsletter for Tuesday, March 29, 2016.

Michaels's Take: Mutual-Fund Sponsors Banked on a Softer SEC

Goldman Sachs and Bear Stearns: A Financial-Crisis Mystery Is Solved

Executive at Blackstone Spinoff Charged With Stealing $25 Million From Customers

SEC Investigating SunEdison's Disclosures to Investors About Its Liquidity

Mutual-Fund Sponsors Banked on a Softer SEC

After years of enjoying lighter-touch regulation under the Securities and Exchange Commission, the asset-management industry issued an unusual plea in 2014: Please regulate us.

Mutual-fund sponsors, including Fidelity Investments and BlackRock Inc., appealed to their long-time regulator because they feared a crackdown from the Financial Stability Oversight Council, which wields the power to subject any big financial firm to the type of enhanced oversight reserved for Wall Street banks.

As the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/hedge-funds-for-retail-class-face-regulatory-reckoning-1459137603", the industry's strategy has yet to pay the dividends it hoped for. Comment letters filed with the SEC on Monday argue the agency's proposal to restrict mutual funds' use of leverage would devastate some alternative mutual funds which make extensive use of derivatives and force them to withdraw from the retail market. No industry lobbyist could have planned for that outcome.

It's understandable that mutual funds counted on a softer approach. For decades the SEC operated as a  disclosure agency," meaning it worked to ensure that individuals and institutions got the information they need to make investment decisions. It sued companies and individuals that issued false information or otherwise strayed from its rules. But its army of lawyers and accountants didn't consider it their role to measure whether one mutual fund's troubles could infect the rest of the financial system.

That approach began to change, of course, after the financial crisis. In recent speeches, Chairman Mary Jo White has talked up the SEC's evolution into a regulator that does more than inform investors of risk and return.  More and more, the complexity of products, changes in market participant behavior, pervasive network technology, and systemic risks call for additional protections beyond those that can be achieved through disclosure alone," "https://www.sec.gov/news/speech/white-speech-beyond-disclosure-at-the-sec-in-2016-021916.html" in February.

That doesn't mean that industry will always agree. The fresh fight over the SEC's leverage cap follows a similar fracas over its plan to ensure funds don't get caught during a market backdraft holding too many illiquid investments.  There is no justification for such detailed regulatory intervention," the Investment Company Institute wrote in a comment letter in January.

Ms. White and other SEC officials want to complete many of the asset-management rules before the end of the Obama administration early next year. In theory, that means both sides have incentives to compromise. Finishing several rules would allow Ms. White and the SEC to declare victory over both emerging financial risks and the threat of other regulators intruding on their turf.

To be sure, the effort could stall. The SEC's rulemaking process is notoriously slow and its progress could be delayed by the arrival of two new commissioners, who may want to put their own stamp on the regulations. The SEC is currently operating with only three commissioners after two veteran members resigned last year.

Yet industry groups like the ICI are aware that they, too, need to strike a balance. Any hint that the SEC has gone wobbly on its plans could invite renewed scrutiny by the oversight council. That's why the latest industry comment letters continue to profess support for the SEC's effort while opposing many important details of its proposals.

Smoothing Out Quarter-End Volatility

Before the financial crisis, the Federal Reserve needed to bolster its reserves to prevent spikes in the federal-funds rate, the rate banks charge to other banks that are borrowing money, typically overnight. Since the crisis, however, the Fed faces the opposite problem, with the excess of reserves causing increased volatility. Throughout the 1980s and 1990s, and particularly in the years leading up to the financial crisis, the fed-funds rate experienced significant volatility around the end of each quarter, during the periods of highest demand. To offset that volatility, the Fed injected extra reserves into the system, allowing it to keep the fed-funds rate on target even during times of spiking demand, "http://libertystreeteconomics.newyorkfed.org/2016/03/how-the-fed-smoothed-quarter-end-volatility-in-the-fed-funds-market.html#.Vvl3leIrJhG".

FRONT PAGE - COMPANIES & MARKETS
Big 10 banks fined $43bn over seven years for failures in customer reporting

By Laura Noonan, Investment Banking Correspondent 
337 words
28 March 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
13
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Failures in customer reporting have cost the world's top investment banks $43bn in fines over the past seven years, making it the single most expensive compliance issue, according to new research.

The main types of failure and wrong-doing that resulted in fines worth a total of $150bn for 10 US and European banks between 2009 and 2015 have been tracked by Corlytics, an analytics firm. These compliance issues range from rigging foreign exchange rates and money laundering to product mis-selling.

The 10 banks covered by Corlytics' work are Barclays, Bank of America, Citigroup, Credit Suisse, Deutsche Bank, Goldman Sachs, HSBC, JPMorgan, Morgan Stanley and UBS. The fines relate to both civil and criminal cases brought by regulators and other authorities with powers to levy penalties.

The seven years of fines have already taken a heavy toll on the banks, wiping out the equivalent of 14 per cent of their equity capital.

John Byrne, chief executive of Corlytics, said that client reporting failures were the source of substantial fines for banks in a wide variety of cases, including misleading customers on investments and not communicating clearly enough with borrowers.

"It can involve any aspect of client disadvantage or loss due to inaccurate or misleading reports or communication," he added. Corlytics' work shows that poor disclosure to clients was a factor in the $25bn of fines paid by US banks in a 2012 settlement for abusive foreclosure practices.

The second most expensive issue for the 10 banks was failures in how they sold residential mortgage securities, which resulted in a penalties total of $27.7bn. The banks meanwhile paid $20.2bn in fines in relation to securitisation failures. "Rate setting" fines came to $14.6bn, partly stemming from banks' manipulation of foreign exchange and interest rates.

Mr Byrne said that while many banks have "experienced serious issues with regulators, some have not".

Pro Private Markets
VC Funds Stay Out of Blockchain Loop; Venture-capital firms, a go-to source of capital for tech wizards, say they are not rushing to open their checkbooks on blockchain--the technology that has captured the imagination of Wall Street.

By Anna Irrera 
927 words
28 March 2016
14:52
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Venture-capital firms, a go-to source of capital for tech wizards, say they are not rushing to open their checkbooks on blockchain--the technology that has captured the imagination of Wall Street.

Last year was the year that big banks, exchanges and high-profile finance professionals flocked to distributed ledgers such as blockchain, championing their potential to revolutionize capital markets--especially post-trade processes--and save the industry large amounts of time and money.

Autonomous Research has suggested blockchain could help banks eliminate $16 billion, or one-third of annual clearing and settlement costs by 2021.

Huge industry collaborations are under way and the likes of UBS, Deutsche Bank and Nasdaq have already declared successful tests in scenarios ranging from bond trading to the sale and settlement of shares.

VC firms invested $460 million in blockchain and bitcoin startups in 2015, according to a March report by KPMG and CB Insights. But while this figure was over 50% up on the previous year it was modest compared with investment in other areas of financial innovation, such as robo-advisory, alternative lending and machine learning, according to the report, which cautioned investors  to take a balanced approach" to their blockchain strategies.

While not dismissing the potential of blockchain, many venture capital investors say it is too early to pick winners in a market that will require a significant overhaul of financial infrastructures to grow.

Matt Harris, a managing director at New York-based Bain Capital Ventures, which has invested in the Digital Currency Group, said:  The issue for VCs in investing in blockchain startups is that, as yet, no one really knows what it means.

 We understand bitcoin and the associated blockchain, and can act on our views and beliefs there, but right now the more generic blockchain space is pretty muddled."

Valuations are also a concern, according to some influential investors, who believe that hype around blockchain at the big banks is skewing prices.

Mark Beeston, a managing partner at capital markets fintech fund Illuminate Financial, said:  Blockchain seems to be priced fundamentally differently to other capital markets fintech: it is getting B2C-type early-stage valuations, which would appear to be driven by the perception of billion-dollar savings."

Chain, the San Francisco-based blockchain startup, Digital Asset, which is run by former J.P. Morgan commodities chief Blythe Masters, and the Montreal-based blockchain startup Blockstream have all secured funding rounds in the tens of millions of dollars over the past year--with a lot of the money coming from big financial firms.

As potential beneficiaries and users of the technology, banks and other market participants are making investments that are strategic, and therefore different to the nature of venture-capital firms.

Allard Luchsinger, a director at Dutch venture-capital firm Velocity Capital, said:  It's smart of the banks [to invest] because for them there is a use case that is so clear. We are a VC and not a bank so we need to invest in companies that we think are really going to scale up."

Jon Korngold, managing director and global head of financial services at U.S.-based General Atlantic, went further. He said:  While the banks' investments will not ensure success for any one platform, it's probably safe to conclude that the current universe of potential winners will be limited to those with investments from the banks, with the other platforms who have yet to secure bank funding at risk of finding themselves on the outside of the winners' circle, looking in."

The current state of blockchain development is often likened to the early days of the Internet and, as such, Illuminate's Mr. Beeston said it was  far from clear" whether startups would be the biggest financial winners from its evolution, or whether finance's bigger names would come out on top.

Global interdealer broker ICAP on March 15 said it had successfully tested a new blockchain-based system, called Project Infinity, in the FX markets that it hoped to bring to market. U.S. exchange group Nasdaq has also taken an aggressive approach on blockchain and is participating in bids to provide distributed ledger services and solutions to capital markets firms.

In their March report, KPMG and CB Insights wrote that the  ability for blockchain to become a true game-changer is still in progress", noting that there were  substantial barriers that must be overcome in order to implement it successfully within banking and capital markets."

Overhauls of market infrastructures take time. As well as a need for banks to come together and agree on implementation standards and for existing technology systems to be replaced, regulators will have their say. In February, European lawmakers called for a task force to be set up to explore ways of regulating blockchain use.

Manu Gupta, a general partner at Zurich-based Lakestar, a backer of well-known fintech companies such as alternative lender SoFi, fixed income information startup Algomi and bank-backed chat service Symphony, said the firm spent time looking at blockchain but decided not to invest.

 You are changing the plumbing of banking--that is the reason why we ultimately backed away from it. We recognize that, without the endorsement of the banks, the adoption rates will take forever," he said.

Heard on the Street
Overheard

292 words
28 March 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J

English
Copyright  2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

[Financial Analysis and Commentary]

Gilead Sciences has dodged one of the threats to its hepatitis C juggernaut.

True, Gilead shares fell after the company lost a legal challenge from Merck to patents surrounding its hepatitis C drugs Sovaldi and Harvoni last week. A federal jury ordered Gilead to pay Merck $200 million in damages.

But the outcome, which Gilead plans to appeal, could have been far worse: Merck had asked for $2 billion in damages. A separate hearing will take place to decide whether Merck is entitled to a royalty on future sales.

Investors worry the hepatitis C franchise has matured after generating cumulative sales of more than $30 billion. Those drugs accounted for 60% of Gilead's 2015 sales. As such, Gilead trades at a much lower multiple of forward earnings than peers. The jury's manageable award doesn't eliminate those fears, but Gilead can cross one problem off its list.

---

Keefe, Bruyette & Woods analysts made waves last week by suggesting in a research note a breakup of Citigroup, starting with the sale of international assets like Mexican unit Banamex. Not everyone was buying it. Karen Shaw Petrou at Federal Financial Analytics likened it to becoming a mermaid -- a nice idea but hardly realistic.

There must be willing buyers, and "I have not seen home-country banks in emerging economies exactly rolling in dough right now," Ms. Petrou wrote. And global banks that could be buyers, like HSBC, Standard Chartered and Barclays, have their own issues. That leaves listings of emerging-market units. But as stand-alone banks, they could suffer from lower credit ratings.

This maiden isn't likely to sprout a tail anytime soon.

MARKETS & INVESTING
AI tools prove a hard sell with fund managers; Analysis: Equities

By Robin Wigglesworth 
847 words
26 March 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
13
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

'Quantitative' financiers are sceptical that new technologies will help in battle for returns

Isaac Newton may have been one of the finest minds of all time, but he turned out to be a miserable investor. "I can calculate the motions of the heavenly bodies, but not the madness of people," he lamented after losing a fortune in the South Sea bubble .

Increasingly, however, technology-savvy investors think they can harness mathematics and computer science to predict financial markets. Some of the most advanced asset managers are turning to artificial intelligence , with investment algorithms that can autonomously learn, adapt and scour vast data sets for tradable patterns.

But some "quantitative" financiers, or quants , are sceptical that these tools are any more than a somewhat better mousetrap, and argue that areas such as "machine learning" are overhyped and AI used as a marketing gimmick.

"Everyone wants the Holy Grail, something they can invest in and it will make 1 per cent a month forever," says Ewan Kirk, head of Cantab Capital, a quantitative hedge fund. "I don't want to be cynical, but I am sceptical."

David Harding, head of Winton Capital, one of the biggest quantitative hedge funds in the world, is also doubtful that AI represents a quantum leap for the investment industry. "I'm not a Luddite, we're always interested in new ways to make money. But I have to be very sceptical because I constantly have world-class people showing me miracle cures that don't actually work," he says.

Improvements in computing power have revolutionised the investment world, with algorithmic traders and investors increasingly influential across markets. Money is pouring into computer-driven hedge funds that have consistently managed to parse signals amid market noise. As a result money managers are scrambling to hire computer scientists, often pitting them in direct competition for talent with Silicon Valley's tech giants and hot start-ups.

AI is at the forefront of this. The field has taken several leaps forward in recent years. Most notably, Google's DeepMind AI arm has created a programme that recently thrashed a legendary player of Go, an ancient Chinese game so complex that most experts had reckoned it would take at least a decade before a computer could beat a human champion .

The potentially wider applications of techniques used by the likes of DeepMind's AlphaGo algorithm have fuelled optimism that investment management could be on the cusp of another technological revolution, possibly similar in scale to the electronification of markets in the 1970s and 1980s.

"Machine learning and artificial intelligence is going to play a very large role in quant managers, but also with traditional asset managers that are aggressively expanding in this space," says Osman Ali, a fund manager at Goldman Sachs Asset Management.

Popular AI approaches such as machine learning can be used by computers to develop autonomously. For example, a machine learning algorithm can master a computer game such as Super Mario independently, at first playing the arcade classic randomly but quickly figuring out how the controls work.

There is therefore widespread enthusiasm for the potential of machine learning algos to find fleeting but profitable patterns in the vast sea of data.

"I think of algos as little children that can scale tremendously," says Brad Betts of BlackRock's Scientific Active Equity arm. "And you can teach them to read millions of books at the same time."

Yet scepticism, even among quants, is pervasive. They see areas such as machine learning and deep learning - the latter underpinned DeepMind's Go exploits - merely as extensions of techniques that have long been in use.

"Lots of people [have been using] techniques that could be called machine learning for decades," argues Robert Hillman, head of Neuron Capital. "There's a huge difference between image recognition and using AI in markets. Will this be a paradigm change for investing? I don't think so . . . It's not a fundamental change, it's an efficiency improvement."

Mr Kirk says that most common AI approaches are focused on pattern recognition, such as telling the difference between a cat and a dog in an image. But markets are dominated by noise and chaos; the patterns are harder to find.

"As a geek I'm super-excited about AlphaGo, but it's a big leap from beating a game with clearly defined rules and objectives [to] investing," he says.

Even quants that are cautiously optimistic on the future of AI in investing warn of pitfalls. Algorithms that may look ingenious and backrest superbly against historical data have a habit of unravelling when confronted with unforgivingly fickle financial markets.

"Playing Super Mario might not necessarily work for markets. If you hit the button you always know what will happen, but you don't in markets," says another quant at a large hedge fund. "It can take time for it to find the good trades and to optimise them. It can go through a lot of bad trades."
